Diyat — Blood Money
beginner
6 min
Foundational Maxims on Diyat and Injury Compensation
Foundational maxims on diyat valuation and liability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and explain the Hanafi legal maxims (52-56) concerning the state of the act, diyat amounts, limb compensation, benefit valuation, and confession.

Core Idea
Diyat amounts are divinely fixed; loss of limb function or beauty triggers full diyah.

This lesson covers the opening maxims from the Book of Diyat. These principles establish the rules for compensation: when liability attaches, how amounts are determined, and what is considered for valuation. They are the bedrock for understanding Hanafi law on bodily injuries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maxim
Read each maxim (52-56) in Arabic and English. Note the key legal concept it encapsulates.
Focus on the precise wording; these are axiomatic.
2
Understand the Scope
Determine which area of fiqh each maxim applies to (diyat, contracts, evidence).
Maxim 55 applies to benefits in contracts, not just injuries.
3
Apply to a Hypothetical
Create a simple scenario and test which maxim governs the outcome.
E.g., someone loses a hand: maxim 54 applies, full diyah.

باب فى اعتبار حالة القتل
(۵۲) الْإِعْتِبَارُ لِحَالَةِ الْفِعْل
كتاب الديات
(۵۳) الْمَقَادِيرُ تُعْرَفُ بِالتَّوْقِيفِ
فصل فيما دون النفس
(۵۴) إِذَا فَاتَ جِنْسُ مَنْفَعَةِ الْأَطْرَافِ عَلَى الْكَمَالِ أَوْ أَزَالَ جَمَالا مَقْصُودًا فِي الْآدَمِيٌّ عَلَى الْكَمَالِ يَجِبُ كُلُّ الدِّيَةِ
(۵۵) الْمَنَافِعُ لَا تَتَقَوَّمُ إِلَّا بِعَقْدِ أَوْ بِشُبُهَةٍ
(۵۶) الْإِقْرَارُ لَا يَتَعَدَّى الْمُقِرَّ
فصل في الجنين
(۵۷) اَلضَّمَانُ لَا يَجِبُ بالشَّكِّ
(۵۸) كُلُّ أَحَدٍ مَأْذُونٌ فِي إِقَامَةِ الْقُرُبَاتِ
باب ما يحدثه الرجل في الطريق
باب جناية البهيمة والجناية عليها
(۵۹) الْمُرُورُ فِى طَرِيقِ الْمُسْلِمِينَ مُبَاحٌ مُقَيَّدٌ بِشَرُطِ السَّلَامَةِ
(۲۰) الْفِعْلُ الْمُبَاحُ فِي غَيْرِهِ سَبَبٌ لِلضَّمَانِ
(٢١) اَلتَّسْبِيبُ بِوَصْفِ التَّعَدِّى سَبَبٌ لِلصَّمَانِ
(۲۲) اَلْفِعْلُ الاخْتِيَارِيُّ يُضَافُ إِلَى فَاعِلِهِ وَلَا يَجُوزُ إِضَافَتُهُ إِلَى غَيْرِهِ
(۶۳) جُرْحُ الْعَجُمَاءِ جُبَارٌ
باب جناية الملوك والجناية عليه
(۲۴) اَلتَّأْجِيلُ فِي الْأَعْيَانِ بَاطِلٌ
(۲۵) اَلسِّرَايَةُ فِي الْأَوْصَافِ الشَّرْعِيَّةِ دُونَ الْأَوْصَافِ الْحَقِيقِيَّةِ
باب غصب العبد والمدبر والصبي والجناية في ذلك
(۲۶) الْغَصْبُ قَاطِع لِلسَّرَايَةِ
(۶۷) الْمَحْجُورُ عَلَيْهِ مُؤَاخَذْ بِأَفْعَالِهِ
(۲۸) شَرْطُ الْعِلَّةِ يُنَزَّلُ مَنْزِلَةَ الْعِلَّةِ إِذَا كَانَ تَعَدِّيًا
(۲۹) صِحَّةُ الْقَصْدِ لَا مُعْتَبَرٌ فِي حُقُوقِ الْعِبَادِ
باب القسامة
(۷۰) الْيَمِينُ قَولٌ صَحِيحٌ
(۱) اَلْقَسَامَةُ تَتْبَعُ احْتِمَالَ الْقَتْلِ
(۷۲) الْمَوْجُودُ الأَوَّلُ إِنْ كَانَ بِحَالٍ لَوْ وُجِدَ الْبَاقِي تَجْرِى فِيهِ الْقَسَامَةُ لَا تَجِبُ فِيهِ وَإِلَّا فَتَجِبُ فِيهِ
(۷۳) مَنِ انْتَصَبَ خَصُمًا فِى حَادِثَةٍ ثُمَّ خَرَجَ مِنْ كَوْنِهِ خَصْمًا لَمْ تُقْبَلُ شَهَادَتُهُ فِي تِلْكَ الْحَادِثَةِ بِالْإِجْمَاعِ
(۷۴) إِذَا كَانَتْ لِرَجُلٍ عَرْضِيَّةُ أَنْ يَصِيرَ خَصِمًا ثُمَّ بَطَلَتْ تِلْكَ الْعَرْضِيَّةُ فَشَهِدَ قُبِلَتْ شَهَادَتُهُ بِالْإِجْمَاعِ
Chapter on Considering the State of Killing
(52) Consideration is for the state of the act.
Book of Blood Money
(53) Measurements are known by divine revelation.
Section on What is Below the Soul
(54) When the complete type of benefit of limbs is lost, or it removes intended beauty in the complete human, the full blood money is obligatory.
(55) Benefits are not compensated except by contract or by semblance.
(56) Confession does not extend beyond the confessor.
Section on the Fetus
(57) Guarantee does not become obligatory due to doubt.
(58) Everyone is permitted to perform acts of worship.
Chapter on What a Man Does on the Road
Chapter on Injury by an Animal and Injury to It
(59) Passing through the Muslims' road is permissible conditioned on safety.
(20) A permissible act in itself is a cause for guarantee.
(21) Causing with the attribute of transgression is a cause for guarantee.
(22) A voluntary act is attributed to its doer and cannot be attributed to another.
(63) The wound of an animal is of no consequence.
Chapter on Injury by Kings and Injury to Them
(24) Deferment in specific objects is void.
(25) Continuation of effect applies to legal descriptions, not to real descriptions.
Chapter on Usurpation of a Slave, a Mudabbar, a Child, and Injury Regarding That
(26) Usurpation cuts off the continuation of effect.
(67) A person under interdiction is held responsible for his actions.
(28) The condition of the effective cause is treated as the effective cause when it involves transgression.
(29) Correctness of intention is not considered in rights of people.
Chapter on the Qasama
(70) The oath is a valid statement.
(1) The qasama depends on the probability of killing.
(72) The first existing one: if it is in a state such that, had the remaining existed, qasama would apply, it is not obligatory; otherwise, it is obligatory.
(73) Whoever becomes a litigant in a case then ceases to be a litigant, his testimony in that case is not accepted by consensus.
(74) If a man had the possibility of becoming a litigant, then that possibility ceased, then he testified, his testimony is accepted by consensus.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of Hanafi legal maxims (qawa‘id fiqhiyyah) drawn from the Hidayah commentary, primarily from the Book of Diyat (blood money) and related chapters. Each maxim is a concise legal principle that serves as a foundation for deriving rulings. The maxims cover various topics: the basis of liability (consideration of the state of the act, maxim 52), the definition of diyat amounts by revelation (53), compensation for loss of limb function and beauty (54), that benefits are not compensable without a contract or semblance (55), the non-transference of confession (56), guarantee not arising from doubt (57), permission for devotional acts (58), road use conditioned on safety (59), that a permissible act can lead to liability (20), causation with transgression as a basis for guarantee (21), attribution of voluntary acts to the doer (22), the animal wound rule (63), the voidness of deferment in specific objects (24), the principle of seraya (continuation of effect) applying only to legal descriptions (25), usurpation cutting off seraya (26), the liability of a interdicted person for his actions (67), the condition of the cause when transgressive (28), disregard of intention in civil rights (29), the oath as valid (70), qasama depending on probability of killing (71), and rules on witness testimony and qasama application (72-74). The student should memorize these principles as they are repeatedly applied in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
دية (diyah): Monetary compensation for homicide or bodily injury, paid to the victim or heirs.
إقرار (iqrar): Confession or admission of a right or liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to maxim (53), how are diyat amounts determined?
A) By the judge's discretion.
B) By local custom.
C) By divine revelation (tawqif).
D) By the victim's demand.

Key Takeaway
The first set of maxims establishes that diyat is a fixed, revealed system, and full compensation is due only when the complete benefit or intended beauty of a limb is lost.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate

Maxims on Liability and Causation
Hanafi maxims on guarantee, causation, and liability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxims on guarantee (daman), causation, and attribution of acts to determine liability for injuries.

Core Idea
Guarantee never arises from doubt; causation with transgression triggers liability.

This lesson covers maxims concerning when a person becomes liable for damages. The key principles are: guarantee does not arise from doubt (57), a permissible act can still lead to guarantee if it causes harm (20), transgressive causation is a basis for guarantee (21), voluntary acts are attributed to their doer alone (22), the animal wound is of no consequence (63), the condition of an effective cause when transgressive is treated as the cause itself (28), and correctness of intention is irrelevant in civil rights (29). These maxims form the core of Hanafi tort law.

Guided Path
1
Identify Each Maxim
Read maxims 57, 20, 21, 22, 63, 28, 29 in Arabic and English. Note the specific legal conclusion.
Some maxims are negative (e.g., guarantee does not...).
2
Classify the Maxim
Determine whether the maxim relates to liability (daman), causation (tasbib), or attribution (idafah).
Maxims 20,21,28 are about causation; 22 about attribution; 57 and 29 about limits of liability.
3
Apply to Scenarios
For each maxim, think of a scenario where it would be the controlling principle.
E.g., maxim 57: if uncertain whether one caused harm, no guarantee.

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of Hanafi legal maxims (qawa‘id fiqhiyyah) drawn from the Hidayah commentary, primarily from the Book of Diyat (blood money) and related chapters. Each maxim is a concise legal principle that serves as a foundation for deriving rulings. The maxims cover various topics: the basis of liability (consideration of the state of the act, maxim 52), the definition of diyat amounts by revelation (53), compensation for loss of limb function and beauty (54), that benefits are not compensable without a contract or semblance (55), the non-transference of confession (56), guarantee not arising from doubt (57), permission for devotional acts (58), road use conditioned on safety (59), that a permissible act can lead to liability (20), causation with transgression as a basis for guarantee (21), attribution of voluntary acts to the doer (22), the animal wound rule (63), the voidness of deferment in specific objects (24), the principle of seraya (continuation of effect) applying only to legal descriptions (25), usurpation cutting off seraya (26), the liability of a interdicted person for his actions (67), the condition of the cause when transgressive (28), disregard of intention in civil rights (29), the oath as valid (70), qasama depending on probability of killing (71), and rules on witness testimony and qasama application (72-74). Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Liability or guarantee to compensate for loss or damage.
تسبب (tasbib): Indirect causation or causing by setting in motion a chain of events.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to maxim (29), is the correctness of intention relevant in civil rights (huquq al-ibad)?
A) Yes, it is a defense.
B) No, it is not considered.
C) Only in property disputes.
D) Only if the intention is malicious.

Key Takeaway
Liability in Hanafi law is based on objective causation and harm, not intention; permissible acts can trigger guarantee, and transgression makes conditions equivalent to causes.

Maxims on Road Use, Animal Injuries, and Property
Maxims on road use, animal injuries, and property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and apply the Hanafi maxims on public road use, animal injuries, deferment in specific objects, seraya (continuation of effect), and usurpation.

Core Idea
Public road use is permissible only if safe; animal wounds are generally not compensable.

This lesson covers maxims from the chapters on road use, animal injuries, and property. Maxim 59 requires safety when using the public road. Maxim 63 exempts animal owners from liability unless negligent. Maxim 24 nullifies any contract that defers delivery of specific objects. Maxim 25 explains that seraya (continuation of effect) applies only to legal descriptions, not physical ones. Maxim 26 states that usurpation cuts off seraya. Maxim 67 holds that even interdicted persons are liable for their acts. These maxims govern everyday interactions and property rights.

Guided Path
1
Read the Maxims
Read maxims 59, 63, 24, 25, 26, 67 in Arabic and English. Understand the literal meaning.
Pay attention to the conditions in each maxim.
2
Identify the Domain
Determine which area of fiqh each maxim governs: road use, animal torts, sales, property damage, etc.
Maxim 24 is from sales (contracts), while others are from torts.
3
Apply to Hypotheticals
Create a short scenario for each maxim and test its operation.
For maxim 25, consider a man who cuts a tree on his neighbor's property causing a fence to collapse.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of Hanafi legal maxims (qawa‘id fiqhiyyah) drawn from the Hidayah commentary, primarily from the Book of Diyat (blood money) and related chapters. Each maxim is a concise legal principle that serves as a foundation for deriving rulings. The maxims cover various topics: the basis of liability (consideration of the state of the act, maxim 52), the definition of diyat amounts by revelation (53), compensation for loss of limb function and beauty (54), that benefits are not compensable without a contract or semblance (55), the non-transference of confession (56), guarantee not arising from doubt (57), permission for devotional acts (58), road use conditioned on safety (59), that a permissible act can lead to liability (20), causation with transgression as a basis for guarantee (21), attribution of voluntary acts to the doer (22), the animal wound rule (63), the voidness of deferment in specific objects (24), the principle of seraya (continuation of effect) applying only to legal descriptions (25), usurpation cutting off seraya (26), the liability of a interdicted person for his actions (67), the condition of the cause when transgressive (28), disregard of intention in civil rights (29), the oath as valid (70), qasama depending on probability of killing (71), and rules on witness testimony and qasama application (72-74). The student should memorize these principles as they are repeatedly applied in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
سراية (seraya): Continuation of effect from a cause to a consequence, especially in liability and property.
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation or wrongful taking of property without right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Road use requires safety; animal owners are not strictly liable; deferment in specific objects is void; seraya operates only on legal descriptions, and usurpation severs it.

Maxims on Qasama and Testimony
Maxims on qasama procedure and testimony rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules for qasama (oath swearing in blood feuds) and the admissibility of testimony from litigants.

Core Idea
Qasama depends on probability of killing; an oath is a valid legal statement.

This lesson covers maxims from the chapter on qasama. Qasama is a procedure used when a person is found killed but the killer is unknown; the heirs can swear oaths and claim diyat. These maxims define the conditions for qasama, the validity of oaths, and rules about testimony of litigants. Understanding these maxims is crucial for the Hanafi approach to criminal evidence.

Guided Path
1
Understand Qasama
Read maxims 70, 1 (actually 71), 72, 73, 74. Note the role of oaths and the probability of killing.
Maxim numbers may be misprinted; maxim 1 is actually 71.
2
Distinguish Scenarios
For maxim 72, differentiate between cases where qasama is obligatory and where it is not.
It depends on whether the first found body indicates a probability of killing.
3
Apply Testimony Rules
Maxims 73 and 74 deal with the testimony of someone who was a litigant. Understand the distinction.
Key: if the person was actually a litigant, he cannot testify; if he only had the capacity to be a litigant but it ceased, he can.

Diyat — Blood Money
beginner
6 min
Diyah Initially Due from Killing is on the 'Aqilah
Ruling about diyah being on the 'aqilah for initial killing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that diyah initially due from a killing is on the 'aqilah, and distinguish between initial and subsequent diyah in terms of liability.

Core Idea
Diyah from accidental killing is paid by the tribe, not the killer alone — solidarity for unintended harm.

This lesson introduces a core principle from the Hanafi chapter on jinayat (offenses). The rule clarifies who bears the financial burden of blood money when a killing occurs unintentionally. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying diyah in accidental deaths and recognizing the role of the 'aqilah in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Obligation
The passage speaks about 'kullu diyah' (every blood money) that becomes wajib (obligatory) bil qatl (by killing). First, note that the obligation arises from the act of killing itself.
The diyah is a financial compensation for homicide.
2
Distinguish Initial vs. Subsequent Cause
The phrase 'ibtida'an la bi ma'na yahduthu min ba'd' sets a condition: the diyah must be initially due, not due from a later‑developing cause. Understand the contrast: if the death is immediate from the act, it is 'initial'; if a wound later becomes fatal, it is 'subsequent'.
This distinction determines who pays.
3
State the Responsible Party
The rule concludes 'fahiya 'ala al‑'aqilah' – the diyah is upon the 'aqilah. The 'aqilah are the male agnates (father's side) of the killer, who collectively bear the payment.
The killer himself does not pay from his own wealth for unintentional killing.
4
Understand the Wisdom
The 'aqilah system spreads the financial burden across the tribe as a form of mutual support and solidarity, ensuring the victim's family receives compensation without impoverishing the individual actor.
This applies to accidental killing (khata') and quasi‑intentional killing (shibh al‑amd).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الجنايات (٤٣) كُلُّ دِيَةٍ وَجَبَتْ بِالْقَتْلِ ابْتِدَاءً لَا بِمَعْنًى يَحْدُتُ مِنْ بَعْدُ فَهِيَ عَلَى الْعَاقِلَةِ.
Book of Crimes: Every blood money that becomes due initially by killing, not by a meaning that arises afterwards, is upon the 'aqilah (tribe).
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This is a principle from the chapter on crimes (jinayat) in Al-Hidayah. It distinguishes between two types of diyah: one that is obligatory directly from the act of killing (such as accidental killing) and one that becomes obligatory later (such as when a wound becomes fatal). The rule states that the first type is payable by the 'aqilah (the male agnates/tribe of the killer), not the killer himself. The second type is payable by the killer individually. The 'aqilah's responsibility is a form of solidarity for unintentional killings.

Key Terms
دِيَة (diya): Blood money, compensation for homicide.
عَاقِلَة (‘āqila): The male agnates (tribe) of the killer who are responsible for paying diyah.
ابْتِدَاءً (ibtidā’an): Initially, i.e., immediately due from the act of killing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who is responsible for paying diyah initially due from a killing?
A) The killer himself.
B) The ‘aqilah (tribe).
C) The state.
D) The victim's family.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the 'aqilah is responsible for diyah arising directly from an unintentional killing.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
10 min
Principle of Diyah on the 'Aqilah and Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Shibh al-'Amd
Establishes principle of diyah on 'aqilah and mentions internal Hanafi dispute on shibh al-'amd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that diyah initially wajib from killing is on the 'aqilah, explain its application to shibh al-'amd, and distinguish the two positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the mojib of shibh al-'amd.

Core Idea
Diyah from killing is on the 'aqilah unless it arises from a later cause — 'amd is treated differently.

This lesson examines a foundational Hanafi rule in the law of diyat (blood money). The passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah establishes that any diyah that becomes obligatory directly due to the act of killing — not from a subsequent agreement or confession — must be paid by the killer's 'aqilah (tribal support group). The author then applies this rule to shibh al-'amd (semi-intentional killing) and notes an internal Hanafi dispute about the precise legal consequences (mojib) of this category. By studying this passage, you will learn not only the substantive rule but also how Hanafi jurists analyze exceptions and intra-madhhab differences.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the core principle
Identify the rule: every diyah initially wajib from killing is on the 'aqilah. Note that 'initially' excludes cases where diyah becomes wajib later (e.g., settlement).
The opening sentence of the passage states this explicitly.
2
Distinguish intentional killing ('amd)
In 'amd, qisas is initially wajib, so if diyah becomes due (e.g., by settlement), it is on the killer's own wealth, not the 'aqilah.
The passage contrasts 'amd with other categories.
3
Apply to shibh al-'amd
Since shibh al-'amd triggers diyah directly, the principle applies: diyah mughallazah is on the 'aqilah.
The author explicitly states this application.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Both Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn agree on sin, kaffarah, and diyah on the 'aqilah as the mojib. The exact difference is not detailed here, but it does not affect diyah liability.
The quote from al-'Inayah confirms agreement on these three elements.
5
Identify the exceptions
Scenarios where diyah is on the killer personally: settlement after 'amd, father killing son intentionally, confession to mistake when killing was intentional.
These exceptions are listed in the quoted text.

كتاب الجنايات (۴۳) كُلُّ دِيَةٍ وَجَبَتْ بِالْقَتْلِ ابْتِدَاءً لَا بِمَعْنًى يَحْدُتُ مِنْ بَعْدُ فَهِيَ عَلَى الْعَاقِلَةِ. ترجمہ: ہر وہ دیت جو ابتداء قتل کی وجہ سے واجب ہو، اس علت کی وجہ سے نہ ہو، جو قتل کے بعد پیدا ہوتو وہ عاقلہ پر ہوگی۔ تشريح مذکور و اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ وہ دیت جو شروع ہی سے نفس قتل کی وجہ سے واجب ہو تو وہ قاتل کے عاقلہ پر ہوگی اور اگر دیت ابتداء قتل کی وجہ سے واجب نہ ہو یعنی مقتل کی وجہ سے تو قصاص واجب تھا البتہ بعد میں کسی علت کی وجہ سے واجب ہوا ہو، جیسے قتل عمد میں مال پر صلح کر لیا تو یہ دیت قاتل کے مال سے دی جائے گی نہ کہ عاقلہ کے مال سے، خلاصہ یہ کہ قتل عمد میں اگر قصاص ساقط ہو جائے کسی وجہ سے تو دیت قاتل کے مال سے ادا کی جائے گی، کیونکہ قتل عمد میں نفس قتل سے ابتداء دیت واجب نہیں ہوتی بلکہ ابتداء قصاص واجب ہوتا ہے، اور قتل کی باقی چار قسموں میں دیت عاقلہ پر ہوگی کیونکہ قتل عمد کے علاوہ باقی میں نفس قتل سے ابتدائ دیت ہی واجب ہوتی ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ مقتل شبہ عمدہ میں دیت مخلفہ عاقلہ پر واجب ہوگی، کیونکہ شب عمد میں قتل کی وجہ سے ابتدا ہی دیت واجب ہوتی ہے اور ہر وہ دیت جو ابتداء نفس قتل سے واجب ہو وہ عاقلہ پر ہوتی ہے، لہذ اقتل شبہ عمد میں بھی دیت عاقلہ پر ہوگی۔ وقوله ( وموجب ذلك أى موجب شبه العمد على القولين: يعنى قول أبي حنيفة وقولهما (الإثم لأنه قتل وهو قاصد فى الضرب) على ما مر من تفسيره والكفارة لشبهه بالخطأ، والدية مغلظة على العاقلة والأصل أن كل دية وجبت بالقتل ابتداء لا بمعنى يحدث من بعد فهى على العاقلة احترز بقوله لا بمعنى يحدث من بعد عما تصالحوا فيه على الدية وعن قتل الوالد ولده عمدا وعن إقرار القاتل بالقتل خطأ وقد كان قتله عمدا فإن في هذه الصورة تجب الدية على القاتل في ماله العناية كتاب الجنايات، ح ۱۰ ص ۲۱۲ قال وموجب ذلك أى موجب شبه العمد على القولين أى على قول أبي حنيفة رَحِمَهُ اللَّـهُ وقولهما: (الإثم لأنه قتل وهو قاصد في الضرب
Book of Jinayat (43): Every diyah that becomes obligatory due to killing initially, not due to a meaning that arises afterwards, is upon the 'aqilah. Translation: Every blood money that is wajib from the beginning due to the act of killing, not due to a cause that arises after the killing, is upon the 'aqilah. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that the diyah which is wajib from the beginning due to the very act of killing is upon the killer's 'aqilah. And if the diyah is not wajib initially from the killing (i.e., due to the killing), rather qisas was wajib, but later became wajib due to some cause, such as settling for wealth in intentional killing, then this diyah is paid from the killer's wealth, not from the 'aqilah's wealth. In summary, in intentional killing ('amd), if qisas is dropped for some reason, the diyah is paid from the killer's wealth, because in 'amd, diyah is not initially wajib from the act of killing; rather, qisas is initially wajib. In the other four types of killing, the diyah is upon the 'aqilah, because in all except 'amd, diyah is initially wajib from the act of killing. The author of Hidayah, after mentioning this principle, is stating that in shibh al-'amd (semi-intentional killing), the heavy diyah (mughallazah) is wajib upon the 'aqilah, because in shibh al-'amd, diyah is initially wajib due to the killing, and every diyah that is wajib initially from the act of killing is upon the 'aqilah. Therefore, in shibh al-'amd, the diyah is upon the 'aqilah. And his saying: 'And the mojib of that, i.e., the mojib of shibh al-'amd, is according to two opinions: meaning the saying of Abu Hanifa and their saying (the Sahibayn): (sin because he killed while intending the blow) according to its interpretation that passed, and kaffarah because it resembles mistake, and the heavy diyah upon the 'aqilah, and the principle that every diyah that becomes wajib due to killing initially, not due to a meaning that arises afterwards, is upon the 'aqilah. He restricts by his saying 'not due to a meaning that arises afterwards' to cases where they settled upon diyah, and to the case of a father killing his son intentionally, and to the case of a killer confessing to mistake killing while actually he killed intentionally; for in these situations the diyah becomes wajib upon the killer in his wealth.' (Al-'Inayah, Kitab al-Jinayat, vol. 10 p. 212) He said: 'And the mojib of that, i.e., the mojib of shibh al-'amd, is according to the two opinions, i.e., according to the saying of Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) and their saying: (sin because he killed while intending the blow)...'
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Hidayah explains a core Hanafi principle regarding diyah (blood money). The principle: any diyah that becomes obligatory directly due to the act of killing (not from a later cause like a settlement) is to be paid by the 'aqilah (the tribal group responsible for the killer). The passage then elaborates by contrasting intentional killing ('amd) where qisas is originally wajib, and diyah becomes wajib only if qisas is dropped (e.g., by settlement). In that case, the diyah is on the killer personally, not the 'aqilah. For all other categories of killing (shibh al-'amd, khata', etc.), diyah is initially wajib and thus on the 'aqilah. The commentator clarifies that the author of Hidayah presents this to show that in shibh al-'amd, the diyah (which is mughallazah, heavy) is on the 'aqilah. The passage then quotes from al-'Inayah, which mentions an intra-Hanafi dispute: the 'mojib' (what is necessitated) of shibh al-'amd is subject to two opinions—that of Abu Hanifa and that of the Sahibayn. According to both, the mojib includes sin (ism) because the killer intended the act, and kaffarah (expiation) because it resembles a mistake. The diyah, heavy, is on the 'aqilah. The quote from 'Inayah also lists exceptions: where the diyah becomes wajib on the killer's own property, such as when the parties settle for diyah, a father kills his son intentionally, or the killer confesses to mistake killing when it was actually intentional. These are cases where the diyah is not wajib 'initially' from the act of killing but due to a subsequent cause. The passage thus emphasizes the importance of the initial cause (ibtida') in determining who bears the diyah.

Key Terms
دية (diyah): Blood money/compensation paid for unlawful killing (except where qisas applies)
عاقلة (āqilah): The group of male relatives (tribe) who bear responsibility for paying diyah on behalf of the killer
شبه عمد (shibh al-ʿamd): Semi-intentional killing: deliberately striking but not intending to kill, and the strike is not usually lethal
موجب (mūjib): The mandatory consequences/liabilities that arise from an act
مغلظة (mughallaẓah): Heavy or aggravated (diyah) with increased amount and often stricter conditions

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle stated in the passage, a person commits shibh al-'amd (semi-intentional killing). Who bears the diyah?
A) The killer must pay from his own wealth
B) The 'aqilah (tribal group) of the killer
C) The state treasury (bayt al-mal)
D) No diyah is due because the killer did not intend to kill

Key Takeaway
The core principle: any diyah initially wajib from killing falls on the 'aqilah; shibh al-'amd is included, with mughallazah diyah.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate

Diyah for Shibh 'Amd: Liability on the 'Aqilah
Explaining the principle of diyah liability in shibh 'amd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the principle that determines whether diyah is on the 'aqilah or the killer's own wealth, and apply it to shibh 'amd.

Core Idea
Diyah is on the 'aqilah when it is initially due to the killing itself; otherwise it is on the killer.

This lesson examines a key principle from Hanafi fiqh regarding blood money (diyah). The author distinguishes between cases where diyah is the initial obligation (due from the 'aqilah) and cases where it arises after qisas is dropped (due from the killer personally). We focus on shibh 'amd (semi-intentional killing) as a paradigm case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for shibh 'amd
State the ruling: in shibh 'amd, the diyah is heavy and obligatory on the 'aqilah.
2
Trace the underlying principle
The principle: any diyah that is initially obligatory due to the act of killing is on the 'aqilah. If diyah becomes obligatory only after qisas is dropped, it is on the killer's own wealth.
3
Apply the principle to shibh 'amd
In shibh 'amd, the killing does not mandate qisas initially; rather, diyah is the initial obligation. Hence it falls on the 'aqilah.
4
Note the exceptions
Exceptions where diyah is on the killer: (1) settlement for diyah after intentional killing, (2) father killing son intentionally, (3) killer confessing to accidental killing when it was intentional.
5
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The text mentions 'two sayings' of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, but the operative ruling remains that diyah is on the 'aqilah.

Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage from Usool E Hidayah explains a key principle regarding the liability for diyah (blood money) in cases of killing. The author distinguishes between situations where diyah is obligatory from the very beginning due to the act of killing itself, and where it becomes obligatory later after qisas is dropped. The ruling is that any diyah which is initially obligatory (such as in categories of killing other than intentional murder) is to be paid by the 'aqilah (the killer's tribe or relatives, responsible for paying blood money). In intentional murder, the initial obligation is qisas (retribution), not diyah. If qisas is later dropped, e.g., due to a settlement, then the diyah becomes obligatory on the killer's own wealth, not on the 'aqilah. This principle is then applied to shibh 'amd (semi-intentional killing), which is one of the categories between 'amd and khata. The text clarifies that in shibh 'amd, because the action is intentional but not with the intent to kill, the initial obligation is diyah, and thus it falls on the 'aqilah. The author quotes from the Hidayah and 'Inayah to support this, mentioning that the requirement of shibh 'amd includes sin (since it is a form of intentional wrongdoing), expiation (due to its resemblance to mistake), and heavy diyah on the 'aqilah. Exceptions are mentioned where the diyah lies on the killer's own wealth, such as settling for diyah after intentional killing, a father killing his son intentionally, or the killer confessing to accidental killing when it was intentional.

Key Terms
عاقلہ ('aqilah): The 'aqilah are the paternal relatives (asabah) of the killer who bear responsibility for paying diyah in cases of accidental or semi-intentional killing.
دیت (diyah): Blood money, a financial compensation paid to the heirs of the victim in homicide cases where qisas is not applicable or is waived.
شبہ عمد (shibh 'amd): Semi-intentional killing, where the act was intentional (e.g., striking with a stick) but the death was not intended, and the instrument is not typically fatal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The killer's own wealth
B) The 'aqilah (the killer's tribe)
C) The state treasury
D) The victim's family

Key Takeaway
In shibh 'amd, the diyah is heavy and obligatory on the 'aqilah because it is initially due from the act of killing itself, not after a dropped qisas.

Diyat — Blood Money

Diyah When the Victim Apostatizes After Being Shot
Intra-Hanafi dispute on diyah for an apostate killed by arrow

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on diyah for an apostate killed by an arrow and explain the reasoning behind the intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Core Idea
Liability is determined at the time of the act, not the victim's later status; thus diyah is due even if the victim apostatizes.

This lesson examines a nuanced case from the Hanafi school: a man shoots another who is Muslim, but before the arrow strikes, the target apostatizes and dies as an apostate. Does the shooter owe blood money (diyah)? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two students differ on this point. Understanding their disagreement requires us to fix the moment of liability.

Guided Path
1
State the facts of the case
Identify the timeline: the shooter (rami) shoots at a target (marmi ilayhi) who is Muslim at the moment of shooting. After the arrow is released, the target apostatizes and dies in that state.
The key is the separation between the act (ramy) and the death (wusul al-sahm).
2
Identify the disputed ruling
Abu Hanifa holds that diyah is wajib upon the shooter for the heirs of the apostate. Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that nothing is due—neither diyah nor qisas.
Both agree qisas is dropped due to apostasy creating doubt (shubha).
3
Trace the opposing proofs
Sahibayn argue that apostasy nullifies the victim's value (taqawwum nafsihi), discharging the guarantor (ibra' al-damin). Abu Hanifa argues that liability (daman) arises from the act of shooting; at that moment the victim was a Muslim and thus possessed legal value.
Abu Hanifa uses analogy to ghasp (usurpation) where liability is based on the act.
4
Apply the Hanafi usul principle
The principle 'al-ibrah bi al-fi'l' (consideration is given to the act) governs: the time of the act determines the ruling. Since the act was against a Muslim, diyah is due, though qisas is dropped due to subsequent apostasy as a doubt.
This is distinct from the Shafi'i position (not discussed here) which might consider the time of death.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Under Abu Hanifa, the shooter pays diyah to the apostate's heirs. Under Sahibayn, no payment is made. The mufta bihi (operative view) is not specified in this text, but later Hanafi practice often follows Abu Hanifa.
This difference shows the importance of the principle chosen for the moment of liability.

Source Text

First he became an apostate, then the arrow struck and he died in the state of apostasy. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, diyah is wajib on the shooter, because in the aforementioned case, at the time of the shooter's act, the target was a Muslim, and consideration is given to the state at the time of the act from the perspective of the doer. In the mentioned case, at the time of the act, the target was a Muslim, and killing a Muslim necessitates qisas, but because in the aforementioned case qisas is dropped due to doubt, diyah is wajib. Kitab al-Diyat. And from al-'Inayah: Book of Criminal Injuries, chapter on considering the state at the time of killing, vol. 10, p. 267: 'And whoever shoots a Muslim, and the target apostatizes (God forbid), then the arrow hits him while he is an apostate, then upon the shooter is the diyah for the heirs of the apostate according to Abu Hanifa. They (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: Nothing is upon him, because by apostasy he nullified his worth, and that is a discharge for the guarantor... And for Abu Hanifa is that liability is established by his act, which is the shooting, since there is no act from him after it, and what is such then the time of the act is considered, like usurpation, so the state at the time of shooting is considered, and the target at that time was of value.' And from al-Binayah: Book of Criminal Injuries, chapter on considering the state at the time of killing, vol. 13, p. 156: 'And whoever shoots a Muslim, and the target apostatizes (God forbid), then the arrow hits him while he is an apostate, then upon the shooter is the diyah according to Abu Hanifa, meaning for the heirs of the apostate. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: Nothing is upon him of diyah or qisas... And for him (Abu Hanifa) is that liability is established by his act, which is the shooting, since there is no act from him after it, and what is such, then the state at the time of shooting is considered, and the target at that time is of value.' Al-Hidayah, Book of Diyat, vol. 4, p. 577.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the payment of diyah (blood money) when a person is shot while a Muslim but apostatizes before the arrow hits and dies in apostasy. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that diyah is wajib because the liability (daman) is established at the time of the act (al-ramy). At that moment, the victim was a Muslim, so the act was against a Muslim. Even though qisas is dropped due to the victim's apostasy (which creates a doubt), diyah remains as a financial obligation. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that nothing is owed because apostasy nullifies the value of the victim's life, thereby discharging the guarantor. Abu Hanifa's proof is that the moment of the act determines liability, analogous to ghasp (usurpation). The relied-upon position is not explicitly stated in this text, but Abu Hanifa's view is presented as the primary one.

Key Terms
دية (diyah): Blood money; financial compensation for killing or injury.
ارتداد (irtidad): Apostasy from Islam.
ضمان (daman): Liability or guarantee; here it refers to the obligation to pay diyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is diyah wajib in the case of the apostate?
A) Because the shooter intended to kill a Muslim.
B) Because liability is established at the time of the act (al-ramy).
C) Because the victim's worth is not affected by apostasy.
D) Because qisas is still applicable.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, liability for homicide attaches at the moment of the injurious act, and a subsequent change in the victim's status does not remove the obligation to pay diyah, though it may prevent qisas.

Fixed Measures Are Known by Divine Prescription: Application to Kaffarah of Qatl
Legal maxim and its application to kaffarah of qatl and diyah dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that fixed measures are known only by divine prescription, apply it to kaffarah of qatl, and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Shafi'i/Ahmad view.

Core Idea
Fixed measures in Shari'ah are determined by revelation, not qiyas; thus kaffarah of qatl only includes freeing a slave or fasting.

This lesson examines the foundational Hanafi principle 'al-maqadir tu'raf bi al-tawqif'—that all prescribed quantities in Islamic law are known solely through divine texts. The author of al-Hidayah applies this to the expiation for killing, excluding feeding (it'am) as a valid option. We will walk through the evidence and the inter-madhhab dispute, as well as an intra-Hanafi illustration regarding diyah.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its meaning
Read the principle 'al-maqadir tu'raf bi al-tawqif' and understand that it means all fixed quantities (like amounts of kaffarah, hadd punishments, etc.) are defined by divine texts, not by human reasoning.
Tawqif refers to revelation; qiyas has no role in determining these measures.
2
Identify the Quranic evidence for kaffarah of qatl
Locate Quran 4:92 which specifies the options: freeing a believing slave or fasting two consecutive months. Note that feeding is not mentioned.
The verse is qati' al-thubut and qati' al-dalalah.
3
Analyze why feeding is excluded in the Hanafi school
Because the maxim forbids adding to the prescribed measures via qiyas. The absence of feeding in the nass means it is not a valid option, regardless of analogy to other kaffarat.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf: the practical outcome of the principle.
4
Examine the opposing view and the Hanafi response
Imam al-Shafi'i (in one view) and Imam Ahmad allow feeding by analogy to the kaffarah for zihar or breaking a fast. The Hanafi response is that fixed measures cannot be analogized.
The debate is mukhtalaf fih; the Hanafi position is mufta bihi.
5
Apply the maxim to an intra-Hanafi case on diyah of an apostate
The passage also discusses a scenario: shooting a Muslim who then apostatizes before the arrow hits. Abu Hanifa holds diyah is due (value assessed at time of shooting), while the Sahibayn say no. This illustrates that the measure (diyah) is fixed at the time of the causal act, consistent with tawqif.
This shows the principle's application beyond kaffarah to other fixed measures like diyah.

Commentary: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that whatever fixed measures exist in Shari'ah, for example expiations, their recognition is from the Shari'ah, meaning they are determined by the Lawgiver. Qiyas and opinion have no intervention in them. The author of Hidayah, after mentioning this principle, states that in the kaffarah of killing, feeding (it'am) is not sufficient, because no text has come concerning it. Moreover, these are from the fixed measures of Shari'ah, and whatever is from the fixed measures of Shari'ah, qiyas does not apply to it. Therefore, in kaffarah of killing, qiyas will also not apply to consider feeding as sufficient by analogizing to other kaffarat.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage presents the legal maxim 'Fixed measures are known by divine prescription' (al-maqadir tu'raf bi al-tawqif). The author explains that all fixed quantities in Shari'ah, such as those for kaffarat, are determined solely by revelation and not by human reasoning (qiyas). He then applies this to the kaffarah for killing (qatl shibh amd): only freeing a slave or fasting two months are accepted, and feeding (it'am) is not sufficient because no text supports it. This is supported by the Quranic verse 4:92. The passage also notes a disagreement where Imam Shafi'i and Ahmad (in one narration) allowed feeding if unable to fast. However, the Hanafi position rejects qiyas in these prescribed measures. The earlier Arabic quotes from 'Inayah and Binayah about the diyah of an apostate are used as supporting evidence for the principle that measures are fixed and considered at the time of the act.

Key Terms
مقادير (maqadir): Fixed measures or prescribed quantities in Shari'ah, such as amounts of kaffarat.
توقيف (tawqif): Divine prescription; knowledge that comes only from revelatory texts, not from reason.
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiatory act for sins or violations, such as killing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because feeding is forbidden in Shari'ah.
D) Because the poor do not deserve it.

Key Takeaway
Fixed measures in Shari'ah are known only by divine prescription; therefore, kaffarah of qatl cannot include feeding because it lacks textual support and qiyas is impermissible in this domain.

Full Diyah for Complete Loss of Function or Beauty
Ruling: full diyah for complete function or beauty loss

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions that require full diyah and apply them to scenarios of complete loss of function or beauty.

Core Idea
Full diyah is due when the entire limb function or intended beauty is completely destroyed.

This lesson presents a foundational rule from the Hanafi law of diyat (blood money). It establishes when full diyah is wajib for injuries that do not kill but completely remove the benefit of a limb or destroy an aspect of intended beauty. The rule is concise and serves as a memorization anchor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two grounds
Read the rule carefully: it mentions two independent conditions – complete loss of benefit of limbs, and complete removal of intended beauty in a person.
These are alternative; either qualifies for full diyah.
2
Understand 'ala al-kamal'
The phrase 'ala al-kamal' means completely. Partial loss does not trigger full diyah.
Complete loss means the entire function or beauty is gone.
3
Distinguish from partial loss
Recall that for partial loss (e.g., one finger), the diyah is proportional (hukumat 'adl). This rule applies only when the whole type of benefit is lost.
4
Apply to examples
Practice: total paralysis of a hand qualifies; loss of one finger does not. Complete facial scarring destroying beauty qualifies; a minor scar does not.

Translation: If the complete benefit of limbs is lost entirely or a person's intended beauty is completely removed, full diyah is wajib. Commentary
This passage presents a rule from the chapter on injuries less than life (dun an-nafs). The rule states that if the complete function of a limb is lost or if the intended beauty of a person (e.g., face, hair) is completely destroyed, then full diyah is wajib. The commentary then includes a cross-reference to a previous discussion on kaffarah for killing, reiterating that only freeing a slave or fasting is valid, and feeding is not, due to lack of textual evidence and the principle 'al-maqadir tu'raf bi al-tawqif'. The passage ends with citations from 'Inayah, Binayah, and Hidayah. Although the latter part seems unrelated, it emphasizes the principle that fixed measures are known only by revelation.

Key Terms
دية (diyah): Blood money; here full diyah is the complete amount prescribed for the value of a person (100 camels or equivalent).
منفعة (manfa'ah): Function or utility of a limb; here 'jins manfa'ah' means the entire function of a limb.
جمال مقصود (jamal maqsud): Intended beauty, i.e., the natural beauty that is normally intended and appreciated, such as the face or hair.

Key Takeaway
Full diyah is wajib only when the entire benefit of a limb or the intended beauty is completely destroyed, not for partial loss.

Principle of Maqadir and Kaffarah for Quasi-Intentional Killing
Explains the usul principle and its application to kaffarah for quasi-intentional killing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school disallows feeding (it'am) as expiation for quasi-intentional killing, applying the principle that prescribed amounts are known only by revelation, and evaluate the opposing Shafi'i/Ahmad position.

Core Idea
Prescribed amounts in Islamic law are known only by revelation, not human reasoning.

The Hanafi school insists that certain legal quantities, such as expiations and blood-money, are fixed exclusively by the Lawgiver. In this passage, the author of Al-Hidayah applies this principle to the kaffarah for quasi-intentional killing, restricting it to the two options mentioned in the Quran. This puts the Hanafi position in direct conflict with other schools that allow analogy to other expiations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Issue
What is the kaffarah for qatl shibh al-amd (quasi-intentional killing)? The Quran specifies freeing a slave or fasting two consecutive months. The dispute is whether feeding sixty poor people is an alternative when fasting is not possible.
Focus on the specific question of it'am.
2
Locate the Hanafi Evidence
The Hanafi school cites Quran 4:92, which mentions only freeing a slave and fasting, and the usul principle 'al-maqadir tu'raf bi al-tawqif' – prescribed amounts are known only by revelation.
Notice that the verse is presented as a clear text (nass) that exhausts the options.
3
Identify the Opposing Position
Al-Shafi'i (in one opinion) and Ahmad ibn Hanbal (in one narration) allow it'am by analogy to other expiations where feeding is prescribed, such as in zihar or breaking an oath.
They use qiyas to extend the ruling.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi response is that analogy is not valid in the realm of prescribed amounts (maqadir). Such matters are restricted to what the Lawgiver has explicitly stated; human reasoning cannot add alternatives.
This reflects the Hanafi usul that in 'ibadat and fixed penalties, qiyas is not permissible when the text is exhaustive.
5
Determine the Practical Difference
Under the Hanafi position, if the culprit cannot fast, there is no alternative – he must fast when able. Under the Shafi'i/Ahmad view, he would feed sixty poor. This affects the actual implementation of the punishment.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf – the concrete outcome of the disagreement.

Commentary
This passage from Al-Hidayah and its commentaries discusses the principle that legally prescribed amounts (maqadir) are known only through divine revelation (tawqif), not through analogy (qiyas). The author first applies this to the expiation (kaffarah) for unintentional killing: the Quran specifies only freeing a slave or fasting two consecutive months, so feeding the poor (it'am) is not permissible because no textual evidence supports it. This refutes the opinion of Al-Shafi'i and Ahmad in one narration, who analogize to other expiations. The principle is then applied to diyat (blood money) for injuries below the soul. The ruling: if the entire kind of benefit of a limb is lost completely (e.g., the tongue cut off or both hands paralyzed) or if a desired beauty is completely removed (e.g., the soft part of the nose destroyed), full diyah (100 camels) is wajib. The text explains that this is because the limb or beauty stands in place of the whole body. It then categorizes limbs into singular, paired, fourfold, tenfold, etc., and reaffirms that full diyah is only for complete loss; partial loss requires proportional compensation.

Key Terms
المقادير (al-maqadir): Legally prescribed amounts or quantities, such as those for kaffarah, diyat, etc. Hanafis hold that these are known only by tawqif, not by qiyas.
التوقيف (al-tawqif): The dependence of knowledge on revelation; that rulings are established by explicit textual evidence. In Hanafi usul, this term emphasizes that certain matters cannot be known by reason alone.
الدية (al-diyah): Full blood-money payable for homicide or loss of a limb/benefit equivalent to the whole body. Full diyah is 100 camels; for partial losses, proportional amounts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is it'am not valid as kaffarah in qatl shibh al-amd?
A) Because the person can always fast
B) Because it is not mentioned in the Quran or Sunnah for this specific case
C) Because it is too easy compared to freeing a slave
D) Because it is analogically derived from other kaffarat

Key Takeaway
Prescribed amounts in Islamic law are fixed by revelation alone; analogy cannot introduce alternatives when the text is exhaustive.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
7 min
Ruling on Complete Loss of Limb Benefit or Beauty: Full Diyah
Explains the ruling for complete loss of limb benefit or beauty and categorizes limbs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for full diyah (complete loss of entire benefit or beauty of a limb), identify examples of such loss, and distinguish it from partial loss which requires proportional compensation.

Core Idea
Full diyah is due only when the entire kind of benefit or beauty of a limb is lost completely.

The same passage that discusses the principle of maqadir also lays down the rule for diyat when harm is below the level of killing. The key is that full blood-money is not due for any loss, but only for a complete loss of the entire function or beauty of a limb. This lesson unpacks that condition and its application to various body parts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The passage states: 'When the entire kind of benefit of the limbs is lost completely, or a desired beauty in a human is removed completely, the full blood-money is obligatory.' This is the ruling.
Note the two criteria: loss of benefit (manfa'ah) or loss of beauty (jamal).
2
Understand the Condition
The condition for full diyah is that the loss must be complete (ala al-kamal) and of the entire kind (jins) of benefit or beauty. A partial loss does not trigger full diyah.
The text emphasizes 'ala al-kamal' to exclude incomplete losses.
3
Apply to Examples from the Text
Examples: complete loss of speech (tongue), complete loss of vision (both eyes), removal of the nose (soft part). For paired limbs, loss of both results in full diyah; loss of one requires half.
The text categorizes limbs: singular (nose, tongue) vs paired (eyes, ears).
4
Distinguish Partial Loss
If only part of the benefit is lost (e.g., partial hearing) or beauty is partially diminished, the compensation is proportional, not full diyah.
The text says: 'incomplete does not require full diyah.'
5
Recognize the Principle
The underlying principle is that the limb or beauty is treated as a unit; its complete loss is equivalent to the loss of the whole person in terms of blood-money valuation.
This is specific to diyat; the same logic does not apply to other penalties.

Chapter on what is below the soul. When the entire kind of benefit of the limbs is lost completely, or a desired beauty in a human is removed completely, the full blood-money is obligatory. Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
10 min
Full Diyah for Complete Loss of Benefit or Beauty
Explains the principle of full diyah for loss of complete limb benefit or beauty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for full diyah and identify which conditions (complete loss of benefit or beauty) trigger it.

Core Idea
Full diyah is due only when the complete benefit or intended beauty of a limb is destroyed — partial loss does not suffice.

This lesson explores a key principle in Hanafi diyat law: the obligation of full blood money (100 camels or equivalent) for the complete loss of a limb's function or aesthetic value. You will learn the rule, its proof from the classical texts (al-Hidayah and al-Inayah), and how it applies to different types of limbs. The commentary expands the terse principle into a detailed classification.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify the legal ruling: full diyah (wajib) when the complete type of benefit (jins manfa'ah) of a limb is lost or the intended beauty (jamal maqsud) is completely removed.
The text says 'يجب كل الدية' — it is an obligation.
2
Locate the evidence
Find the proof: 'و الأصل فى الأطراف أنه إذا فوت جنس منفعة على الكمال أو أزال جمالا مقصودا في الآدمي على الكمال يجب كل الدية'. This is the foundation of the rule.
The evidence is a general principle derived from prophetic traditions and Hanafi ijtihad.
3
Analyze the condition of completeness
Notice the restriction 'على الكمال' (completely). Partial loss of benefit or beauty does NOT trigger full diyah. The commentary emphasises this: 'وقيد المنفعة والجمال بالكمال'.
This is the key distinction — students often miss the completeness requirement.
4
Examine the limb classifications
The text from al-Inayah lists limb categories: singular (nose, tongue, penis), paired (eyes, ears, etc.), four (eyelashes), ten (fingers), and more (teeth). Full diyah under the principle applies to any limb, but the amount may vary for paired limbs (each has half diyah unless both lost).
The classification helps understand when full diyah is due for singular vs. multiple limbs.
5
Apply to a concrete example
Apply the principle: someone cuts off another's tongue, causing complete loss of speech — full diyah wajib. Someone breaks the soft cartilage of the nose, destroying its shape (intended beauty) — full diyah wajib.
Partial loss of speech or minor cosmetic change would not trigger full diyah.

Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that if from the limbs of a human, due to a blow, the complete type of benefit of a limb is lost, for example the tongue is cut or both hands become paralyzed, or the intended beauty in a human is completely removed, for example the soft bone of the nose is broken etc., then in such cases full diyah, i.e., one hundred camels, are obligatory, because in this case one limb takes the place of the entire body. The author of Hidayah, after mentioning the mentioned principle, is informing that if a person struck another's nose and the soft bone was broken, or cut the tongue, then full diyah is obligatory, because in the second case the type of benefit of the limb is completely lost, and when the type of benefit of extremities is completely lost, full diyah is obligatory, therefore full diyah is obligatory in this case; and in the first case the intended beauty of the person is completely removed, and in such a case full diyah is obligatory, therefore in the first case (soft bone) also full diyah is obligatory. And his saying: (And for the soft part of the nose, the diyah) meaning for what is below the nasal bone, which is its soft part. And he restricted benefit and beauty to completeness, because incomplete does not necessitate full diyah. (Al-Inayah: Kitab al-Diyat, Fasl fima duna al-Nafs, vol. 10, p. 279)
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof from a commentary on al-Hidayah. It establishes a key principle in the law of diyat (blood money): full diyah (100 camels) is due when a limb's entire type of benefit or the intended beauty of a person is completely destroyed. The commentary first explains the principle in Urdu: if a blow causes complete loss of a limb's function (like cutting the tongue or paralyzing both hands) or complete loss of intended beauty (like breaking the nose's cartilage), full diyah is owed. The commentary then cites the Arabic text from al-Inayah which provides the detailed rule and classification of limbs: singular (nose, tongue, penis), paired (eyes, ears, etc.), four (eyelashes), ten (fingers), and more (teeth). The underlying rationale is that complete destruction of a limb's benefit or beauty is analogous to destroying the person from that aspect, and thus treated like complete destruction. The text emphasizes the restriction to complete loss; partial loss does not trigger full diyah.

Key Terms
دية (diyah): Blood money, specifically 100 camels or their equivalent, payable for homicide or severe bodily harm.
جنس منفعة (jins manfa'ah): The type of benefit of a limb, e.g., speech for the tongue, grip for the hand.
جمال مقصود (jamal maqsud): Intended beauty, e.g., the shape of the nose or ear that is aesthetically valued.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Loss of a paired limb only
D) Injury that leaves a visible scar

Key Takeaway
Full diyah is wajib only when the complete benefit or intended beauty of a limb is destroyed, not for partial loss.

الْمَنَافِعُ لَا تَتَقَوَّمُ إِلَّا بِعَقْدٍ أَوْ بِشُبُهَةٍ: Benefits Not Compensable Except by Contract or Semblance
States the Hanafi maxim and applies it to wound compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim on compensability of benefits to cases of injury and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding compensation for lost benefits.

Core Idea
Benefits are not property unless obtained through a contract or its semblance.

The Hanafi school distinguishes between tangible property and mere benefits. This lesson explores the maxim that benefits are not compensable unless linked to a contract or its semblance. We will see how this principle applies to wound compensation and why the Sahibayn differed from Abu Hanifa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its rationale
Read the maxim carefully. Understand that benefits (manafi') are considered a'rad (accidents) without permanence, hence not property unless attached to a contract or its semblance.
Note that the term 'shubhah' refers to void contracts like fasid ijara.
2
Apply the maxim to the wound case
The author applies the maxim to a specific scenario: a wound heals and hair grows back. According to Abu Hanifa, no compensation is due because the benefit lost (use of the head) is not compensable without a contract.
Compare with the slap example: no compensation for pain.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's objections
Abu Yusuf argued based on the pain inflicted; Muhammad argued for the doctor's fee. Both consider these as compensable, but Abu Hanifa rejects this because they are benefits without contract.
The Hanafi response: pain and treatment are benefits, thus not compensable without contract.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically, Abu Hanifa's view means no monetary compensation for lost use or pain unless there is a contract. The Sahibayn would award some compensation. The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's position.
This ikhtilaf is within the Hanafi school, not cross-madhhab.
5
Review the additional Arabic quotations
The passage includes quotes from Tabyin al-Haqa'iq, al-Hidayah, al-Inayah, and al-Binayah reinforcing the principle and the dispute. Study these to see the chain of authority.
The full diyah rule is mentioned to contrast with partial loss of benefit.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(۵۵) الْمَنَافِعُ لَا تَتَقَوَّمُ إِلَّا بِعَقْدٍ أَوْ بِشُبُهَةٍ .
ترجمہ: منافع متقوم نہیں ہوتے مگر عقد سے یا عقد کے شبہ سے۔
۲۵۷
تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جتنے بھی منافع ہیں وہ متقوم نہیں ہوتے یعنی ان کے مقابلے میں عوض نہیں لیا جا تا الا یہ کہ جب عقد ہو جائے ، مثلاً اجارہ صحیحہ یا مضاربت صحیحہ وغیرہ یا عقد کا شبہ ہو، مثلاً اجارہ فاسدہ یا مضاربت فاسدہ وغیرہ ، تو پھر یہ منافع متقوم ہو جاتے ہیں، کیونکہ منافع اعراض ہیں یعنی ان کے کے لئے بقاء نہیں ہوتی ، اور جو چیز اعراض کے قبیل سے ہو وہ متقوم نہیں ہوتی۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ اگر کسی نے دوسرے کے سر میں زخم لگایا، پھر زخم بھر آیا اور زخم کا اثر جاتا رہا ، پھر دوبارہ اُس کے سر پر بال اُگ آئے تو امام صاحب کے نزدیک تاوان ساقط ہو جائے گا، کیونکہ زخم کا تاوان یا اس کا خرچہ ایک منفعت ہے اور منافع بغیر عقد اور شبہ عقد کے مسلوم نہیں ہوتے ، اور یہ دونوں چیزیں ( عقد ، شبہ عقد ) زخم لگانے والے کے حق میں نہیں ، لہذ امذکورہ بالا صورت میں بھی یہ متقوم نہیں ہوگا اور جب یہ متقوم نہیں ہے تو اس کے مقابلے میں تاوان نہیں آئے گا، جیسے کوئی کسی کو تھپڑ مارے تو اُس
پر تاوان نہیں ہے، البتہ قاضی وقت بطور تنبیہ کوئی سزا دینا چاہے تو اس کی گنجائش ہے۔
وَالأَصْلُ فِي الْأَعْضَاءِ أَنَّهُ إِذَا فَوَّتَ جِئْسَ [؟] مَنْفَعَةٍ عَلَى الْكَمَالِ أَوْ أَزَالَ جَمَالًا مَقْصُودًا فِي الْآدَمِيٌّ عَلَى الْكَمَالِ يَجِبُ كُلُّ الدِّيَةِ، لَّانَّ فِيهِ إِتَّلَافَ النَّفْسِ مِنْ وَجُهِ إذ النَّفْسُ لَا تَبْقَى مُنْتَفَعًا بِهَا مِنْ ذَلِكَ الْوَجْهِ وَإِتِّلَافُ النَّفْسِ مِنْ وَجْهِ مُلْحَقِّ بِالْإِتلافِ مِنْ كُلِّ وَجُهِ فِي الْآدَمِيٌّ تَعْظِيمًا لَهُ. (تبيين الحقائق: كتاب الديات، فصل فيما دون النفس، ج ۶ ص ۱۲۹)
الهداية كتاب الديات، فصل فيما دون النفس، ج۴ ص ۵۹۰
قال ( ومن شج رجلا فالتحمت كلامه ظاهر ...... وقوله (إلا أن أبا حنيفة يقول:
إن المنافع إلخ) جواب عن قول أبي يوسف فالألم الحاصل ما زال، وعن قول محمد إنما لزمه أجر الطبيب ووجهه أن تحمل الألم من المنافع ومعالجة الطبيب كذلك، والمنافع على أصلنا لا تتقوم إلا بعقد كالإجارة الصحيحة والمضاربة الصحيحة، أو بشبهته كالإجارة الفاسدة والمضاربة الفاسدة، ولم يوجد شيء من ذلك في حق الجاني فلا يغرم شيئا. العناية كتاب الديات، ج ۱۰ ص ۲۹۶ ، ۲۹۷) و من شج رجلا فالتحمت ولم يبق لها أثر ونبت الشعر سقط الأرش عند أبى حنيفة رَحِمَهُ اللهُ لزوال الشين الموجب)..... إلا أن أبا حنيفة رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ يقول : إن المنافع على أصلنا لا تتقوم إلا بعقد أى مطلق الشبهة العقد كالإجارة الصحيحة والمضاربة الصحيحة أو بشبهة أى بشبهة العقد المطلق كالإجارة الفاسدة والمضاربة الفاسدة ولم يوجد فى حق الجاني) عقد ولا شبهة فلا يغرم شيئا كما لو لطمه وألهمه البناية كتاب الديات، ج ۱۳ ص ۲۱۱
الهداية كتاب الديات: ج۴ ص ۵۹۱
Origin of principles. (55) Benefits are not compensable except by a contract or by a semblance (of a contract). Translation: Benefits are not compensable except by a contract or by a semblance of a contract. 257 Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that all benefits are not compensable, i.e., no exchange can be taken in return for them except when a contract occurs, for example a valid lease or valid partnership etc., or a semblance of a contract, such as a void lease or void partnership etc., then these benefits become compensable, because benefits are attributes (insubstantial qualities) that is, they have no permanence, and whatever is of the category of attributes is not compensable. The author of al-Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if someone wounds another on the head, then the wound heals and its effect goes away, then hair grows again on his head, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the compensation is dropped, because the compensation for the wound or its expense is a benefit, and benefits are not relinquished without a contract or a semblance of a contract, and these two things (contract, semblance of contract) are not present for the injurer. Therefore, in the above case it is also not compensable, and since it is not compensable, no compensation comes in return, just as if someone slaps another, there is no compensation on him, but if the qadi at the time wishes to give some punishment as a reprimand, there is room for it. And the principle regarding body parts is that if one destroys a type of benefit completely or removes an intended beauty completely in a human, the full diyah is obligatory because in that there is destruction of the life from one aspect, since the life does not remain beneficial from that aspect, and destruction of life from one aspect is attached to destruction from every aspect in a human, to honor him. (Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: Kitab al-Diyat, Fasl fima duna al-Nafs, vol. 6 p. 129) Al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Diyat, Fasl fima duna al-Nafs, vol. 4 p. 590. He said: (And whoever wounds a man and it heals, his words are apparent... and his saying: Except that Abu Hanifa says: The saying 'benefits etc.' is a response to the statement of Abu Yusuf: 'the pain that occurred has ceased', and to the statement of Muhammad: 'only the doctor's fee is incumbent upon him', and its aspect is that bearing pain is from the benefits, and the doctor's treatment is likewise, and benefits according to our principle are not compensable except by a contract like a valid lease and valid partnership, or by its semblance like a void lease and void partnership, and nothing of that exists in the right of the injurer, so he does not pay anything. (Al-Inayah, Kitab al-Diyat, vol. 10 pp. 296-297) And whoever wounds a man and it heals and no trace remains and hair grows, the compensation drops according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) due to the removal of the defect that necessitates it. Except that Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) says: Benefits according to our principle are not compensable except by a contract i.e. absolute semblance of a contract like valid lease and valid partnership, or by a semblance i.e. by an absolute semblance of a contract like void lease and void partnership, and in the right of the injurer there is no contract or semblance, so he does not pay anything, just as if he slaps him and gives him pain. (Al-Binayah, Kitab al-Diyat, vol. 13 p. 211) Al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Diyat: vol. 4 p. 591.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational Hanafi legal maxim concerning the compensability of benefits (manafi'). The author begins by stating the principle: benefits are not considered property (mutaqawwim) except through a valid contract (e.g., valid lease or partnership) or a semblance of a contract (e.g., void lease or void partnership). The reasoning is that benefits are a'rad (accidents/attributes) which lack permanence and thus are not inherently property. The shuruh (commentary) then applies this maxim to a specific case from the law of diyat (blood money): if a person wounds another on the head, the wound heals, and hair grows back, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa, no compensation (arsh) is due because the benefit lost (the use of the head, beauty, etc.) is not compensable without a contract or its semblance. The author cites Tabyin al-Haqa'iq to clarify that when a benefit is completely destroyed or an intended beauty is removed, the full diyah may be obligatory as it is like destroying life in part. The main text then references al-Hidayah and continues with a response to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): Abu Yusuf argued based on the pain that occurred, and Muhammad argued for the doctor's fee; the Hanafi response is that pain and medical treatment are benefits, and without a contract or its semblance, they are not compensable. The passage ends by emphasizing that this ruling follows Abu Hanifa's principle and is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).

Key Terms
مَنَافِع (manāfiʿ): Benefits (usufruct) of a thing, which are not considered property (mutaqawwim) unless obtained through a valid contract or its semblance.
تَقَوُّم (taqawwum): The quality of being considered property (mal mutaqawwim) such that compensation can be taken for it.
شُبْهَة (shubhah): A semblance or doubt similar to a contract, such as a void contract (fāsid).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi maxim, why is no compensation (arsh) due for a wound that heals completely and hair grows back?
A) Because the wound was not intentional.
B) Because the benefit lost is not compensable without a contract or its semblance.
C) Because the diyah is only for death.
D) Because compensation requires a court order.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim that benefits are not compensable except by contract or its semblance is a foundational rule governing liability for lost usufruct in tort and contract law.

The Maxim: Confession Does Not Extend Beyond the Confessor
States a legal maxim and applies it to diyat liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim 'Al-Iqrar la yata'adda al-muqirr', explain its meaning as a limited proof (hujja qasira), and apply it to determine liability for diyat when a person confesses to a crime.

Core Idea
Confession binds only the confessor; it cannot impose liability on others.

In this lesson, we examine a fundamental Hanafi maxim from al-Hidayah: confession does not extend beyond the person making it. The commentator clarifies that confession is a limited proof (hujja qasira), meaning it establishes a right only against the confessor. This principle has a direct practical application in the law of diyat, where a confession to a crime shifts liability from the tribal 'aqila to the confessor's own property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its literal meaning
Read the Arabic maxim 'Al-Iqrar la yata'adda al-muqirr' and its Urdu translation. Note that the maxim states confession does not go beyond the confessor.
The maxim is a concise legal rule that controls the scope of admissions.
2
Understand confession as a limited proof
The commentary explains that iqrar is a hujja qasira (limited proof). This means it binds only the person who makes it and does not affect third parties.
Contrast with evidence like witnesses or circumstantial proof, which may bind others.
3
Apply to diyat liability
The passage applies the maxim: if a person confesses to a crime (jinayat), the compensation (diya) is taken from his own wealth, not from the 'aqila. The reason is that the obligation arose from confession, which is limited to the confessor.
This shows the practical effect: the 'aqila is only liable when the killing is proven by other means, not by the killer's own admission.

Origin of principles. (56) Confession does not extend beyond the confessor. Translation: Confession does not extend beyond the confessor. 258 Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that confession does not extend from the confessor's own person to others, i.e., by the confession, a right will be established on the confessor, but nothing will be incumbent and established on others by this confession; rather it will be limited to the confessor's own person, thus confession is a limited proof. The author of al-Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that every crime (jinayah) that the confessor confesses on his own, its compensation will be given from the confessor's property; the 'aqila (tribal group) will not bear it, because in the above case, the obligation of wealth occurred by confession, and confession is a limited proof, i.e., it is limited to the confessor; nothing is established on others by it. Therefore, in the above case
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage introduces the Hanafi legal maxim that a confession (iqrar) is binding only on the person who makes it and does not affect others. The author explains that iqrar is a 'limited proof' (hujja qasira), meaning it establishes a right against the confessor alone. The commentary then applies this maxim to the law of diyat: if a person confesses to a crime (e.g., killing or injury), the blood money (diya) or compensation (diya) must be paid from the confessor's own wealth, and the 'aqila (the paternal relatives who usually pay diyat for accidental killings) are not liable. This is because the obligation arose solely from the confession, and confession cannot transfer liability to third parties. The passage underscores the principle that a person's admission cannot impose a duty on anyone except himself.

Key Terms
إِقْرَار (iqrār): Confession or admission of a right against oneself. It is binding only on the confessor, not on others.
مُقِرّ (muqirr): The person who makes the confession (iqrār).
عَاقِلَة (ʿāqila): The tribal group or relatives who are responsible for paying diyat (blood money) on behalf of the killer. In the context of confession, they are not liable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Confession is a limited proof that binds only the confessor; it cannot impose liability on third parties, such as the 'aqila in diyat cases.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate

الإقرار لا يتعدى المقر: Acknowledgment Binds Only the Acknowledger
States a legal maxim and applies it to diyat cases, with a subsidiary ruling on head wounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'al-iqrar la yata'adda al-muqirr', explain its meaning as a hujjah qasirah, and apply it to determine that an offender's confession does not impose liability on the 'aqilah in diyat cases.

Core Idea
An acknowledgment is a limited proof: it binds only the one who makes it, not others.

This lesson unpacks a foundational Hanafi legal maxim from Uṣūl al-Hidāyah: الإقرار لا يتعدى المقر (Acknowledgment does not extend beyond the acknowledger). We will explore why an iqrār is considered a ḥujjah qāṣirah (confined proof) and see its direct consequence: if a perpetrator confesses to a crime requiring blood-money, the diyah is taken from his own property, not from his ʿāqilah. The lesson also touches on the related Hanafi view that usufructs (manāfiʿ) of the body are not compensable without a contract or its semblance.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the maxim as a ḥujjah qāṣirah
The maxim states that an acknowledgment (iqrār) does not transfer liability from the acknowledger to any other person. In Hanafi law, iqrār is a proof (ḥujjah) but it is 'confined' (qāṣirah) – it only establishes rights and obligations for the one who makes it.
Contrast with ḥujjah mutaʿaddiyah (extending proof) like testimony, which can bind others.
2
Apply the maxim to diyat
When a person confesses to a crime that requires diyah (e.g., unintentional homicide), the obligation arises from his own acknowledgment. Because the proof does not extend to third parties, the ʿāqilah (paternal relatives) are not liable to pay; the offender must pay from his own wealth.
This is an exception to the general rule that the ʿāqilah pays diyah for unintentional killing when the perpetrator does not confess.
3
Trace the subsidiary ruling on head wounds
The passage also addresses a scenario: if a head wound heals completely leaving no trace and hair grows back, Abū Ḥanīfah rules that no indemnity (arsh) is due. This is because the benefit of healing and pain relief are considered manāfiʿ, which are not compensable without a contract or its semblance.
The Sahibayn (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad) held that some compensation is due for the pain and medical expenses, but Abū Ḥanīfah’s view is relied upon in the school.
4
Understand the uṣūlī basis
The ruling on the head wound is derived by qiyās: since benefits (manāfiʿ) are only valuable (mutaqawwim) when they are the subject of a valid or void contract (like ijārah or muḍārabah), and no such contract exists between the perpetrator and victim, the perpetrator owes nothing.
This principle is stated explicitly in the commentary from al-ʿInāyah and al-Bināyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ترجمہ: اقرار مقر سے متعدی نہیں ہوتا۔
