بھی مقر کے اقرار سے عاقلہ پر کوئی چیز ثابت نہ ہوگی۔
(56) "الإقرار لا يتعدى المقر" (An acknowledgment does not extend beyond the acknowledger). Translation: Acknowledgment does not transfer from the acknowledger. Commentary: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that acknowledgment does not pass from the acknowledger's own person to others, i.e., acknowledgment establishes a right for the acknowledger against the acknowledger, but nothing is incumbent or established upon others due to this acknowledgment; rather, it is confined to the acknowledger's own person, as if acknowledgment is a limited proof. The author of al-Hidayah mentions this principle and informs that every offense the perpetrator acknowledges himself, its compensation will be paid from his own wealth, and the ʿāqilah will not bear it, because in the above case, the obligation of wealth arose from acknowledgment, and acknowledgment is a limited proof, i.e., it is confined to the acknowledger and nothing is established for others from it. Therefore, in the above case, (the phrase "إن المنافع إلخ" [Indeed, benefits etc.] is a response to the statement of Abu Yusuf: "the pain that occurred has not ceased," and to the statement of Muhammad: "only the doctor's fee is obligatory upon him," and the reasoning is that enduring pain is among the benefits and medical treatment is likewise. According to our school, benefits are not valuable except by contract like a valid lease and valid partnership, or by its semblance like a void lease and void partnership, and nothing of that exists regarding the perpetrator, so he is not liable for anything. (al-ʿInāyah, Kitāb al-Diyāt, vol. 10, pp. 296-297) And whoever wounds a person's head and it heals with no trace left and hair grows, the indemnity is dropped according to Abu Hanīfa (may Allah have mercy on him) due to the removal of the defect that necessitates (compensation). ... Except that Abu Hanīfa (may Allah have mercy on him) says: Indeed, benefits according to our school are not valuables except by a contract, i.e., by absolute semblance of a contract like a valid lease and valid partnership, or by a semblance [of a contract] i.e., by a semblance of an absolute contract like a void lease and void partnership, and there is no contract or semblance of a contract in the case of the perpetrator, so he is not liable for anything, just as if he slapped him and caused him pain. (al-Bināyah, Kitāb al-Diyāt, vol. 13, p. 211) (al-Hidāyah, Kitāb al-Diyāt, vol. 4, p. 591) Also, nothing is established upon the ʿāqilah due to the acknowledger's acknowledgment.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents the legal maxim "الإقرار لا يتعدى المقر" (An acknowledgment does not extend beyond the acknowledger) from Uṣūl al-Hidāyah. The author explains that an acknowledgment (iqrār) is a 'limited proof' (ḥujjah qāṣirah); it only affects the person who makes it and does not impose liability on others. The main application discussed is in the law of diyāt (blood‑money). If a person confesses to committing a crime that requires compensation, the payment comes from his own property, and his ʿāqilah (responsible tribe) are not liable. The author then digresses into a related fiqhi issue: if a wounded head heals completely without leaving a trace, according to Abū Ḥanīfah no indemnity (arsh) is due, because the defect that necessitated compensation has vanished. This ruling is based on the Hanafī principle that the usufructs (manāfiʿ) of the body—such as bearing pain or undergoing medical treatment—are not compensable unless they are subject to a contract (like a valid or void lease or partnership) or its semblance. Since the perpetrator never entered into any contract with the victim, he owes nothing. The quotation from al-ʿInāyah and al-Bināyah supports this position. The passage concludes that the acknowledgment itself also does not make the ʿāqilah liable.

Key Terms
الإقرار (al-iqrār): A statement by which a person acknowledges a right or liability against himself.
حجة قاصرة (ḥujjah qāṣirah): A proof that is confined in its effect; in this context, an acknowledgment does not impose liability on third parties like the ʿāqilah.
عاقلة (ʿāqilah): The paternal male relatives (usually based on tribal affiliation) who share in paying blood‑money (diyah) for unintentional homicide or injury.
منافع (manāfiʿ): Usufructs or benefits derived from property or from one's own body, such as bearing pain or receiving medical treatment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, why does a perpetrator owe no compensation for a head wound that heals completely?
A) Because the wound left no trace, so no arsh is due by consensus.
B) Because the benefit of healing (manfaʿah) is not compensable without a contract.
C) Because the victim forgave the perpetrator.
D) Because the ʿāqilah already paid the diyah.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'الإقرار لا يتعدى المقر' means that an acknowledgment is a confined proof; in diyat, the perpetrator's confession obligates only himself to pay, not his ʿāqilah.

The Maxim: Compensation Is Not Obligatory on the Basis of Doubt
States a legal maxim and its application to fetal diyat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal maxim 'الضمان لا يجب بالشك', identify its underlying principle, and apply it to the case of fetal diyat when the cause of death is uncertain.

Core Idea
Compensation is not imposed by doubt; certainty of non‑liability prevails.

This lesson introduces a key Hanafi legal maxim: الضمان لا يجب بالشك — compensation is not obligatory on the basis of doubt. The maxim is rooted in the fundamental uṣūl principle that certainty is not removed by doubt. We will examine its meaning and see how it is applied in the case of a pregnant woman who is assaulted and delivers a stillborn child, determining the perpetrator's liability for the fetus' blood‑money.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its core meaning
The maxim 'الضمان لا يجب بالشك' means that financial liability cannot be established based on mere doubt. This is a direct application of the more general principle that certainty is not removed by doubt.
The maxim appears in the chapter on diyat for the fetus.
2
Trace the underlying uṣūl principle
The foundational principle is 'اليقين لا يزول بالشك' (certainty is not removed by doubt). Here, the original state is the mother’s non‑liability for the fetus’s death until proven otherwise. Doubt cannot overturn this certainty.
This principle is universally accepted in Hanafi uṣūl.
3
State the factual scenario
A pregnant woman is kicked in the belly and dies. She then gives birth to a dead fetus. The question is whether the perpetrator is liable for the fetus's diyah in addition to the mother's.
The mother's death is directly caused by the assault, so her diyah is certain.
4
Apply the maxim to the scenario
Because there is doubt as to whether the fetus died from the assault or from natural causes, the maxim applies: compensation (fetal diyah) is not obligatory. The perpetrator is only liable for the mother's diyah, which is certain.
If the fetus had been born alive and then died from the injury, liability would be certain and the maxim would not apply.
5
Recognize the practical consequence
The maxim protects individuals from liability based on speculation. It ensures that liability is only imposed when the causal link is legally certain.
This principle is consistent with the Hanafi emphasis on certainty in criminal liability.

(۵۷) اَلضَّمَانُ لَا يَجِبُ بِالشَّكِّ.
ترجمہ: ضمان شک سے واجب نہیں ہوتا۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ شک کی بناء پر ضمان واجب نہ ہوگا یعنی اگر سبب ضمان میں شک ہے تو ضمان واجب نہ ہوگا، کیونکہ عدم ضمان یقینی تھا جبکہ ضمان کا صرف شک ہے اور مسلم اصول ہے کہ الْيَقِينُ لَا يَزُولُ بِالشَّيِّ “ کہ یقین شک سے زائل نہیں ہوتا۔

Chapter on the Fetus. (57) "الضمان لا يجب بالشك" (Compensation is not obligatory on the basis of doubt). Translation: Compensation is not obligatory due to doubt. Commentary: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that compensation will not be obligatory on the basis of doubt, i.e., if there is doubt in the cause of liability, compensation will not be obligatory, because non-liability was certain while liability is only doubtful, and the established principle is "Certainty is not removed by doubt." The author of al-Hidayah mentions this principle and informs that if a person kicks a pregnant woman in the belly and she dies, then she gives birth to a dead fetus, only the mother's blood-money is obligatory on the perpetrator, not the fetus's diyah, because there is doubt whether the fetus died due to the blow or naturally.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents the legal maxim "الضمان لا يجب بالشك" (Compensation is not obligatory on the basis of doubt) from Uṣūl al-Hidāyah. The maxim is grounded in the fundamental principle "اليقين لا يزول بالشك" (Certainty is not removed by doubt). The application given is the case of a pregnant woman who is kicked and dies, then delivers a stillborn child. The perpetrator is liable for the mother's full blood-money (diyah) but not for the fetus, because there is doubt as to whether the fetus died from the assault or from natural causes. Since the original state is no liability (insured by certainty of no harm), doubt cannot create liability. This is a standard Hanafī ruling in the chapter on diyat for the fetus (faṣl fī al-janīn).

Key Terms
الضمان (al-ḍamān): Compensation or liability for loss or injury; in this context, financial liability for blood‑money (diyah).
الشك (al-shakk): Doubt regarding the existence of a cause of liability; it is the opposite of certainty (yaqīn).
الجنين (al-janīn): The fetus in the womb, especially with respect to diyat (blood‑money) when harm causes its death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Full diyah for the mother and half diyah for the fetus
B) Only the mother's diyah, not the fetus's
C) Only the fetus's diyah
D) No liability because of doubt

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'compensation is not obligatory on the basis of doubt' protects individuals from liability when the cause of harm is uncertain, as exemplified in fetal diyat.

The Principle of Doubt Preventing Liability in Diyat
Ruling that doubt prevents liability with example of fetal death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle 'al-yaqin la yazulu bi al-shakk' to cases of uncertain causation in diyat and state the ruling for fetal death.

Core Idea
Doubt about causation negates liability; certainty of non-liability is not overridden by doubt.

This lesson examines a foundational Hanafi principle in diyat: that financial liability cannot be imposed when the cause is uncertain. Using the example of a pregnant woman who dies after a blow, the Hidayah demonstrates how the rule 'certainty is not removed by doubt' operates to limit blood money to the mother only.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Principle
The text begins with the rule 'al-daman la yajib bi al-shakk' – compensation is not obligatory due to doubt. This is based on the legal maxim 'al-yaqin la yazulu bi al-shakk' (certainty is not removed by doubt). Recognize that this is a general principle applicable to all cases where liability is uncertain.
This principle is a qaw'idah in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Understand the Scenario
A man kicks a pregnant woman in the abdomen. She dies and then delivers a stillborn child. The question: is the assailant liable for the diyah (blood money) of the fetus separately from the mother?
The example is from the chapter on fetal injuries (fasi fil janin).
3
Apply the Principle of Yaqin
Before the blow, there was certainty that the assailant had no liability for the child's life. The cause of the fetal death is doubtful: it could be from the kick or from the mother's death (which would suffocate the fetus). The doubt does not remove the original certainty of non-liability.
The principle is rational (dalil aqli) and universally applied in Hanafi law.
4
Derive the Ruling
Since causation is doubtful, no diyah is owed for the fetus. The assailant is liable only for the mother's diyah if the blow directly caused her death. The text also notes that if the assailant confesses to an injury, the payment is from his own wealth, not from his 'aqilah (tribal guarantors).
This ruling is consistent with the broader Hanafi rule on doubt in criminal liability.

This passage is a ruling with proof from the chapter on fetal injuries. It states the principle that compensation (daman) is not obligatory when there is doubt about causation. No Liability for Fetus When Mother Dies Due to Doubt in Causation
Ruling and proof for no liability on fetus due to doubt in causation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fetal liability when the mother dies after being struck and explain the principle that doubt repels financial liability.

Core Idea
Doubt about the cause of a fetus's death cancels liability — certainty is required for financial obligations.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a specific case in the law of diyat: a pregnant woman is struck, dies, and then delivers a stillborn. The question is whether the assailant must pay diyah for the fetus as well as the mother. The Hanafi school answers with a clear principle: where causation is uncertain, no liability arises. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the role of shakk (doubt) in repelling financial obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
The scenario is a pregnant woman who is kicked, dies, and then delivers a stillborn. The ruling from Al-Quduri and Al-Hidayah is that the assailant pays diyah for the mother but nothing for the fetus.
2
Recognize the reasoning (dalil)
The Hanafi proof is based on qiyas: the mother's death is one possible cause of fetal death (suffocation), and the beating is another. This creates doubt about causation, and according to the maxim 'al-shakku yudfa`u bihi al-daman' (doubt repels liability), no obligation arises for the fetus.
The text explicitly states: 'the death of the mother is one cause... and the beating another... thus uncertainty arises.'
3
Understand the legal maxim applied
The underlying principle is that certainty is required for imposing financial liability. Since two equally plausible causes exist, the precise cause of the fetal death is unknown, so liability is dropped.
This is consistent with the broader Hanafi usul that shakk prevents imposition of hudud and financial obligations.
4
Compare with other schools (optional)
Other madhahib may hold the assailant liable for the fetus if the beating is the apparent cause, but the Hanafi school requires certainty. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that in identical cases, a Hanafi mufti will only award diyah for the mother, while a Shafi'i mufti might award for both.

Source Text

Thus uncertainty repels liability. This is a clear application of the principle that doubt prevents imposition of penalties and financial liability.

Liability for Acts of Piety in Mosques: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on diyat for accidental harm from acts of piety

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle regarding liability for acts of piety and distinguish the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn in the specific case of a stranger's act in a mosque.

Core Idea
Acts of piety are generally permitted; liability for accidental harm is not incurred even by strangers, according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

This lesson examines a dispute within the Hanafi school about whether someone who performs an act of piety (qurbat) in a mosque is liable if that act accidentally causes death or injury. The key principle is that everyone is permitted to perform such acts, but does that permission extend to non-locals? Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ on this point. Understanding this dispute clarifies the scope of permission and the conditions for liability in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
State the General Principle
Identify the core principle from the Hidayah: 'Every person is permitted to perform acts of piety (kullu ahadin ma'dhunun fi iqamat al-qurbat).' This permission is unconditional and not restricted by safety concerns.
2
Identify the Scenario
Consider the specific case: a person places a mat or hangs a lantern in a mosque, and someone slips and dies. The dispute focuses on whether the doer is liable for diyat (blood money).
3
State Imam Abu Hanifa's Position and Rationale
Imam Abu Hanifa distinguishes between a local resident and a stranger. If the doer is from the locality, he is not liable (by consensus). If the doer is a stranger, he is liable because his act lacks authorization from the mosque's regular users.
Imam Abu Hanifa's view emphasizes the special status of locals in their mosque.
4
State the Sahibayn's Position and Rationale
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the doer is not liable in either case. Their reasoning: the act is an act of piety, and the general permission for all people applies. This permission is not tied to safety or locality; it is akin to having explicit permission from the mosque authorities.
The Sahibayn extend the principle of general permission to its logical conclusion.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi and Practical Impact
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the Sahibayn: no liability in both cases. This means that anyone performing a commendable act in a mosque is not liable for unintended accidental harm, regardless of whether they are a local or a stranger.
This ruling reflects a lenient approach to encourage acts of piety.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يحدثه الرجل في الطريق
(۵۸) كُلُّ أَحَدٍ مَأْذُونٌ فِي إِقَامَةِ الْقُرُبَاتِ.
ترجمہ: قربات قائم کرنے میں ہر ایک کو اجازت ہے۔
تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جتنے بھی بھلائی اور ثواب کے کام ہیں ہر آدمی ان کو سر انجام دے سکتا ہے اور ہر آدمی کو ثواب کے کام کرنے کی اجازت حاصل ہے، لہذا اگر ا قامت قربات، مثلاً تزیین مساجد وغیرہ کسی کی ہلاکت کا سبب بنیں تو اس کام کا فاعل ضامین نہ ہوگا۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلار ہے ہیں کہ اگر کسی مخصوص محلے والوں کی مسجد میں اہل محلہ میں سے کسی نے اس مسجد میں فانوس انکا یا یا اس مسجد میں چٹائی بچھائی اور اس پر پھسل کر کوئی دوسرا آدمی ہلاک ہو گیا تو چٹائی بچھانے والا بالا تفاق ضامن نہ ہوگا اور اگر اہلِ محلہ کے علاوہ کسی دوسرے آدمی نے چٹائی بچھائی اور پھر کوئی ہلاک ہو گیا تو امام صاحب کے نزدیک وہ ضامن ہو گا، جبکہ صاحبین کے نزدیک دونوں صورتوں میں ضامن نہ ہوگا، کیونکہ مسجد میں قندیل لٹکانا یا چٹائی بچھانا قربت و ثواب کے کام ہیں اور اقامت قربات میں ہر ایک کو اجازت ہوتی وہ سلامتی کی شرط کے ساتھ مقید نہیں ہوتی ، اور جس کام میں ہر ایک کو اجازت ہو اس کے کرنے سے آدمی ضامن نہیں ہوتا ، لہذا مسجد میں قندیل لٹکانے والا اور چٹائی بچھانے والا بھی ضامن نہ ہو گا چاہے اہلِ محلہ میں سے ہو یا غیر اہلِ محلہ میں سے ہو، یہ ایسا ہے جیسے اگر اہل مسجد کی اجازت سے وہ یہ کام کرتا اور پھر کوئی مر جاتا تو اس پر ضمان نہ ہوتا ، اسی طرح صورت مذکورہ میں بھی اس پر ضمان نہ ہوگا۔
Chapter: What a man initiates in the road (58) Everyone is permitted to perform acts of piety. Translation: Everyone is allowed to establish acts of piety. Explanation: The meaning of the above principle is that whatever good and reward-worthy deeds exist, every person can perform them, and every person has permission to do reward-worthy deeds. Therefore, if establishing acts of piety, such as beautifying mosques, etc., cause someone's death, the doer of that deed will not be liable. The author of Hidayah mentions the above principle to explain that if in a mosque belonging to a specific locality, someone from the locality hung a lantern or laid a mat, and someone else slipped and died, then the one who laid the mat is by consensus not liable. But if a person other than the locality's residents laid the mat and someone died, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa he would be liable, while according to the Sahibayn he would not be liable in both cases. Because hanging a lantern or laying a mat in a mosque are acts of piety and reward, and in establishing acts of piety everyone has permission, and it is not restricted by the condition of safety. And a deed in which everyone is permitted, a person is not liable for doing it. Therefore, the one who hangs a lantern or lays a mat in a mosque will not be liable, whether he is from the locality or not. This is similar to if he did it with the permission of the mosque's congregation and someone died, he would not be liable. Likewise, in the mentioned case, he will not be liable.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage discusses a dispute within the Hanafi school regarding liability for harm caused by acts of piety in a mosque. The principle (58) states that everyone is permitted to perform acts of piety (qurbat). The commentary explains that such acts, like beautifying mosques, are permissible for all, and this permission is not conditional upon safety. Therefore, if someone dies due to these acts, the doer is generally not liable. However, there is a disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) in a specific scenario: if a person from outside the locality places a mat in a mosque and someone dies, Imam Abu Hanifa holds him liable because he is not a resident of the locality and thus his act might be seen as unauthorized. The Sahibayn argue that the act itself is an act of piety, and permission is general for everyone, so no liability. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the Sahibayn, as indicated by the text's emphasis on the general permission. This demonstrates that in Hanafi fiqh, permission for commendable acts overrides presumptions of liability.

Key Terms
قربات (qurbat): Acts of piety or deeds that bring one closer to Allah, such as beautifying mosques.
اذن (idhn): Permission; here, the general permission for everyone to perform qurbat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, if a person from outside the locality lays a mat in a mosque and someone dies slipping on it, what is the ruling?
A) He is liable because he is a stranger
B) He is not liable because the act is piety and everyone is permitted
C) He is liable only if he did not have permission
D) He is not liable only if he is from the locality

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, acts of piety in mosques are generally not subject to liability for accidental harm, following the Sahibayn's interpretation of general permission.

Road Use and Acts of Piety: The Condition of Safety in Hanafi Law
Principle on road use and a related intra-Hanafi dispute on mosque decorations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that passing in Muslim roads is mubah conditional on safety, and explain why this condition does not apply to acts of piety in a mosque, according to the relied-upon view of the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Road use is mubah but conditional on safety; mosque acts of piety are unrestricted because they are generally permitted.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi principle: that passing in Muslim roads is permissible only when safety is observed. It then explores an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether the same condition applies to acts of piety like placing a lantern in a mosque. The discussion reveals a deeper legal distinction between ordinary permissibility and religiously encouraged acts.

Guided Path
1
State the principle on road use
Identify the ruling for passing in Muslim roads as stated in Al-Hidayah: it is mubah but muqayyad bi shart al-salamah. This means the permissibility is not absolute; causing harm without precaution may incur liability.
2
Identify the mosque scenario from Al-Jami' Al-Saghir
The text narrates a case: if a mosque belongs to a clan, and a person from that clan hangs a lantern or places a mat or pebble, and someone is injured, the person is not liable. But if the person is from outside the clan, he is liable.
3
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa differentiates based on membership: an insider has implicit authorization, an outsider does not, so the outsider is liable.
4
State the Sahibayn's position
The Sahibayn say no liability in either case because these are acts of piety, and everyone is permitted to perform them without the condition of safety.
5
Determine the relied-upon view
The text indicates the Sahibayn's view is the mufta bihi. The principle of safety applies to ordinary road use but not to acts of piety, where general permission prevails.

Chapter: Injury by animals and injury upon them (59) Passing in the road of Muslims is permissible, restricted by the condition of safety. (He said, i.e., Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, in Al-Jami' Al-Saghir: (If the mosque belongs to a clan) 'al-ashira' means tribe, mentioned in Al-Sihah, and here it means the people of the mosque (and a man from them i.e., from the clan, hung a lantern in it) i.e., in the mosque, or placed mats - plural of 'bariya' - ... or placed a pebble - i.e., placed a pebble - and a man was injured (by it i.e., by one of these things, he is not liable...) Commentary
This passage presents a principle (59) concerning the use of Muslim roads: it is permissible but conditional upon safety. Then it quotes a narration from Imam Muhammad in Al-Jami' Al-Saghir regarding a mosque scenario that parallels the previous lesson. Interestingly, the chapter is titled 'Injury by animals and injury upon them' but the content again deals with the mosque lantern case. This suggests that the same ruling applies: if a person places an object (lantern, mat, pebble) in a mosque and someone is harmed, liability depends on whether he is from the clan (i.e., authorized). The dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn is reiterated: Abu Hanifa differentiates between a person from the clan (not liable) and a stranger (liable), while the Sahibayn hold no liability in both cases because acts of piety are generally permitted. The relied-upon position is that of the Sahibayn. The principle of road use (murur) is compared: it is mubah but muqayyad bi shart al-salamah (conditional on safety), meaning that if one causes harm while using the road without observing safety, liability may arise. However, for acts of piety, the condition of safety is not applied strictly.

Key Terms
مرور (murur): Passing or walking; here specifically in the roads of Muslims.
مقيد بشرط السلامة (muqayyad bi shart al-salamah): Restricted by the condition of safety; meaning the permission is not absolute but tied to not causing harm.

Liability for Placing Objects in a Mosque: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for mosque objects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on liability for placing a lamp or mat in a mosque, explain the Sahibayn's proof based on general permission for acts of piety, and distinguish the practical consequence of this dispute.

Core Idea
Acts of piety in a mosque carry general permission, so no liability for harm—unless you're an outsider according to Abu Hanifa.

Imagine a stranger hangs a lamp in a mosque for lighting, it falls and kills a worshipper. Is he liable? Imam Abu Hanifa says yes; his two foremost students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, say no. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and why this difference matters.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and Abu Hanifa's position
Identify the act: hanging a lamp, laying a mat, or placing a pebble in a mosque. According to Abu Hanifa, if the doer is from the locality (ahl al-mahallah), he is not liable; if from outside, he is liable.
The distinction hinges on the doer's relationship to the mosque.
2
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that in both cases (resident or outsider) the person is not liable. They reject the distinction.
Their view is recorded in al-Jami' al-Saghir and is from Zahir al-Riwayah.
3
Understand the Sahibayn's proof
Their reasoning: hanging a lamp or laying a mat are acts of piety (qurbat). Such acts are permissible for everyone; implicit permission exists for all Muslims. Therefore, no condition of safety attaches, and no liability arises from harm caused.
This is a rational (aqli) argument based on the nature of acts of worship.
4
Identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
If an outsider causes harm, Abu Hanifa says he must pay compensation; the Sahibayn say he does not. This affects who is financially responsible for injuries or deaths caused by such objects.
The dispute remains unresolved in the text; both positions are transmitted.
5
Note the authority weight
The Sahibayn's view is from Zahir al-Riwayah (core reliable texts). Abu Hanifa's distinction is also transmitted but not given explicit tarjih here. Later fatwa practice may favor one, but this text presents both without resolution.
Students should recognize that not all disputes have a single mufta bihi ruling in the classical sources.

Source Text

(وإن كان الذى فعل ذلك من غير العشيرة ضمن (قالوا) أى المشايخ (هذا) يعنى هذا التفصيل عند أبي حنيفة رَحِمَهُ اللهُ وقالا: لا يضمن في الوجهين ..... لأن هذه أى المذكور من الأشياء من القرب. وكل أحد) من أهل المسجد (مأذون في إقامتها أى بإقامة هذه الأشياء فلا يتقيد بشرط السلامة كما إذا فعله بإذن واحد من أهل المسجد بإذن واحد من أهل المسجد حيث لا يضمن. :البناية: كتاب الديات، باب ما يحدثه الرجل في الطريق، ج ۱۳ ص ۲۴۰، ۲۴۱)
If a person other than the residents of the locality laid a mat and then someone died, according to Imam Abu Hanifa he will be liable, while according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) he will not be liable in either case, because hanging a lamp or laying a mat in the mosque are acts of piety and devotion, and in establishing acts of piety everyone has permission and it is not restricted by the condition of safety. And for an act for which everyone has permission, a person is not liable. Therefore, the one who hangs a lamp or lays a mat in the mosque will not be liable, whether he is from the residents of the locality or not. This is like if he did this with the permission of the people of the mosque and then someone died, he would not be liable. Similarly, in the mentioned case, he will not be liable. (He said, i.e., Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, in al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'When the mosque belongs to a clan...') the clan: the tribe, mentioned in al-Sihah, and here it means the people of the mosque. ('Then a man from them, i.e., from the clan, hung a lamp in it, i.e., in the mosque, or placed mats, plural of buriya... or pebbles, i.e., placed a pebble, and a man was injured or killed by it, i.e., by any of these things, he is not liable. And if the one who did that was from outside the clan, he is liable.') They (i.e., the mashayikh) said: This means this detail (of distinction) is according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He is not liable in both cases... because these mentioned things are among acts of piety. And everyone (from the people of the mosque) is permitted to establish them, i.e., to do these things, so it is not restricted to the condition of safety, just as if he did it with the permission of one of the mosque's people, he would not be liable. (Source: al-Binayah, Kitab al-Diyat, Bab ma yuhdithuhu al-rajul fi al-tariq, vol. 13, pp. 240-241)
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning liability (daman) when someone places an object in a mosque—such as hanging a lamp, laying a mat, or placing pebbles—and that object causes harm to a person. The dispute is between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). Imam Abu Hanifa distinguishes between a resident of the locality (the 'clan' or 'ahl al-mahallah') and an outsider: if a resident does it, he is not liable; if an outsider does it, he is liable. The Sahibayn, however, hold that in both cases the person is not liable. Their reasoning is that such acts are acts of piety (qurbat) for which general permission exists; everyone is allowed to perform them without being conditioned on safety. Therefore, no liability attaches to the doer, regardless of his relationship to the mosque. The text then quotes al-Jami' al-Saghir of Imam Muhammad to support the Sahibayn's view, but notes that the distinction (tafsil) is Abu Hanifa's. The commentary explains that just as explicit permission from the mosque's people removes liability, so does the implicit general permission for acts of piety. The ruling is based on the principle that acts of public benefit are not restricted by safety conditions. The passage does not explicitly state which view is relied upon (mufta bihi), so both positions are presented.

Key Terms
قندیل (qindeel): A lamp or lantern hung in a mosque for lighting.
بوارى (bawaari): Mats (plural of buriya) laid on the mosque floor.
حصاة (hasaat): Small pebbles or stones placed in the mosque, perhaps for a purpose.
ضمان (daman): Liability; financial responsibility for harm caused.
قربات (qurbat): Acts of piety or devotion that draw one closer to Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), when is a person who hangs a lamp in a mosque liable if it causes harm?
A) He is liable in all cases.
B) He is liable only if he is from outside the locality.
C) He is not liable in any case.
D) He is liable only if he did not have permission.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, liability for placing objects in a mosque depends on whether the act is a qurbah (act of piety) and, for Abu Hanifa, on the doer's relationship to the mosque.

The Principle of Passing on Muslim Roads and Rider's Liability
Commentary on the legal principle that passing on a Muslim road is permissible conditional on safety, applied to rider liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the principle that passing on a Muslim road is mubah conditional on safety, and apply it to determine whether a rider is liable for harm caused by his animal.

Core Idea
Passing on public roads is permissible only if it causes no harm.

This lesson introduces a foundational Hanafi principle about shared public spaces: the road belongs to everyone, so the right to use it is balanced by the duty not to harm others. We will see how this principle governs the liability of a rider when his animal causes injury. The rule is rational, derived from the nature of shared rights, not from a specific text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Default Ruling
Recognize that passing (murur) on a Muslim road is the act under discussion. Its default ruling is mubah (permissible) because everyone needs access to roads.
This is a general permissibility, not unrestricted.
2
Identify the Condition That Restricts the Permissibility
The permissibility is restricted by the condition of safety (shart al-salamah). Harm must be avoided. If the condition is violated, liability arises.
The condition balances the rights of the individual and others.
3
Understand the Rationale: Balancing Rights
The road is shared (mushtarak). The user acts in his own right (to pass) and in others' rights (to safety). Absolute permissibility would harm others; absolute restriction would harm the user. So a middle path: permissibility with a safety condition.
This is a rational (aqli) derivation.
4
Apply to Rider Liability
If a rider's animal causes harm by kicking (foreleg/hind leg), trampling, or butting with the head, the rider is liable (damin). The condition of safety is breached.
Not all animal actions trigger liability; only those directly involving the rider's control.
5
State the Consequence
The consequence is that the rider must compensate for the damage. The act of passing was mubah but the violation of the condition makes him responsible.
Liability is financial (diyat).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اصول ہدایہ رائج پر
ترجمہ: مسلمانوں کے راستے میں گزرنا مباح ہے لیکن سلامتی کی شرط کے ساتھ مقید ہے۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ مسلمانوں کے عام راستہ سے ہر آدمی گزر سکتا ہے، بشرطیکہ سلامتی کے ساتھ گزرے کسی کو نقصان نہ پہنچائے ، اور اگر سلامتی کی شرط فوت ہو جائے اور کسی کو نقصان پہنچائے تو اس صورت میں گزرنے والا ضامن ہوگا۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ تمہید رکھی ہے کہ اگر سواری نے کسی کو لات مار کر یا روند کر یا سر سے ٹکر مار کر نقصان پہنچایا تو سوار ضامن ہوگا، کیونکہ مسلمانوں کے عام راستے میں گزرنا مباح تو ہے لیکن عدم نقصان کی شرط کے ساتھ اور اس تمہید کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ گزرنے والا من وجہ اپنے حق میں تصرف کرتا ہے کہ اسے بھی اس راستے میں گزرنے کا حق حاصل ہے اور من وجہ وہ دوسروں کے حق میں تصرف کرتا ہے کیونکہ دوسروں کو بھی اس راستہ میں گزرنے کا حق ہے، اس لیے دونوں کی رعایت کرتے ہوئے کہا گیا کہ گزرنا مباح تو ہے لیکن سلامتی کی شرط کے ساتھ مقید بھی ہے، لہذا نقصان کی صورت میں ضامن ہوگا اور مذکورہ بالا صورت میں جب سواری نے نقصان پہنچایا تو راکب ضامن ہوگا۔
والأصل أن المرور في طريق المسلمين مباح مقيد بشرط السلامة لأنه يتصرف في حقه من وجه وفى حق غيره من وجه لكونه مشتركا بين كل الناس) أما أنه يتصرف في حقه فلأن الإنسان لا بد له من طريق يمشى فيه لترتيب مهماته، فالحجر عن ذلك حرح وهو مدفوع وأما أنه يتصرف في حق غيره فلأن غيره فيه كهو في الاحتياج، فبالنظر إلى حقه يستدعى الإباحة مطلقا، وبالنظر إلى حق غيره يستدعى الحجر مطلقا، فقلنا بإباحة مقيدة بشرط السلامة عملا بالوجهين وبقية كلامه واضحة. (العناية كتاب الديات، باب جناية البهيمة والجناية عليها، ج ۱۰ ص ۳۲۶
(الراكب صامن لما أوطات الدابة ما أصابت بيدها أو رجلها أو رأسها أو كدمت أو
Principles of Hidayah, established.
Translation: Passing in the way of the Muslims is permissible but restricted with the condition of safety. 
Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that every person can pass through the general road of the Muslims, provided that he passes with safety and does not cause harm to anyone. And if the condition of safety is forfeited and he causes harm to someone, then in that case the passer will be liable. The author of Hidayah mentioned this principle and then introduced that if a riding animal caused harm by kicking, trampling, or butting with its head, the rider is liable, because passing in the general road of the Muslims is permissible but with the condition of no harm. The reason for this introduction is that the passer, in one aspect, acts in his own right, because he also has the right to pass on that road; and in another aspect, he acts in the right of others, because others also have the right to pass on that road. Therefore, considering both aspects, it was said that passing is permissible but also restricted with the condition of safety. Hence, in case of harm, he will be liable. In the above-mentioned case, when the riding animal caused harm, the rider is liable. 
And the principle is that passing in the way of the Muslims is permissible restricted with the condition of safety, because he acts in his own right from one aspect and in the right of others from another aspect, since it is shared among all people. As for acting in his own right, because a person inevitably needs a road to walk on to arrange his necessities, so preventing him is harmful and is repelled. And as for acting in the right of others, because others are like him in need. So considering his own right, absolute permissibility is required, and considering the right of others, absolute restriction is required. So we said: permissibility restricted with the condition of safety, acting upon both aspects. The rest of his words are clear. (Al-Inayah, Kitab al-Diyat, Bab jana'at al-bahima wa al-janayah 'alayha, vol. 10, p. 326) 
(The rider is liable for what the riding animal tramples, whether it strikes with its foreleg, hind leg, head, or gores or...
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage explains a fundamental principle of Hanafi fiqh regarding shared public spaces: passing through a public road (tariq al-muslimeen) is permissible (mubah) but is conditioned upon safety (shart al-salamah). The principle balances the right of the individual to use the road with the right of others to be safe from harm. The commentary from Al-Inayah elaborates that the road is a shared resource (mushtarak bayn al-nas), so each person's right to pass is absolute in itself but must yield to the equal right of others. Therefore, the permissibility is not unrestricted; it is qualified. If a person (or his animal) causes harm while using the road, the passer becomes liable (damin) for the damage. The specific application discussed is the liability of a rider (rakib) when his riding animal causes injury by kicking, trampling, or striking with its head. The rider is liable because he was using the road under the condition of safety, and that condition was violated. The principle is derived not from a specific textual proof (naqli) but from rational inference (aqli) based on the nature of shared rights. This passage serves as a foundational rule for cases of tort and liability in public thoroughfares.

Key Terms
المرور (al-murur): The act of passing or traversing a public road.
مباح مقيد بشرط السلامة (mubah muqayyad bi shart al-salamah): Permissible but restricted by the condition of safety; the action is allowed only if it does not cause harm.
الراكب (al-rakib): The rider of an animal (horse, camel, etc.) who is held liable for harm caused by the animal.
ضامن (damin): Liable for compensation; responsible to pay damages for harm caused.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle mentioned, when is a rider liable for harm caused by his animal on a Muslim road?
A) The rider is never liable because the road is public.
B) The rider is always liable regardless of the animal's actions.
C) The rider is liable if the animal causes harm by kicking, trampling, or head-butting.
D) The rider is liable only if he intentionally caused the animal to act.

Key Takeaway
The rider is liable for harm caused by his animal's actions on a public road because the permissibility of passing is conditioned on safety.

Passing Through a Public Road: Permissible with Condition of Safety
Principle of passing through a public road conditional on safety

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that passing through a public road is mubah conditional on safety, and apply it to determine when a passer becomes liable (daman) for harm caused.

Core Idea
Passage on a public road is permissible only if safe — violation triggers liability.

This lesson examines a foundational Hanafi principle: the right to use public roads is shared, so it carries a duty of care. The passage from al-Hidayah and al-'Inayah explains how permissibility is conditioned on safety, and how liability arises when that condition is breached. This ruling balances individual need with collective rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
The passage states the principle: 'المرور في طريق المسلمين مباح مقيد بشرط السلامة' — passing through the path of Muslims is permissible but conditioned upon safety.
The condition (shart) is safety, meaning no harm to others.
2
Understand the dual right
The principle is based on the road being shared: the passer acts in his own right (he needs to walk) and in the right of others (they have the same need). This duality demands a balanced ruling.
The text says 'يتصرف في حقه من وجه وفى حق غيره من وجه'.
3
Apply to a concrete case
The author applies it to a rider whose animal causes harm by kicking, trampling, or hitting: the rider is liable because the condition of safety was violated.
The rider (الراكب) is responsible for the animal's actions.
4
Derive the ruling
Thus, the ruling: permissibility is not absolute; it is qualified. If harm occurs, liability (daman) arises. The ruling comes from istihsan balancing both rights.
This is a qiyas or istihsan-based ruling.

Therefore, considering both rights, it is said that passing is permissible but conditioned upon safety, so in case of harm he will be liable. The principle: Passing through the path of Muslims is permissible but conditioned upon safety, because he acts in his own right from one aspect and in the right of others from another aspect, as it is shared among all people. As for acting in his own right: because a person inevitably needs a path to walk for arranging his needs, so preventing him from that is hardship (haraj) and such hardship is repelled. As for acting in the right of others: because others have the same need as he does. So looking at his own right, absolute permissibility is demanded; looking at the right of others, absolute prohibition is demanded. Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage presents a legal principle (qa'idah) from the Hanafi school regarding the use of public property. The principle states that passing through a public road is permissible (mubah) but is qualified by a condition of safety. The commentary explains that the permissibility is not absolute; it is restricted by the requirement not to cause harm. If harm occurs, the passer becomes liable for damages. The reasoning is that a public road belongs to no single individual; everyone has a right to use it. Therefore, a person acts both in his own interest (exercising his right to use the road) and in the interest of others (respecting their equal right). This dual perspective leads to the conclusion that use is permitted but must be exercised without harming others. The specific application given is that if a rider's animal causes injury by kicking, trampling, or hitting, the rider is liable. This follows the principle because the rider's use of the road is permissible only if he ensures safety; if he fails, he must compensate.

Key Terms
المرور (al-murūr): The act of passing or traversing through a public road or path.
مباح (mubāḥ): Legally permissible; an act that is neither commanded nor prohibited, with no reward or punishment.
السلامة (as-salāmah): Safety; the condition of not causing harm to others.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability; financial responsibility to compensate for harm caused.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Absolutely permissible without any condition.
B) Permissible only if one does not cause harm to others.
C) Prohibited because it interferes with others' rights.
D) Recommended (mandub) but not obligatory.

Key Takeaway
Passing through a public road is permissible only on the condition of safety; breaching that condition triggers liability.

Permissible Acts and Liability: The Hanafi Principle
Principle from al-Hidayah on liability for permissible acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that a permissible act can cause liability and apply it to scenarios of unintentional harm in public spaces.

Core Idea
A permissible act becomes a cause of liability when it harms another’s right.

This lesson unpacks a key Hanafi principle from al-Hidayah: actions that are permissible for the doer can still create financial liability if they cause harm to another. Using examples of horsemen colliding and a sleeping person turning over, the text shows that liability follows from the infringement of another's right, even when the act itself is not prohibited.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
Read the Arabic principle: الْفِعْلُ الْمُبَاحُ فِى غَيْرِهِ سَبَبٌ لِلضَّمَانِ. Recognize that it states a general rule about liability.
The principle is concise: a mubah (permissible) act becomes a cause of daman (liability) when it affects another.
2
Understand the Condition
Note that the act is permissible for the doer. However, when it impacts another person, the doer's right is no longer absolute; the other’s right enters the picture.
The permissibility is not a shield against liability.
3
Examine the First Example
Two horsemen collide and both die. Each rider's act of riding is permissible for himself, but it caused the other's death. Therefore, each rider's 'aqlah (clan) must pay full diyah to the other's heirs.
The liability is transferred to the 'aqlah because the killing was unintentional (quasi-intentional or accidental).
4
Examine the Second Example
A sleeping person turns over and kills another. Turning over is permissible, but it causes death, so the sleeper is liable for diyah.
This example reinforces the principle: even an involuntary permissible act can create liability if it harms another.
5
Connect to Public Road Rule
The text concludes by tying back to the rule of passing through a public road: it is permissible but conditioned on safety. If harm occurs, liability arises.
The principle is rooted in balancing individual freedom and the rights of others in shared spaces.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اصول ہوا یہ رائج
۲۶۳
(۲۰) الْفِعْلُ الْمُبَاحُ فِى غَيْرِهِ سَبَبٌ لِلضَّمَانِ.
ترجمہ: مباح کام غیر کے حق میں باعث ضمان ہوتا ہے۔ تشریح مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ ایک کام فاعل کے حق میں مباح ہوتا ہے
لیکن اگر یہ مباح کام دوسرے پر واقع ہو تو اس کے باعث فاعل پر ضمان آتا ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ اگر دو گھڑ سوار آپس میں ٹکرا گئے اور دونوں کی موت ہوگئی، تو ان میں سے ہر ایک کے عاقلہ پر دوسرے کی دیت واجب ہے، کیونکہ ان میں سے ہر ایک کا چلنا اپنے حق میں تو مباح ہے، مگر ان میں سے ہر ایک کا یہ فعل مباح دوسرے کے حق میں ضمان کا سبب ہے، لہذا ان میں سے ہر ایک کے عاقلہ دوسرے کی دیت کے ضامن ہوں گے، جیسے کہ اگر سوئے ہوئے آدمی نے دوسرے پر کروٹ بدل کر اسے مار دیا تو اس پر دیت آئے گی اور یہ ضامن ہوگا، کیونکہ مذکورہ صورت میں کروٹ بدلنا تائم کے لئے مباح کام ہے، لیکن مباح کام جب غیر پر واقع ہو تو با عبث ضمان ہوتا ہے، لہذا مذکورہ صورت میں بھی کروٹ دوسرے پر بدل کر ہلاک کرنا باعث عمان ہوگا۔ اسی خبطت الكدم العض بمقدم الأسنان كما يكدم الحمار، والخبط الضرب باليد (وكذا) أى وكذا يضمن الراكب إذا صدمت الصد الضرب بالجسد..... (ولا يضمن ما نفحت برجلها أو ذنبها نفحت الدابة الشيء إذا ضربته بحافرها. (والأصل) أى فى هذا أن المرور في طريق المسلمين مباح مقيد بشرط السلامة لأنه يتصرف في حقه من وجه، وفي حق غيره من وجه لكونه مشتركا بين كل الناس، فقلنا بالإباحة مقيدا بما ذكرنا من شرط السلامة ليعتدل النظر من الجانبين أى من بين صاحب الدابة وجانب الرجل الذي جنى عليه من جهة الدابة (البناية كتاب الديات جناية البهيمة والجناية عليها، ج ۱۳ ص ۲۵۵ الهداية كتاب الديات باب جناية البهيمة والجناية عليها، ج ٢ ص ٦٠٣
(20) The permissible act in relation to another is a cause of liability. Translation: A permissible act becomes a cause of liability for another. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that an act is permissible for the doer, but if this permissible act affects another, it becomes a cause of liability for the doer. The author of al-Hidayah, after mentioning the principle, explains that if two horsemen collide and both die, then upon the 'aqlah of each, the full diyah of the other is wajib (obligatory), because the riding of each is permissible for himself, but this permissible act is a cause of liability for the other. So the 'aqlah of each will be liable for the diyah of the other. Likewise, if a sleeping person turns over and strikes another, causing death, he becomes liable for diyah. The principle is that passing through the road of Muslims is permissible but conditioned upon safety, because he acts in his own right from one aspect and in the right of others from another aspect. (Al-Binayah, Kitab al-Diyat, Bab al-Bahimah wa al-Jinayah 'Alayha, vol. 13, p. 255; al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Diyat, Bab al-Bahimah wa al-Jinayah 'Alayha, vol. 2, p. 603).
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage presents another legal principle: 'A permissible act, when it concerns the right of another, becomes a cause of liability (daman).' The principle is then applied to the case of two horsemen colliding; each horseman's act of riding is permissible for himself, but since it resulted in harm to the other, each is liable for the full diyah (blood-money) through his 'aqlah (the clan responsible for paying diyah). The author compares this to the case of a sleeping person who turns over and unintentionally kills someone; turning over is permissible for the sleeper, but it causes liability. The explanation ties back to the earlier principle about passing through a public road: the act is generally permissible but conditioned on safety. The source references are from al-Binayah and al-Hidayah, confirming the Hanafi position that liability can stem from a permissible act when it infringes on another's right.

Key Terms
الْفِعْلُ الْمُبَاحُ (al-fi'l al-mubāḥ): A legally permissible act; one that is neither commanded nor prohibited.
سَبَبٌ لِلضَّمَانِ (sababun liḍ-ḍamān): A cause that creates financial liability (daman).
عاقلہ ('āqilah): The clan, tribe, or group of male relatives who are collectively responsible for paying diyah (blood-money) for unintentional killings.
دیت (diyah): Blood-money; the financial compensation paid to the victim's heirs for unlawful killing or injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of two horsemen colliding, why is each rider's 'aqlah liable for the full diyah of the other?
A) Because riding a horse in a public road is prohibited.
B) Because each rider's permissible act became a cause of harm to the other, creating liability.
C) Because the riders intended to harm each other.
D) Because the horses were uncontrollable.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle from al-Hidayah establishes that a permissible act becomes a cause of liability when it harms another, ensuring compensation for the injured party.

The Permissible Act That Affects Another Creates Liability
States a legal maxim on liability from permissible acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim on liability from permissible acts and apply it to scenarios of accidental harm.

Core Idea
A permissible act that causes harm to another incurs liability – permissibility does not excuse consequences.

This passage introduces a foundational Hanafi maxim: even an act that is perfectly lawful for the doer can give rise to liability if it causes harm to another. The author of al-Hidayah illustrates this with several vivid examples, from horsemen colliding to a sleeper rolling over onto someone. The principle underscores that Islamic law balances individual freedom with responsibility for unintended consequences. Let us unpack the maxim and its applications.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its meaning
Read the Arabic formulation and the Urdu explanation. Understand that 'al-fi‘l al-mubāḥ' is an act permissible for the doer, but when it affects another (fī ghayrihi), it becomes a cause for liability (ḍamān).
The maxim does not change the permissibility of the act; it adds a consequence.
2
Examine the primary examples
Analyze the three cases from al-Hidayah: two horsemen colliding, a sleeper turning over, and a person digging a well on a public road. In each, the act is mubah for the doer, but because it caused harm, the doer (or his 'aqila) must pay blood-money.
The principle applies regardless of intent – it is about causation and harm.
3
Identify the underlying condition
Note the condition mentioned in the commentary from al-Binayah: passing through a Muslim road is permissible but conditioned on safety. This highlights that permissibility is not absolute; it is qualified by not harming others.
The maxim reflects the balance between individual right and collective safety.
4
Extract the legal principle
Formulate the legal rule: If a permissible act directly causes harm to another, the doer is liable for damages. This is a strict liability principle in Hanafi fiqh for unintentional harm.
This is distinct from liability arising from prohibited acts.
5
Apply to novel scenarios
Consider other cases where a mubah act might cause harm: e.g., a driver carefully reversing his car but hitting a pedestrian; a farmer burning stubble on his land that spreads to a neighbor's crop. Apply the maxim: the act is permissible, but because it caused harm, liability arises.
Ensure the harm is direct and caused by the act itself.

ترجمہ: مباح کام غیر کے حق میں باعث ضمان ہوتا ہے۔ تشریح مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ ایک کام فاعل کے حق میں مباح ہوتا ہے لیکن اگر یہ مباح کام دوسرے پر واقع ہو تو اس کے باعث فاعل پر ضمان آتا ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ اگر دو گھڑ سوار آپس میں ٹکرا گئے اور دونوں کی موت ہوگئی، تو ان میں سے ہر ایک کے عاقلہ پر دوسرے کی دیت واجب ہے، کیونکہ ان میں سے ہر ایک کا چلنا اپنے حق میں تو مباح ہے، مگر ان میں سے ہر ایک کا یہ فعل مباح دوسرے کے حق میں ضمان کا سبب ہے، لہذا ان میں سے ہر ایک کے عاقلہ دوسرے کی دیت کے ضامن ہوں گے، جیسے کہ اگر سوئے ہوئے آدمی نے دوسرے پر کروٹ بدل کر اسے مار دیا تو اس پر دیت آئے گی اور یہ ضامن ہوگا، کیونکہ مذکورہ صورت میں کروٹ بدلنا تائم کے لئے مباح کام ہے، لیکن مباح کام جب غیر پر واقع ہو تو باعث ضمان ہوتا ہے، لہذا مذکورہ صورت میں بھی کروٹ دوسرے پر بدل کر ہلاک کرنا باعث ضمان ہوگا۔ اسی خبطت الكدم العض بمقدم الأسنان كما يكدم الحمار، والخبط الضرب باليد (وكذا) أى وكذا يضمن الراكب إذا صدمت الصد الضرب بالجسد..... (ولا يضمن ما نفحت برجلها أو ذنبها نفحت الدابة الشيء إذا ضربته بحافرها. (والأصل) أى فى هذا أن المرور في طريق المسلمين مباح مقيد بشرط السلامة لأنه يتصرف في حقه من وجه، وفي حق غيره من وجه لكونه مشتركا بين كل الناس، فقلنا بالإباحة مقيدا بما ذكرنا من شرط السلامة ليعتدل النظر من الجانبين أى من بين صاحب الدابة وجانب الرجل الذي جنى عليه من جهة الدابة (البناية كتاب الديات جناية البهيمة والجناية عليها، ج ۱۳ ص ۲۵۵ الهداية كتاب الديات باب جناية البهيمة والجناية عليها، ج ٢ ص ٦٠٣
طرح ایک شخص کنواں کھودے اور راستے پر چلنے والے کو معلوم نہ ہو اور وہ گر کر مر جائے تو یہ حافر ضامن ہوگا، اس لیے کہ راستہ پر چلنا ہر ایک کے لیے مباح ہے اور اس شخص نے کنواں کھودا جو دوسرے کی ہلاکت کا باعث بنا، لہذا یہ ضامن ہوگا۔
The permissible act with respect to another is a cause for liability. Translation: A permissible act becomes a cause for liability in respect of another. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that an act is permissible for the doer, but if this permissible act occurs upon another, then liability is incurred by the doer. The author of al-Hidayah, mentioning this principle, explains that if two horsemen collide and both die, then upon the 'aqila of each is wajib the blood-money of the other, because the walking of each is permissible for themselves, but this permissible act of each is a cause for liability in respect of the other. Therefore the 'aqila of each will be guarantors for the blood-money of the other. Similarly, if a sleeping person turns over upon another and kills him, blood-money is due upon him and he is liable, because in that case turning over is a permissible act for the sleeper, but when a permissible act occurs upon another it becomes a cause for liability. Likewise, (the striking) al-kadm (biting with the front teeth like a donkey bites), and al-khabt (striking with the hand) (and likewise) i.e., likewise the rider is liable if he collides (with his body) ... (And he is not liable for what the animal strikes with its leg or tail) (al-nafh: the animal striking with its hoof). (The principle) i.e., in this, that passing through the Muslims' road is permissible, conditioned upon safety, because it is a right from one aspect and a right of others from another aspect due to being shared among all people. So we say permissibility is conditioned upon what we mentioned of the condition of safety so that consideration is balanced from both sides, i.e., between the owner of the animal and the person injured by the animal. (al-Binayah, Kitab al-Diyat, Bab Jinaayat al-Bahimah, vol.13 p.255; al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Diyat, Bab Jinaayat al-Bahimah, vol.2 p.603). Likewise, a person who digs a well, and a passerby unaware falls and dies, then the digger is liable, because walking on the road is permissible for everyone, and that person dug the well which caused the death of another, therefore he is liable.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage presents the twentieth legal maxim from Usool al-Hidayah: 'The permissible act that affects another is a cause for liability.' The author first states the maxim in Arabic, then provides an Urdu translation and explanation. The key point is that an act may be permissible (mubah) for the doer individually, but if it causes harm to another person, the doer becomes liable for damages. The author cites multiple examples from the Hidayah: two horsemen colliding accidentally, a sleeping person turning over and killing another, and a person digging a well on a public road causing someone to fall in. In each case, the original act (walking, turning over, digging) is permissible, but because it results in harm to another, liability (daman) attaches. The commentary from al-Binayah adds the principle that passing through a Muslim road is permissible but conditioned on safety, reflecting the balance of rights between the road user and others. This maxim underscores that permissibility does not absolve one from responsibility for consequences to others.

Key Terms
الفعل المباح (al-fi‘l al-mubāḥ): An act that is legally permissible for the doer, neither commanded nor prohibited.
الضمان (al-ḍamān): Liability to compensate for harm caused, typically by paying blood-money (diyah) or damages.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The 'aqila of each horseman pays the blood-money of the other.
C) The state pays the blood-money.
D) Each horseman's own estate pays his own blood-money.

Key Takeaway
A permissible act that directly causes harm to another person creates liability for damages in Hanafi fiqh.

Causing Harm with Transgression Creates Liability
States a legal maxim with examples from diyat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim that causation with transgression creates liability and apply it to distinguish between permissible and transgressive causation in tort cases.

Core Idea
Only causation with transgression (ta‘addī) creates liability; purely permissible acts do not make one liable.

This lesson introduces the twenty-first legal maxim from Usool al-Hidayah: causation with transgression is grounds for liability. The principle refines the concept of causal responsibility: not every cause leads to liability—only those where the cause involved exceeding proper bounds. We will explore the maxim through the classic example of two horsemen colliding and the well-digger scenario.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its core condition
Read the Arabic maxim: التَّسْبِيبُ بِوَصْفِ التَّعَدِّى سَبَبٌ لِلضَّمَان. Identify the two key elements: causation (tasbib) and transgression (ta'addi). The maxim declares that when both are present, liability (daman) arises.
The word بِوَصْفِ indicates that transgression qualifies the causation.
2
Understand the distinction between permissible and prohibited acts
Note that the maxim does not attach liability to causation alone; it requires that the act of causation be transgressive (e.g., digging a well on a public road). Purely permissible acts (e.g., walking on a road) do not generate liability even if they contribute to harm.
This prevents over-broad liability for everyday actions.
3
Apply to the horsemen collision example
Study the ruling: two free horsemen collide by mistake → each pays full blood-money to the other's family. If they are slaves, no blood-money because they are 'wasted'. If intentional, each pays half. The maxim explains: the act of riding is permissible, but the collision involves transgression (both acted without care).
The restriction to 'free' and 'mistake' shows the maxim's nuanced application.
4
Apply to the walker and well-digger scenario
When a person walks on a public road and falls into an unlawfully dug well, the digger is liable, not the walker. Even though both acts (walking and digging) contributed to the fall, walking is permissible, so it does not attract liability. The digger's transgression (digging in a public path) makes him liable.
This illustrates that permissibility shields from liability even when causation is present.
5
Generalize the maxim
Formulate the rule: in Hanafi tort law, an indirect cause (tasbib) only creates liability if the causative act involved transgression. If the act is permitted (mubah), the harm is not attributed to the actor for liability purposes.
This maxim works alongside others, e.g., when both acts are transgressive, liability may be shared.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٢١) التَّسْبِيبُ بِوَصْفِ التَّعَدِّى سَبَبٌ لِلضَّمَان.
ترجمہ: وصف تعدی کے ساتھ سبب بننا ضمان کا سبب ہے۔
تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ اگر ایک آدمی دوسرے کی ہلاکت کا سبب بنا تو دیکھا جائے اگر اس کے سبب بنے میں اس نے تعدی کی تو وہ ضامن ہوگا، اور اگر اس نے (وإذا اصطدم فارسان حران خطأ (فماتا) منه فعلى عاقلة كل واحد منهما دية الآخر) لأن قتل كل واحد منهما مضاف إلى فعل الآخر. قيدنا بالحرين لأنهما لو كانا عبدين فهما هدر سواء كان خطأ أو عمداً، أما الأول فلأن الجنايات تعلقت برقبة كل منهما دفعاً وفداء، وقد فات بغير فعل المولى، وأما الثاني فلأن كل واحد منهما هلك بعدما جنى فيسقط. وقيدنا بالخطأ لأنه لو كان عامدين ضمن كل واحد منهما نصف الدية، لأن فعل كل واحد منهما محظور، وأضيف التلف إلى فعلهما كما في الاختيار اللباب في شرح الكتاب كتاب الديات، باب جناية البهيمة والجناية عليها، ج ۳ ص ١٦٨) وأما فيما نحن فيه فالمشى مباح محض فلم ينعقد موجبا للضمان في حق نفسه أصلا فكان صاحبه قاتلا له من غير معارضة أحد له في قتله، فيجب على عاقلة كل منهما تمام دية الآخر، كمن مشى حتى سقط في البئر ضمن الحافر وإن كان السقـوط بـالـحـفـر والمشى جميعا، لكن لما كان المشى مباحا لم يعتبر . (العناية : كتاب الديات، باب جناية البهيمة والجناية عليها، ج ۱۰ ص ۳۳۰ الهداية كتاب الديات باب جناية البهيمة والجناية عليها، ج ۳ ص ۶۰۴
Causation with the description of transgression is a cause for liability. Translation: Causing with the attribute of transgression is a cause for liability. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that if a person causes the death of another, then it is examined: if in his causing he committed transgression, then he is liable; and if not... (And if two free horsemen collide by mistake and both die, then upon the 'aqila of each is the blood-money of the other) because the killing of each is attributed to the act of the other. We restricted it to 'free' because if they were slaves, they are wasted (no blood-money) whether it was mistake or intentional. As for the first (free horsemen), the crimes attach to the neck of each for defense or ransom, and that is lost without the act of the master. As for the second (slaves), each perishes after committing a crime, so it is dropped. And we restricted it to 'mistake' because if they were intentional, each would be liable for half the blood-money, because the act of each is prohibited, and destruction is attributed to their acts as in al-Ikhtiyar, Lubab fi Sharh al-Kitab, Kitab al-Diyat, Bab Jinaayat al-Bahimah, vol.3 p.168. But in our case, walking is pure permissible, so it does not constitute a cause for liability in their own right at all. So each becomes a killer of the other without anyone opposing him in killing, so upon the 'aqila of each is due the complete blood-money of the other, like one who walks and falls into a well; the digger is liable even though the fall was due to both the well and the walking, but because walking is permissible, it is not considered. (al-'Inayah: Kitab al-Diyat, Bab Jinaayat al-Bahimah, vol.10 p.330; al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Diyat, Bab Jinaayat al-Bahimah, vol.3 p.604)
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage presents the twenty-first legal maxim from Usool al-Hidayah: 'Causation with the description of transgression is a cause for liability.' The Arabic maxim is followed by an Urdu explanation: if someone causes another's death, we look at whether the causation involved transgression (ta'addi). If it did, the causer is liable; if not (i.e., the act was purely permissible), liability may still arise but through the previous maxim. The author then quotes a detailed ruling from al-Ikhtiyar on two free horsemen colliding by mistake. They are free so blood-money is due; if they were slaves, no blood-money because they are 'wasted' (hader). If the collision was intentional, each pays half blood-money because both acts were prohibited. The passage then distinguishes the case under discussion (walking on a road) where walking is pure permissible, so the act does not itself constitute a cause for liability; rather, the death is attributed entirely to the other's act (e.g., the well-digger). This maxim refines the previous one: the permissibility of the act matters, but when both acts are permissible, liability falls on the one who committed transgression (like digging a well in a public road).

Key Terms
التسبيب (al-tasbīb): Causation, i.e., being the cause of harm, whether directly or indirectly.
التعدي (al-ta‘addī): Transgression, exceeding proper bounds, e.g., digging a well in a public path or acting in a way that violates the condition of safety.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Qawida 21, if a person walks on a public road and falls into a well dug by another, who is liable?
A) The walker, because he chose to walk.
B) The digger, because his act involved transgression.
C) Both are equally liable.
D) No one is liable because both acts were permissible.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi tort law, causation only creates liability when it involves transgression; permissible causation is not actionable.

Causation with Transgression: The First Principle of Liability
First maxim and well case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that indirect causation (tasbīb) with transgression (taʻaddī) creates liability (ḍamān) and apply it to scenarios of harm caused by permissible acts.

Core Idea
Causation alone does not necessitate liability; transgression (taʻaddī) is the decisive element.

In Hanafi tort law, merely causing harm is not enough to trigger liability. The critical factor is whether the causation involved transgression—exceeding what is legally permissible. This lesson introduces the foundational maxim that causation plus transgression equals liability, illustrated by the classic case of a well dug on a public path.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its permissibility
First, determine whether the underlying act (e.g., walking, digging) is permissible (mubāḥ) or prohibited. Permissible acts do not generate liability for the actor if harm results.
The well-digging is a transgression because it obstructs a public path without warning.
2
Determine if the causer committed transgression
Transgression (taʻaddī) occurs when the causer acts negligently or violates a duty. If the act was permissible, the causer is not liable; if transgression is present, liability attaches.
The digger acted with taʻaddī by digging without warning or permission.
3
Apply the maxim to the case
Since the digger's action was a cause (sabab) of death and involved transgression, the maxim applies: he is liable (ḍāmin). The passerby's walking is permissible and thus not a contributing factor.
The maxim is: 'التَّسْبِيبُ بِوَصْفِ التَّعَدِّى سَبَبٌ لِلضَّمَان'.
4
Distinguish from the horsemen collision
In the example of two horsemen colliding by mistake, each is liable because both acted with taʻaddī (riding negligently). Their acts are not permissible, unlike walking.
If both acted intentionally, each pays half the diyah because their mutual fault reduces liability.

تعدی نہیں کی تو وہ ضامن نہ ہو گا۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر کسی نے اونٹوں کی قطار کو آگے سے کھینچا اور اونٹوں نے کسی کو روند کر ہلاک کر دیا تو وہ قاعد ضامن ہوگا، کیونکہ مذکورہ بالا صورت میں قاعد اس آدمی کی ہلاکت کا سبب متعدی بنا ہے اور مسبب متعدی پر ضمان آتا ہے، لہذا مذکورہ صورت میں بھی قاعد پر ضمان آئے گا، البتہ اگر مالی نقصان ہوا ہے تو وہ قائد کے مال پر ہوگا اور اگر جانی نقصان ہوا ہے تو اس کی ادائیگی عاقلہ پر ہوگی ۔
(۲۲) اَلْفِعْلُ الاخْتِيَارِيُّ يُضَافُ إِلَى فَاعِلِهِ وَلَا يَجُوزُ إِضَافَتُهُ إِلَى غَيْرِهِ. ترجمہ فعل اختیاری کی نسبت اس کے فاعل کی طرف ہوتی ہے غیر فاعل کی طرف فعل اختیاری کی نسبت کرنا جائز نہیں ہے۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جو فعل اختیاری ہو اس کی نسبت بصورت منمان
(قال) أى القدورى: (ومن قاد (قطارا القطار الإبل يقطر على نسق واحد : والجمع قطر. (فهو أى القائد (صامن لما أوطأ) أى القطار (فإن وطئ بعير) أى من القطار إنسانا) ضمن به القائد والدية على العاقلة، لأن القائد عليه حفظ القطار كالسائق وقد أمكنه ذلك وقد صار متعديا بالتقصير فيه والتسبيب بوصف التعدى سبب للضمان، إلا أن ضمان النفس على العاقلة فيه وضمان المال في ماله. البناية كتاب الديات، باب جناية البهيمة والجناية عليها، ج ۱۳ ص ۲۶۴) وَمَنْ قَادَ قِطَارًا فَهُوَ ضَامِنٌ لِمَا أَوْطَأَ فَإِنْ وَطِئَ بَعِيرٌ إِنْسَانًا ضَمِنَ بِهِ الدِّيَةَ عَلَى الْعَاقِلَةِ، وَإِنْ أَتْلَفَ مَالًا فَعَلَيْهِ الضَّمَانُ مِنْ مَالِهِ. (مجمع الضمانات بابا
مسائل الجنايات فصل الخامس في جناية البهيمة، ج ۱ ص۱۸۷)
الهداية كتاب الديات، باب فى جناية البهيمة والجناية عليها، ج۴ ص ۶۰۴ في
A person dug a well on a pathway, and a passerby unaware fell and died; the digger is liable because walking on the path is permissible for everyone, and the digger caused the death. So he is liable.
(21) Causation with the attribute of transgression is a cause for liability.
Translation: Creating a cause with transgression is a cause for liability.
Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that if a person causes the death of another, then if he acted with transgression in causing it, he is liable; if he did not act with transgression, he is not liable. (And if two free horsemen collide by mistake, both die, then on the ʻāqilah of each is the blood money of the other) because the killing of each is attributed to the other's action. We restricted it to free men because if they were slaves, both are wasted, whether by mistake or intentionally. As for the first, because the injuries are attached to the neck of each for payment and ransom, and they perished without the master's action. As for the second, because each one died after committing injury, so it falls. And we restricted it to mistake because if they were intentional, each would be liable for half the blood money, because each one's action is prohibited, and the destruction is attributed to both actions, as in al-Ikhtiyār, al-Lubāb fī Sharh al-Kitāb, Kitāb al-Diyāt, Bāb Jīnāyat al-Bahīmah, vol. 3, p. 168. As for our case, walking is pure permissible, so it does not create liability for the person himself at all, so the owner is the killer of him without any opposition to him in killing, so upon the ʻāqilah of each is the full blood money of the other. Like one who walks and falls into a well, the digger is liable even though the fall was by both digging and walking, but because walking is permissible, it is not considered. (al-ʻInāyah: Kitāb al-Diyāt, Bāb Jīnāyat al-Bahīmah, vol. 10, p. 330; al-Hidāyah, Kitāb al-Diyāt, Bāb Jīnāyat al-Bahīmah, vol. 3, p. 604)

Principles of Hidayah
265
If he did not commit transgression, he is not liable.
The author of Hidayah, after mentioning this principle, states: If someone pulls a camel train from the front and the camels trample and kill a person, then the leader is liable because in the aforementioned case the leader became a cause with transgression for the person's death, and a cause with transgression incurs liability. Thus, in this case, the leader is liable. However, if the loss is of property, it is from the leader's wealth; if it is of life, its payment is on the ʻāqilah.
(22) A voluntary act is attributed to its doer, and it is not permissible to attribute it to other than him.
Translation: The attribution of a voluntary act is to its doer; it is not permissible to attribute a voluntary act to other than the doer.
Explanation: The meaning of the above principle is that a voluntary act is attributed [to its doer] in the case of [prevention?].
(He said, i.e., al-Qudūrī: And whoever leads a train—the train of camels, one after another, plural qitār—then he, i.e., the leader, is liable for what it causes. So if a camel, i.e., of the train, tramples a human, the leader is liable for the blood money upon the ʻāqilah. Because the leader is responsible for guarding the train like the driver, and he had the ability to do so, and he became a transgressor by negligence in it, and causation with the attribute of transgression is a cause for liability, except that liability for life is on the ʻāqilah, and liability for property is on his own wealth.) (al-Bināyah, Kitāb al-Diyāt, Bāb Jīnāyat al-Bahīmah, vol. 13, p. 264) And whoever leads a train is liable for what it tramples; if a camel tramples a human, he is liable for the blood money on the ʻāqilah; if it destroys property, then liability is from his own wealth. (Majmaʻ al-Ḍamānāt, Bāb Masāʼil al-Jīnāyāt, al-Faṣl al-Khāmis fī Jīnāyat al-Bahīmah, vol. 1, p. 187)
Al-Hidāyah, Kitāb al-Diyāt, Bāb fī Jīnāyat al-Bahīmah, vol. 4, p. 604.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage presents two important legal maxims (qawāʻid) from Hanafi fiqh related to liability (ḍamān) in torts and criminal injuries. The first maxim states that causation (tasbīb) combined with transgression (taʻaddī) creates liability. The author explains that if one causes harm through a permissible act (like walking on a public path), he is not liable, but if his act involves transgression (like digging a well on a path without warning), he becomes liable. The example of two horsemen colliding by mistake illustrates that each is liable for the other's death through their joint actions, but if intentional, liability is halved due to mutual fault. The distinction between free and slave victims is also noted: slave deaths are considered 'wasted' (hadar) because the injury attaches to the slave's value. The second maxim states that voluntary acts are attributed solely to their doer, reinforcing individual responsibility. The camel-leader (qāʼid) case applies the first maxim: leading a camel train is a permissible act, but if the leader fails to control it (transgression), he is liable. However, the type of liability differs: for loss of life, the blood money (diyah) is paid by the ʻāqilah (tribal or professional group), while for property damage, he pays from his own wealth. This distinction is based on the principle that liability for life is shared by the ʻāqilah to spread the burden, while property liability remains personal.

Key Terms
تسبيب (tasbīb): Creating a cause for harm without directly committing the act.
تعدی (taʻaddī): Transgression or exceeding the bounds of what is permissible, such as acting negligently or violating a duty.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability to compensate for loss or injury, whether financial or physical.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi maxim, which element is essential for liability in indirect causation?
A) Direct physical contact between causer and victim
B) The causer's act being prohibited by default
C) The presence of transgression (taʻaddī) in the causative act
D) The victim's act being impermissible

Key Takeaway
Liability for indirect causation requires transgression (taʻaddī); permissible acts by the victim do not reduce the causer's responsibility.
