Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
10 min
Attribution of Voluntary Acts and Liability of the Camel Leader (Qāʼid)
Second maxim and camel leader case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim that voluntary acts are attributed to their doer and derive the ruling that the leader (qāʼid) of a camel train is liable for harm caused by the animals due to transgression in control, distinguishing between life and property liability.

Core Idea
A voluntary act is attributed only to its doer; however, if control is neglected, the causer becomes liable through transgression.

The second maxim emphasizes individual responsibility for voluntary actions. In the context of harm caused by animals, the leader who fails to control them is considered a transgressor and is liable. This lesson explores the application of both maxims to the camel leader (qāʼid) case and the distinction between liability for life (paid by ʻāqilah) and property (paid from personal wealth).

Guided Path
1
State the second maxim
The voluntary act is attributed to its doer. This means that if harm is caused by an animal, we look to the person who had control over it, not to the animal itself.
The maxim is: 'الْفِعْلُ الاخْتِيَارِيُّ يُضَافُ إِلَى فَاعِلِهِ'.
2
Identify the leader's role
The leader (qāʼid) guides the camel train from the front. He has the ability to control the animals, just like the driver (sāʼiq) who drives from behind.
Both are responsible for safeguarding the train (ḥifẓ).
3
Establish transgression
If the leader fails to control the train and harm results, he has committed transgression (taʻaddī) by negligence. This brings the first maxim into play.
Transgression here is the failure to control despite ability.
4
Apply the first maxim (qiyas)
Since the leader is analogous to the driver, and both causally contribute to harm with transgression, the leader is liable. The qiyas is based on the common illa: ability to control and duty to safeguard.
The first maxim: tasbīb with taʻaddī creates liability.
5
Distinguish life vs property liability
For loss of life, blood money (diyah) is paid by the ʻāqilah (the leader's tribal or professional group). For property damage, the leader pays from his own wealth.
This is a general Hanafi rule: life liability is spread to avoid crushing individual burden.

265
If he did not commit transgression, he is not liable.
The author of Hidayah, after mentioning this principle, states: If someone pulls a camel train from the front and the camels trample and kill a person, then the leader is liable because in the aforementioned case the leader became a cause with transgression for the person's death, and a cause with transgression incurs liability. Thus, in this case, the leader is liable. However, if the loss is of property, it is from the leader's wealth; if it is of life, its payment is on the ʻāqilah.

Commentary
This passage presents two important legal maxims (qawāʻid) from Hanafi fiqh related to liability (ḍamān) in torts and criminal injuries. The first maxim states that causation (tasbīb) combined with transgression (taʻaddī) creates liability. The author explains that if one causes harm through a permissible act (like walking on a public path), he is not liable, but if his act involves transgression (like digging a well on a path without warning), he becomes liable. The example of two horsemen colliding by mistake illustrates that each is liable for the other's death through their joint actions, but if intentional, liability is halved due to mutual fault. The distinction between free and slave victims is also noted: slave deaths are considered 'wasted' (hadar) because the injury attaches to the slave's value. The second maxim states that voluntary acts are attributed solely to their doer, reinforcing individual responsibility. The camel-leader (qāʼid) case applies the first maxim: leading a camel train is a permissible act, but if the leader fails to control it (transgression), he is liable. However, the type of liability differs: for loss of life, the blood money (diyah) is paid by the ʻāqilah (tribal or professional group), while for property damage, he pays from his own wealth. This distinction is based on the principle that liability for life is shared by the ʻāqilah to spread the burden, while property liability remains personal.

Key Terms
قائد (qāʼid): One who leads a camel train (qitār) from the front.
عاقلہ (ʻāqilah): The group of male relatives (or a professional group) who pay blood money (diyah) on behalf of a member who caused accidental death or injury.
تسبيب (tasbīb): Creating a cause for harm without directly committing the act.
تعدی (taʻaddī): Transgression or exceeding the bounds of what is permissible, such as acting negligently or violating a duty.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability to compensate for loss or injury, whether financial or physical.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a camel leader (qāʼid) causes the death of a person through negligence, who pays the blood money (diyah)?
A) The leader from his own wealth
B) The camel owner
C) The leader's ʻāqilah
D) Both the leader and the owner equally

Key Takeaway
The leader's liability is established by qiyas to the driver; life compensation is borne by the ʻāqilah, while property compensation is personal.

The Voluntary Act Is Attributed to Its Doer
Introducing the legal maxim on attribution of voluntary acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim on attribution of voluntary acts and apply it to determine liability in cases of animal-caused harm.

Core Idea
A voluntary act is legally attributed only to its doer — no one else can bear its liability.

This lesson presents a foundational Hanafi legal maxim that governs the attribution of voluntary acts for purposes of liability. The maxim is stated in Arabic and then applied to two classic cases from al-Hidayah: a person leading a line of camels and a person releasing an animal. Through these examples, we see how the doer's voluntary action — leading or releasing — is the basis for liability, even when the harm is directly caused by an animal. The lesson also draws an analogy with coercion, showing that if the doer acts as a mere tool, the act may be attributed to the coercer.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Read the Arabic maxim 'اَلْفِعْلُ الاخْتِيَارِيُّ يُضَافُ إِلَى فَاعِلِهِ' and its Urdu/English translation. Understand that the maxim restricts attribution to the doer of the voluntary act.
The maxim is a qa‘ida fiqhiyya (legal maxim) used in diyat and other chapters.
2
Define key terms
Identify the terms: الفعل الاختیاری (voluntary act), فاعل (doer), إضافة (attribution), ضمان (liability). Understand that 'voluntary' implies free will and choice, distinguishing it from involuntary acts.
The Hanafi school defines volition carefully; coercion may transfer attribution.
3
Analyze the first case (leading camels)
Read the case: a person leads a line of camels; a camel tramples a person or property. The leader is liable because leading is a voluntary act attributed to him. Liability for personal injury is on the ‘āqila, for property on his own wealth.
The leader is considered a transgressor if negligent; causation with transgression triggers liability.
4
Analyze the second case (releasing an animal)
Read the case: a person releases an animal and is its driver; the animal immediately causes harm. The releaser is liable because releasing is his voluntary act, and the animal's act is attributed to him as its impeller.
The condition 'in its immediate course' (fī fawrihā) ensures direct causation.
5
Understand the coercion analogy
The text compares the animal's act to that of a coerced person: if a coercer forces someone to act as a tool, the act is attributed to the coercer. Similarly, the animal, being under the human's control, is like a tool.
This analogy reinforces that the maxim applies when the doer has effective control over the cause.

Source Text

في
فاعل کی طرف ہوگی غیر فاعل کی طرف نہیں ہوگی، یعنی فاعل ہی ضامن ہوگا نہ کہ غیر فاعل ۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر کسی نے جانور، مثلا کتا چھوڑ دیا اور وہ اس کے لئے سائق بھی تھا، پھر فوراً اس جانور نے کسی کو نقصان پہنچایا تو مرسل ضامن ہوگا، کیونکہ ارسال فعل اختیاری ہے اور فعل اختیاری کی نسبت فاعل کی طرف ہوتی ہے، الیڈ امڈ کور [؟] صورت میں بھی ارسال کی نسبت مرسل کی طرف ہوگی ، اور وہ مرسل ضامن ہوگا۔ جس طرح مکرہ کا فعل منسوب ہوتا ہے مگرہ کی طرف جو آلہ بنے کی صلاحیت رکھتا ہو۔
(البناية: كتاب الديات، باب جناية البهيمة والجناية عليها، ج ۱۳ ص ٢٦٦) قَالَ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ وَمَنْ أَرْسَلَ بَهِيمَةً وَكَانَ لَهَا سَائِقًا فَأَصَابَتْ فِي فَوْرِهَا ضَمِنَ لِأَنَّهُ الْحَامِلُ لَهَا فَأُضِيفَ فِعْلُهَا إِلَيْهِ كَمَا يُضَافُ فِعْلُ الْمُكْرَهِ إِلَى الْمُكْرِهِ فِيمَا يَصْلُحُ آلَةً لَهُ . تبيين الحقائق كتاب الديات، باب جناية البهيمة والجناية عليها، ج ۶ ص ۱۵۲) الهداية كتاب الديات باب فى جناية البهيمة والجناية عليها، ج۴ ص ۶۰۵
(22) The voluntary act is attributed to its doer, and it is not permissible to attribute it to other than him. Translation: The attribution of a voluntary act is to its doer; it is not permissible to attribute a voluntary act to other than its doer. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that any voluntary act is attributed to its doer, not to others. The author of al-Hidayah, after mentioning this principle, states: If someone leads a line of camels, he is liable for what the camels trample. If a camel tramples a person, the leader is liable for the blood-money on behalf of the ‘aqila (tribal guarantors), because the leader is responsible for guarding the line like a driver, and he is able to do so. He becomes transgressor by negligence and causation with the description of transgression is a cause for liability, except that liability for a person is on the ‘aqila and liability for property is on his wealth. (al-Binayah, Book of Diyat, Chapter on Injury by Animals and Injury to Them, vol. 13 p. 264) Whoever leads a line of camels is liable for what they trample; if a camel tramples a person, he is liable for the blood-money on the ‘aqila, and if it destroys property, the liability is on his wealth. (Majma‘ al-Damanat, Section on Issues of Injuries, Fifth Chapter on Injuries by Animals, vol. 1 p. 187) Al-Hidayah, Book of Diyat, Chapter on Injury by Animals and Injury to Them, vol. 4 p. 604. It will be attributed to the doer, not to other than the doer, meaning the doer will be liable, not the other. The author of al-Hidayah, after mentioning this principle, states: If someone releases an animal, for example a dog, and he is also its driver, and immediately that animal causes harm to someone, then the releaser will be liable, because releasing is a voluntary act and a voluntary act is attributed to its doer; in this case too, the releasing is attributed to the releaser, and he will be liable, just as the act of a coerced person is attributed to the coercer if the coerced is capable of being a tool. (al-Binayah, Book of Diyat, Chapter on Injury by Animals and Injury to Them, vol. 13 p. 266) He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Whoever releases an animal and is its driver, and it causes harm immediately, he is liable because he is its impeller, so its act is attributed to him, just as the act of a coerced person is attributed to the coercer in what is suitable as a tool for him. Tabyin al-Haqa’iq, Book of Diyat, Chapter on Injury by Animals and Injury to Them, vol. 6 p. 152. Al-Hidayah, Book of Diyat, Chapter on Injury by Animals and Injury to Them, vol. 4 p. 605.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This lesson presents a fundamental legal maxim: a voluntary act is attributed only to its doer. The author first states the maxim in Arabic, then provides an Urdu translation and explanation. The maxim is then applied to two cases from the Hidayah. The first case: a person leading a line of camels is liable for any damage they cause, because his leading is a voluntary act. The second case: a person who releases an animal while driving it from behind is liable for immediate harm, since the release is his voluntary act. This attribution holds even if the harm is caused by the animal, as the animal's act is attributed to the human actor due to his causal role. The author also draws an analogy with coercion: the act of a coerced person is attributed to the coercer if the coerced serves as a mere tool.

Key Terms
الفعل الاختیاری (al-fi‘l al-ikhtiyārī): An act performed by a person with free will and choice, as opposed to involuntary acts like sleep or coercion.
فاعل (fā‘il): The doer or agent who voluntarily performs an act.
إضافة (iḍāfah): Legal attribution of an act to a person for purposes of liability.
ضمان (ḍamān): Civil liability, typically for compensation or blood-money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim in this lesson, who is liable if a person voluntarily releases a dog that immediately bites someone?
A) The owner of the dog if he was absent
B) No one, because animals are not liable
C) The person who released it, because the release is a voluntary act
D) The person who was bitten for not avoiding the dog

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'al-fi‘l al-ikhtiyārī yudāfu ilā fā‘ilihī' establishes that liability for harm follows the voluntary act of the doer, not the indirect cause.

The Maxim of Liability for Animal Injuries
Defining and illustrating the maxim of non-liability for animal injuries

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'Jarh al-‘ajma’ jubar' and apply it to distinguish between cases where the owner is liable and not liable for animal-caused harm.

Core Idea
Autonomous animal harm = no liability; controlled animal harm = owner liable.

This lesson introduces a foundational maxim from the Hanafi school: injuries caused by a dumb animal (al-‘ajma’) are without liability (jubar). This maxim sets the default rule for animal-caused damages. However, the rule shifts when the owner actively releases or drives the animal, making him responsible. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly assigning liability in cases involving livestock or pets.

Guided Path
1
Memorise the maxim
Learn the Arabic wording: 'جرح العجماء جبار' (Jarh al-‘ajma’ jubar). Its meaning: injury by a dumb animal is unavenged/uncompensated.
‘Ajma’ refers to any animal that cannot speak, typically livestock.
2
Identify the scope
The maxim applies when the animal acts autonomously, without the owner's control or intervention. Harm caused by a freely roaming animal that escapes on its own is not the owner's responsibility.
This includes straying cattle, runaway horses, etc.
3
Recognise the exception
If the owner actively releases or drives the animal (e.g., sends a dog after prey), the act is attributed to him. He becomes liable for any immediate harm caused.
The driver is behind the animal, spurring it on — act transfers to him.
4
Apply the distinction
Given a scenario, determine whether the animal acted on its own (no liability) or was under the owner's active control (liability). Use the criterion of owner's causative involvement.
Example: A cow kicks someone while being milked — owner may be liable because he is engaged with the animal. But if the cow breaks a fence and tramples crops at night, owner is not liable.

Source Text

(23) The injury of a dumb animal (beast) is unavenged (without liability). Translation: The wound of animals, livestock, is wasted (no compensation). Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that if livestock cause harm to someone without the owner's control, it is forgiven, meaning the owner is not liable. The author of al-Hidayah, after mentioning this principle, states: If an animal escapes from its owner on its own and causes harm to someone's property or person, whether at night or day, then the owner [is not liable]... (then a quote) and whoever releases an animal and is its driver, al-Sadr al-Shahid (may Allah have mercy on him) and others said: he meant by 'animal' the dog, and by its being a driver that he is behind it, and it caused harm immediately, i.e., immediately upon release. If it kills a owned game, he is liable because the act transfers to him by means of driving. (al-Binayah, Book of Diyat, Chapter on Injury by Animals and Injury to Them, vol. 13 p. 266) He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Whoever releases an animal and is its driver, and it causes harm immediately, he is liable because he is its impeller, so its act is attributed to him, just as the act of a coerced person is attributed to the coercer in what is suitable as a tool for him. Tabyin al-Haqa’iq, Book of Diyat, Chapter on Injury by Animals and Injury to Them, vol. 6 p. 152. Al-Hidayah, Book of Diyat, Chapter on Injury by Animals and Injury to Them, vol. 4 p. 605.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This lesson presents the maxim 'Injury by a dumb animal is without liability' (jubar). The maxim means that if an animal causes harm without the owner's intervention, the owner is not liable. This is a general rule for cases where the animal acts autonomously. The author then contrasts with the previous lesson: if the owner actively releases or drives the animal, he becomes liable because the act is voluntary and attributed to him. The quote from al-Binayah clarifies that the word 'animal' in that context (releasing) specifically means a dog, and the driver is behind it; immediate harm triggers liability. Thus, the maxim applies only when the animal is not under the owner's control.

Key Terms
جرح العجماء (jurh al-‘ajmā’): Injury caused by a dumb animal (beast) that cannot speak; here specifically livestock.
جبار (jubār): Without liability; the injury is unavenged, meaning no compensation is due.
مالک (mālik): Owner of the animal; his liability depends on his level of control.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under the maxim 'Jarh al-‘ajma’ jubar', when is the owner of a cow not liable if the cow injures someone?
A) If the owner was present but did not stop the cow
B) If the cow escaped from the owner's pen on its own and injured someone
C) If the owner was milking the cow when it kicked someone
D) If the owner deliberately set the cow on someone

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'Jarh al-‘ajma’ jubar' establishes that owners are not liable for harm caused by their animals' autonomous actions, but liability arises when they actively release or drive the animal.

The Principle of Jabar and Its Exception
Ruling with proof: liability for animal damage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for damage caused by an animal, distinguishing between the owner's driving and the animal's spontaneous escape.

Core Idea
The owner is liable only if he actively drives the animal; otherwise, damage is jabar (free of liability).

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on liability for harm caused by animals, based on the hadith 'Jarh al-'Ajma' Jabar' and the exception when the owner is the driver. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying the correct ruling in cases of animal-inflicted damage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what action is being ruled upon (damage by an animal) and what the two possible rulings are: no liability (jabar) when the animal escapes on its own, and liability (daman) when the owner drives it.
The ruling depends on whether the owner was actively involved in causing the animal to act.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof cited in the text: the hadith 'Jarh al-'Ajma' Jabar' (the wound of a dumb animal is free) narrated by Abu Dawood and Ibn Majah.
This hadith establishes the general rule of no liability for spontaneous animal acts.
3
Apply the exception
Note that the text explicitly carves out an exception: if the owner releases the animal and drives it (sa'iq), the act is attributed to him and he is liable for immediate harm.
The key is the owner's active role as driver; mere ownership without action does not trigger liability.
4
Understand the analogy to coercion
Recognize that the exception is supported by qiyas: the driver is analogous to a coercer (mukrih), and the animal to the coerced person, so the act is attributed to the instigator.
This analogy strengthens the exception by showing it aligns with established legal principles.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is as stated in the text: liability only when the owner drives; otherwise no liability.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position; other schools may differ, but Hanafis follow this distinction.

Source Text

اصول ہدایہ رائج
۲۶۷
پر ضمان نہیں آئے گا، کیونکہ یہ چوپایوں کا زخم اور نقصان ہے جو رائیگاں ہے، لہذا ما لک پر کوئی
ضمان نہ ہو گا۔
ولو انفلتت دابة فأصابت مالا أو آدميا ليلا أو نهارا لا ضمان على صاحبها لقوله عَلَيْهِ الصَّلاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ ) أى لِقَوْلِ النَّبِيِّ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ جُرْحُ الْعَجْمَاءِ جبار، الحديث، رواه أبو داود وابن ماجه (البناية: كتاب الديات، باب جناية البهيمة والجناية عليها ، ج ۱۳ ص ۲۶۹،۲۶۸)
The actor's side will be, not the non-actor's side, meaning the actor is liable, not the non-actor. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle and explains that if someone releases an animal, e.g., a dog, and he is its driver, then the animal immediately causes harm to someone, the releaser is liable, because releasing is a voluntary act, and the voluntary act is attributed to the actor. Similarly, in this case, the releasing is attributed to the releaser, and that releaser will be liable. Just as the act of the coerced person is attributed to the coercer when the coerced is suitable as a tool. (23) 'Jarh al-'Ajma' Jabar' (the wound of a dumb animal is free). Translation: The wound of animals, cattle, is free. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that if cattle cause harm to someone without the owner's control, it is forgiven, meaning the owner is not liable. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle and explains that if an animal escapes from the owner on its own and causes harm to someone's property or life, whether night or day, the owner is not liable... And whoever released an animal and was its driver, al-Sadr al-Shahid (may Allah have mercy on him) and others said: He meant by 'animal' the dog, and by 'being its driver' that he was behind it, and it caused harm immediately, i.e., immediately after the release. If it kills owned game, he is liable, because the act transferred to him through the driving. (Al-Binayah: Book of Diyat, Chapter on Injury by Animals and Injury Upon Them, vol. 13 p. 266). He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Whoever released an animal and was its driver, and it caused harm immediately, he is liable, because he is the one who impelled it, so its act is attributed to him, just as the act of the coerced person is attributed to the coercer in what is suitable as a tool for him. (Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: Book of Diyat, Chapter on Injury by Animals and Injury Upon Them, vol. 6 p. 152). (Al-Hidayah: Book of Diyat, Chapter on Injury by Animals and Injury Upon Them, vol. 4 p. 605). Principles of Hidayah, 267: He will not be liable, because this is the wound and harm of animals which is free, so the owner will have no liability. And if an animal escapes and causes harm to property or a person, night or day, there is no liability on its owner, due to his saying (peace and blessings be upon him), i.e., the Prophet's (peace and blessings be upon him) saying: 'Jarh al-'Ajma' Jabar', a hadith, narrated by Abu Dawood and Ibn Majah. (Al-Binayah: Book of Diyat, Chapter on Injury by Animals and Injury Upon Them, vol. 13 pp. 268-269).
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hidayah and its commentaries, addressing liability for damage caused by animals. It presents two scenarios. First, if the owner releases and drives an animal (e.g., a dog) and it immediately causes harm, the owner is liable because his voluntary act of driving is the cause. This is analogous to coercion: the act of the coerced person is attributed to the coercer if the coerced acts as a mere tool. Second, if the animal escapes on its own, without any action by the owner, the owner is not liable based on the hadith 'Jarh al-'Ajma' Jabar' (the wound of a dumb animal is free), reported by Abu Dawood and Ibn Majah. The Hanafi school distinguishes these cases, holding that the owner's active involvement triggers liability, while spontaneous acts of animals do not.

Key Terms
جُبَار (jubar): Without liability, free; here used for damage caused by an animal without owner's action being exempt from compensation.
سائق (sa'iq): Driver; one who drives an animal from behind.
مرسل (mursil): Releaser; one who lets an animal loose.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is the owner liable for damage caused by his animal?
A) If the animal escapes on its own at night
B) If the owner releases and drives the animal and it causes immediate harm
C) Always, because the owner is responsible for his property
D) Never, because the hadith says all animal wounds are free

Key Takeaway
Liability for animal damage under Hanafi law depends on the owner's active driving; spontaneous escape is jabar (free).

Qiyas to Coercion: Reasoning Behind the Driver's Liability
Qiyas explanation for driver's liability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi qiyas that compares an animal driver to a coercer and derive the ruling of liability.

Core Idea
The driver is liable because his act impels the animal, just as the coercer's act impels the coerced.

This lesson focuses on the analogical reasoning (qiyas) used by Hanafi jurists to establish the exception to Jabar. By comparing the driver of an animal to a coercer (mukrih), they derive liability where the owner is the effective cause of the harm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original case (asl)
The case of coercion (ikrah): a person forces another to commit an act under duress. The coercer is held liable for the act because the coerced is like a tool.
The basis is that the true cause is the coercer's will, not the coerced's.
2
Identify the new case (far')
The driver (sa'iq) of an animal: he releases and drives it, and it causes immediate harm.
The immediate harm is key (fi fawriha) to ensure causation.
3
Extract the effective cause (illa)
The effective cause common to both cases: the direct agent (coerced/animal) is not fully independent; the instigator (coercer/driver) is the true cause of the act.
The illa is jaliyyah (clear) – the instigator's role in impelling the act.
4
Apply the ruling of the asl to the far'
Since the illa exists in the driver's case, the ruling of liability is transferred from the coercer to the driver.
Thus, the driver is liable for the animal's harm.
5
Evaluate the validity of the qiyas
This qiyas is valid (sahih) in Hanafi usul because the illa is shared and there is no conflicting evidence.
It is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy), often accepted without objection.

مکرہ (mukrih): Coercer; one who compels another to act under duress.
مکرَہ (mukrah): Coerced person; one who acts under duress.
آلہ (ala): Tool; instrument. In legal context, someone who acts without independent will.

Invalidity of Deferment in Physical Objects (Ta'jil fi al-A'yan)
States the legal maxim that deferment is invalid in physical objects and applies it to the case of a slave's transgression.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle regarding deferment in physical objects and apply it to the case of handing over a slave or paying ransom.

Core Idea
Ta'jil in a'yan is batil; immediate delivery is required because the object is present.

In Hanafi fiqh, deferment (ta'jil) is only permissible for prices (thaman) due to necessity. For physical objects ('ayan), deferring delivery is futile because the object is already present. This principle appears in the chapter of diyat when a master decides to hand over his slave who committed a transgression—he must do so immediately. The same applies to ransom if he chooses to pay instead.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Maxim
State the maxim: 'Al-ta'jil fi al-a'yan batil' (Deferment in physical objects is invalid). Understand its literal meaning.
This is a core Hanafi principle.
2
Understand the Rationale
Explain why: deferment is legislated for acquisition (tahsil). Acquiring something already present (tahsil al-hasil) is impossible and unnecessary.
3
Apply to the Slave Case
The slave who committed a crime (jinayat) is a physical object ('ayn). When the master chooses to hand him over, he must do so immediately; deferment is invalid.
This is the immediate context in Al-Hidayah.
4
Extend to the Ransom
If the master chooses ransom (fidya), it is a substitute for the slave. Hence it takes the same ruling: immediate payment, no deferment.
The ruling applies to substitutes as well.
5
Contrast with Thaman
Note that deferment is valid in thaman (price) due to the buyer's need to acquire funds. This is the exception.
Cross-madhhab: Shafi'is allow deferment in a'yan by agreement.

Source Text

باب جناية الملوك والجناية عليه
(۲۴) اَلتَّأْجِيلُ فِي الْأَعْيَانِ بَاطِلٌ.
ترجمہ: اعیان میں ادھار باطل ہے۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ اعیان یعنی شے موجود میں ادھار جائز نہیں ہے، کیونکہ ادھار ثمن میں ضرورت کی وجہ سے جائز ہے کہ آدمی کے پاس موجودہ وقت میں ثمن نہیں ہوتا بعد میں کما کر دے دے گا لیکن اعیان میں یہ ضرورت نہیں ہے، کیونکہ اعیان تو فی الحال موجود ہوتے ہیں، لہذا عدم ضرورت کی وجہ سے اعیان میں ادھار باطل ہے، اسی طرح جو شے اعیان کا نائب ہو اس کا بھی یہی حکم ہے۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ مولیٰ نے جانی غلام ولی جنایت کو تاوان کے بدلے میں دینے کا فیصلہ کیا تو فی الحال غلام دینا ضروری ہے، اور ادھار باطل ہے، کیونکہ غلام از قبیل اعیان ہے، اور جو چیز از قبیل اعیان ہو اس میں تاجیل باطل ہوتا ہے، لہذا غلام میں بھی ادھار باطل ہوگا، اور اگر غلام کی جگہ فدیہ دینا چاہے تو بھی تاجیل باطل ہے، کیونکہ فدیہ دینا دفع غلام کے قائم مقام ہے، لہذا جب فدیہ دفع عبد کا نائب ہے تو جو حکم دفع عبد کا ہوگا وہی حکم فدیہ کا ہوگا اور دفع عبد فی الفور واجب ہے تو فدیہ بھی فی الفور واجب ہوگا۔
قال فإن دفعه ملكه ولى الجناية فإن دفع المولى العبد الجاني ملكه المجنى عليه وإن فداه فداء بأرش الجناية وكل ذلك يلزمه حالا، أما الدفع فلأن الواجب عند اختياره عين العبد وهو ظاهر، فالتأجيل في الأعيان باطل لأن التأجيل شرع للتحصيل ترفها وتحصيل الحاصل باطل، وأما الفداء فلأنه لما جعل بدلا عن العبد في الشرع قام مقامه ولهذا سمى فداء فيأخذ حكمه. (العناية: كتاب الديات، باب جناية المملوك والجناية عليه، ج ۱۰ ص ۳۴۰) (قال) أى القدورى: (فإن دفعه ملكه ولى الجناية أى فإن دفع المولى العبد في جناية خطأ ملكه أى ملك العبد ولى الجناية وسقطت المطالبة عن المولى وإن فداه فداه بأرشها أى بأرش الجناية وكل ذلك أى الدفع والفداء (يلزمه حالا) أى يلزم المولى على وجه الحلول (أما الدفع فلأن التأجيل في الأعيان باطل لأن التأجيل إنما شرع للتحصيل وتحصيل الحاصل محال (وعند اختياره) أى اختيار المولى الدفع الواجب عين) أى عين العبد (وأما الفداء ) عطف على قوله أما الدفع (فلأنه) أى فلأن الفداء (جعل بدلا عن العبد في الشرع فكان قائما مقامه فإذا كان كذلك يأخذ حكمه فلم يصح التأجيل كما لا يصح في الدفع. (البناية: كتاب الديات، باب جناية المملوك والجناية عليه، ج ۱۳ ص ۲۷۹، ۲۸۰) الهداية: كتاب الديات، جناية المملوك والجناية عليه، ج۴ ص ٦١١
Chapter: Crimes of slaves and crimes against them. (24) Deferment in physical objects is invalid. Translation: Deferment in physical objects is invalid. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that deferment in physical objects, i.e., existing items, is not permissible because deferment is permissible in price due to necessity: a person may not have the price at the present time and will earn it later. But in physical objects this necessity is absent because physical objects are present immediately. Therefore, due to lack of necessity, deferment in physical objects is invalid. Similarly, anything that stands in place of a physical object has the same ruling. The author of Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, is indicating that when the master decides to give the slave who committed the transgression to the guardian of the victim in exchange for the compensation, the slave must be given immediately, and deferment is invalid because the slave is of the category of physical objects. And with regard to ransom, it is also invalid because the ransom stands in place of handing over the slave. So when the ransom is the substitute for handing over the slave, the same ruling that applies to handing over the slave applies to the ransom: handing over the slave is obligatory immediately, so the ransom is also obligatory immediately. He said: If he hands him over, he becomes the property of the guardian of the transgression. If the master hands over the slave who committed the crime, he becomes the property of the victim's guardian, and if he ransoms him, he ransoms him with the blood-money. All of that is binding upon him immediately. As for handing over, because the obligatory at his choice is the slave himself, and that is apparent, so deferment in physical objects is invalid because deferment was legislated for acquisition as a luxury, and acquiring what is already acquired is invalid. As for the ransom, because when it was made a substitute for the slave in the law, it stood in his place and therefore is called a ransom, so it takes his ruling. (Al-'Inayah: Kitab al-Diyat, Bab Janayat al-Mamluk, vol. 10 p. 340) He said: i.e., Al-Quduri: (If he hands him over, he becomes the property of the guardian of the transgression) i.e., if the master hands over the slave for a wrong transgression, he becomes the property of the slave to the guardian of the transgression, and the claim falls from the master. And if he ransoms him, he ransoms him with its blood-money, i.e., with the blood-money of the transgression. All of that, i.e., the handing over and the ransom, is binding upon him immediately, i.e., binding upon the master on a prompt basis. As for handing over, because deferment in physical objects is invalid because deferment is only legislated for acquisition and acquiring what is already acquired is impossible. And when he chooses handing over, the obligatory is the slave himself. And as for the ransom, it is a substitute for the slave in the law, so it stands in his place, and if so, it takes his ruling, so deferment is not valid just as it is not valid in handing over. (Al-Binayah: Kitab al-Diyat, Bab Janayat al-Mamluk, vol. 13 pp. 279-280) Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Diyat, Janayat al-Mamluk, vol. 4 p. 611
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage presents a key legal principle from the Hanafi school: deferment (ta'jil) is invalid in physical objects ('ayan). The principle is stated as a maxim and then explained. The commentary clarifies that deferment is only valid in prices (thaman) due to necessity—the buyer may not have the price at hand. In contrast, physical objects are present and can be delivered immediately, so deferring delivery is futile. The author then applies this to the case of a slave who commits a transgression: the master must hand over the slave immediately; deferment is invalid. The same applies to the ransom (fidyah) because it substitutes for the slave. The text includes extensive quotations from Al-'Inayah and Al-Binayah, showing that the principle is well-established. The reasoning is based on the idea that deferment is meant to facilitate acquisition; when the object is already present, there is no need.

Key Terms
التأجيل (al-ta'jil): Deferment or postponement of delivery or payment to a future date.
الاعيان (al-a'yan): Physical objects or specific commodities that exist and are present.
الفداء (al-fidya'): Ransom paid to free a slave from liability for a crime.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle mentioned, why is deferment (ta'jil) invalid in physical objects ('ayan)?
A) Because the Prophet forbade deferment in all transactions.
B) Because physical objects are present immediately, so there is no need for deferment.
C) Because deferment changes the ownership rights.
D) Because physical objects are subject to price fluctuation.

Key Takeaway
Deferment in physical objects is invalid because they are present and can be delivered immediately; this principle applies to both the object and its substitute.

Immediate Obligation of Daf‘ and Fidā’ for a Slave's Crime
Stating the immediate obligation of daf and fida for a slave's crime.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that both handing over (daf‘) and ransom (fidā’) for a slave's crime are immediately obligatory and explain the two proofs: the impossibility of deferring a specific object and the substitute nature of ransom.

Core Idea
The master's obligation for a slave's crime is immediate—neither handing over nor ransom can be deferred.

When a slave commits a crime, the master has two options: hand over the slave or pay ransom. This lesson examines why both options must be executed immediately, without delay. The source text from Al-Hidayah and its commentaries builds the proof on the nature of obligations tied to specific objects and the principle of substitution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two options
The master may either hand over the slave (daf‘) or pay ransom (fidā’). Both are presented as alternatives.
The choice is the master's, but the timing is not.
2
Note the immediate obligation
Both daf‘ and fidā’ are binding immediately (ḥāl), not deferred.
The text says 'كل ذلك يلزمه حالا' – all of that is binding immediately.
3
Analyze the proof for daf‘
The obligation when choosing to hand over is the very person of the slave (ʿayn al-ʿabd). Deferment in specific objects is invalid because deferment is legislated for facilitation, and obtaining what is already obtained is impossible.
التأجيل في الأعيان باطل لأن التأجيل شرع للتحصيل ترفها وتحصيل الحاصل باطل
4
Analyze the proof for fidā’
Ransom is made a substitute (badal) for the slave in the law, so it stands in his place and takes the same ruling: immediate obligation.
لما جعل بدلا عن العبد في الشرع قام مقامه ولهذا سمي فداء فيأخذ حكمه
5
Connect to the principle
The underlying logic is that deferment is for ease of collection; when the object (or its substitute) is already present, deferment is meaningless.

(البناية: كتاب الديات، باب جناية المملوك والجناية عليه، ج ۱۳ ص ۲۷۹، ۲۸۰) الهداية: كتاب الديات، جناية المملوك والجناية عليه، ج۴ ص ٦١١

قال (وإذا استدانت الأمة المأذون لها ثم ولدت) فرق بين ولادة الأمة بعد استدانتهـا وبـيـن ولادتها بعد جنايتها في أن الولد يباع معها في الأولى دون الثانية، فإن الدين وصف حكمى فيها واجب في ذمتها متعلق برقبتها استيفاء حتى صار المولى ممنوعا من التصرف فى رقبتها ببيع أو هبة أو غيرهما فكانت من الأوصاف الشرعية القارة فى الأم فتسرى إلى الولد كالكتابة والتدبير والرهن. وأما موجب الجناية فالدفع أو الفداء وذلك في ذمة المولى لا في ذمتها حتى لا يـصـيـر الـمـولـى ممنوعا من التصرف في رقبتها ببيع أو هبة أو استخدام ..... وقوله ( والسراية فى الأوصاف الشرعية دون الأوصاف الحقيقية) بناء على أن الوصف الحقيقى فى محل لا يمكن أن ينتقل إلى غيره، وأما الوصف الشرعي فهو أمر اعتبارى يتحول بتحوله العناية: كتاب الديات
باب جناية المملوك والجناية عليه، ج ۱۰ ص ۳۴۸)
Usool e Hidayah, page 268: Whatever ruling applies to handing over the slave, the same ruling applies to the ransom; and handing over the slave is immediately obligatory, so the ransom is also immediately obligatory. (65) As-sarāyatu fī al-awṣāfi al-sharʿiyyati dūna al-awṣāfi al-ḥaqīqiyyati. Translation: Transfer (saraya) can occur in sharʿi attributes, not in real attributes. He (al-Quduri) said: 'If he hands him over (the slave), the owner of the crime takes possession;' meaning if the master hands over the slave who committed the crime, the victim takes possession of the slave, and if he ransoms him, he ransoms him with the blood-money (arash) of the crime. All of that is binding immediately. As for handing over, because the obligation when he chooses is the very person of the slave, and that is apparent; deferment in specific objects is invalid, because deferment is legislated for facilitation, and obtaining that which is already obtained is impossible. As for the ransom, because it is made a substitute for the slave in the law, it stands in his place, and therefore it is called ransom, so it takes his ruling. (Al-ʿInayah: Book of Diyat, Chapter on Crime of the Slave, vol. 10 p. 340) (He said, i.e., al-Quduri:) 'If he hands him over, the owner of the crime takes possession;' i.e., if the master hands over the slave in a crime of error, the victim takes possession of the slave, and the claim against the master drops; and if he ransoms him, he ransoms him with its arash, i.e., the arash of the crime; and all that, i.e., handing over and ransom, is binding immediately, i.e., binding upon the master as a present obligation. As for handing over, because deferment in specific objects is invalid, because deferment is only legislated for facilitation, and obtaining that which is already obtained is impossible. And when he chooses, i.e., the master chooses to hand over, the obligation is the very person of the slave. As for the ransom, it is connected to his statement 'as for handing over' because the ransom is made a substitute for the slave in the law, so it stands in his place; when that is the case, it takes his ruling, so deferment is not valid just as it is not valid in handing over. (Al-Binayah: Book of Diyat, Chapter on Crime of the Slave, vol. 13 pp. 279-280) Al-Hidayah: Book of Diyat, Crime of the Slave, vol. 4 p. 611. Page 269: Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that in sharʿi attributes, i.e., that which is established in the dhimma (liability) by revelation, like debt, loss and penalty transfer (saraya) juristically; but in real attributes, saraya cannot be agreed upon. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if a female slave authorized for trade took a loan exceeding her value and then gave birth, the child will also be sold with her for the debt, because debt is a sharʿi attribute established in her dhimma, attached to her neck for recovery, even to the extent that the master is prevented from disposing of her neck by sale, gift, or otherwise, so it is from the sharʿi attributes that are settled in the mother and thus transfer to the child, like kitabah, tadbir, and rahn. But if the female slave committed a crime and then gave birth, the child is not given in compensation, because the crime is from the real attributes, and saraya is not established in it. He (al-Hidayah) said: 'If a female slave authorized for trade incurs debt and then gives birth,' the difference between the female slave giving birth after incurring debt and after committing a crime is that the child is sold with her in the first case but not in the second. For debt is a sharʿi attribute in her, established in her dhimma, attached to her neck for recovery, to the extent that the master is prevented from disposing of her neck by sale, gift, or otherwise, so it is from the sharʿi attributes that are settled in the mother and thus transfer to the child, like kitabah, tadbir, and rahn. As for the consequence of crime, it is handing over or ransom, and that is in the master's dhimma, not in hers, so much so that the master is not prevented from disposing of her neck by sale, gift, or use... And his statement 'Saraya occurs in sharʿi attributes, not in real attributes' is based on the fact that a real attribute in a subject cannot transfer to another, while a sharʿi attribute is a conceptual matter that transforms as the subject transforms. (Al-ʿInayah: Book of Diyat, Chapter on Crime of the Slave, vol. 10 p. 348)
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage discusses a key Hanafi legal maxim: 'Transfer of attributes occurs in sharʿi attributes, not in real attributes.' The term 'saraya' refers to the extension of a legal obligation or attribute from a primary subject to something connected to it, such as a child born to a female slave. The text clarifies that this transfer only applies to sharʿi attributes—obligations established by the Law, like debt, which are conceptual and can be deemed to attach to the slave's neck (raqaba)—not to real attributes like the physical act of a crime. The author first explains the immediate obligation of handing over a slave or paying ransom for a crime: the master must choose one and it must be done without delay because the obligation is on a specific object (the slave) or its substitute (ransom) that stands in its place; deferment is invalid. Then, the principle of saraya is invoked to distinguish between two cases involving a female slave who gives birth: if she incurred debt, the child is sold with her because debt is a sharʿi attribute that transfers to the child; but if she committed a crime, the child is not given in compensation, because the obligation arising from the crime (handing over or ransom) is on the master, not on the slave, and the crime itself is a real attribute that does not transfer. Examples given include kitabah (contract of manumission), tadbir (postponed emancipation), and rahn (pledge), which also transfer to the child.

Key Terms
دفع (dafʿ): Handing over of the offending slave to the victim or his heirs as compensation for the crime.
فداء (fidāʾ): Ransom paid by the master in lieu of handing over the slave; equal to the arash (blood-money) of the crime.
أرش (arsh): Blood-money or monetary compensation for injury, synonymous with diyah in this context.
حلول (ḥulūl): Immediate obligation, as opposed to deferred (mu’ajjal).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this lesson, what is the obligation when a master chooses to ransom his slave?
A) He must pay the ransom immediately.
B) He may delay payment up to three years.
C) He may delay until the slave is freed.
D) He does not need to pay if the victim agrees to wait.

Key Takeaway
The master's obligation for a slave's crime is immediate: handing over the slave or paying ransom cannot be deferred because the obligation is on a specific object or its substitute.

The Principle of Saraya in Shar‘ī vs. Real Attributes
Explaining the Hanafi principle of saraya with two key applications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of saraya to determine whether a legal attribute (like debt) transfers to a child born to a female slave, and distinguish it from a real attribute (like crime) which does not transfer.

Core Idea
Shar‘ī attributes transfer to a child; real attributes do not—debt sells the child, crime does not.

The Hanafi school recognizes that certain legal obligations are 'conceptual' (shar‘ī) and can extend from a mother to her child, while physical acts (ḥaqīqī) remain with the doer. This lesson, drawn from Al-Hidayah, explains the maxim 'Saraya occurs in shar‘ī attributes, not in real attributes' and its application to two key cases: a female slave who incurs debt and one who commits a crime.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
The principle: al-sarāyatu fī al-awṣāfi al-sharʿiyyati dūna al-awṣāfi al-ḥaqīqiyyati.
Saraya means the legal extension of an attribute from the original subject to a connected entity.
2
Define shar‘ī attribute
A conceptual attribute established by law, like debt, which exists in the dhimma and attaches to the slave's person (raqaba). It is 'settled' (qārra) and cannot be removed except by fulfillment.
Examples: debt, kitabah, tadbir, rahn.
3
Define real attribute
A physical action, like committing a crime, which is a real attribute inherent to the subject. It cannot transfer to another.
The text says: 'الوصف الحقيقى فى محل لا يمكن أن ينتقل إلى غيره'
4
Apply to debt case
If a female slave authorized for trade incurs debt and then gives birth, the child is sold with her. The debt attribute transfers to the child because debt is shar‘ī.
The child is sold to satisfy the debt.
5
Apply to crime case
If she commits a crime and then gives birth, the child is not given in compensation. The crime is a real attribute; the obligation (handing over or ransom) is on the master, not the slave, and does not transfer to the child.
The text says: 'موجب الجناية فالدفع أو الفداء وذلك في ذمة المولى لا في ذمتها'

Source Text

قال (وإذا استدانت الأمة المأذون لها ثم ولدت) فرق بين ولادة الأمة بعد استدانتهـا وبـيـن ولادتها بعد جنايتها في أن الولد يباع معها في الأولى دون الثانية، فإن الدين وصف حكمى فيها واجب في ذمتها متعلق برقبتها استيفاء حتى صار المولى ممنوعا من التصرف فى رقبتها ببيع أو هبة أو غيرهما فكانت من الأوصاف الشرعية القارة فى الأم فتسرى إلى الولد كالكتابة والتدبير والرهن. وأما موجب الجناية فالدفع أو الفداء وذلك في ذمة المولى لا في ذمتها حتى لا يـصـيـر الـمـولـى ممنوعا من التصرف في رقبتها ببيع أو هبة أو استخدام ..... وقوله ( والسراية فى الأوصاف الشرعية دون الأوصاف الحقيقية) بناء على أن الوصف الحقيقى فى محل لا يمكن أن ينتقل إلى غيره، وأما الوصف الشرعي فهو أمر اعتبارى يتحول بتحوله العناية: كتاب الديات
باب جناية المملوك والجناية عليه، ج ۱۰ ص ۳۴۸)
Usool e Hidayah, page 268: Whatever ruling applies to handing over the slave, the same ruling applies to the ransom; and handing over the slave is immediately obligatory, so the ransom is also immediately obligatory. (65) As-sarāyatu fī al-awṣāfi al-sharʿiyyati dūna al-awṣāfi al-ḥaqīqiyyati. Translation: Transfer (saraya) can occur in sharʿi attributes, not in real attributes. He (al-Quduri) said: 'If he hands him over (the slave), the owner of the crime takes possession;' meaning if the master hands over the slave who committed the crime, the victim takes possession of the slave, and if he ransoms him, he ransoms him with the blood-money (arash) of the crime. All of that is binding immediately. As for handing over, because the obligation when he chooses is the very person of the slave, and that is apparent; deferment in specific objects is invalid, because deferment is legislated for facilitation, and obtaining that which is already obtained is impossible. As for the ransom, because it is made a substitute for the slave in the law, it stands in his place, and therefore it is called ransom, so it takes his ruling. (Al-ʿInayah: Book of Diyat, Chapter on Crime of the Slave, vol. 10 p. 340) (He said, i.e., al-Quduri:) 'If he hands him over, the owner of the crime takes possession;' i.e., if the master hands over the slave in a crime of error, the victim takes possession of the slave, and the claim against the master drops; and if he ransoms him, he ransoms him with its arash, i.e., the arash of the crime; and all that, i.e., handing over and ransom, is binding immediately, i.e., binding upon the master as a present obligation. As for handing over, because deferment in specific objects is invalid, because deferment is only legislated for facilitation, and obtaining that which is already obtained is impossible. And when he chooses, i.e., the master chooses to hand over, the obligation is the very person of the slave. As for the ransom, it is connected to his statement 'as for handing over' because the ransom is made a substitute for the slave in the law, so it stands in his place; when that is the case, it takes his ruling, so deferment is not valid just as it is not valid in handing over. (Al-Binayah: Book of Diyat, Chapter on Crime of the Slave, vol. 13 pp. 279-280) Al-Hidayah: Book of Diyat, Crime of the Slave, vol. 4 p. 611. Page 269: Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that in sharʿi attributes, i.e., that which is established in the dhimma (liability) by revelation, like debt, loss and penalty transfer (saraya) juristically; but in real attributes, saraya cannot be agreed upon. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if a female slave authorized for trade took a loan exceeding her value and then gave birth, the child will also be sold with her for the debt, because debt is a sharʿi attribute established in her dhimma, attached to her neck for recovery, even to the extent that the master is prevented from disposing of her neck by sale, gift, or otherwise, so it is from the sharʿi attributes that are settled in the mother and thus transfer to the child, like kitabah, tadbir, and rahn. But if the female slave committed a crime and then gave birth, the child is not given in compensation, because the crime is from the real attributes, and saraya is not established in it. He (al-Hidayah) said: 'If a female slave authorized for trade incurs debt and then gives birth,' the difference between the female slave giving birth after incurring debt and after committing a crime is that the child is sold with her in the first case but not in the second. For debt is a sharʿi attribute in her, established in her dhimma, attached to her neck for recovery, to the extent that the master is prevented from disposing of her neck by sale, gift, or otherwise, so it is from the sharʿi attributes that are settled in the mother and thus transfer to the child, like kitabah, tadbir, and rahn. As for the consequence of crime, it is handing over or ransom, and that is in the master's dhimma, not in hers, so much so that the master is not prevented from disposing of her neck by sale, gift, or use... And his statement 'Saraya occurs in sharʿi attributes, not in real attributes' is based on the fact that a real attribute in a subject cannot transfer to another, while a sharʿi attribute is a conceptual matter that transforms as the subject transforms. (Al-ʿInayah: Book of Diyat, Chapter on Crime of the Slave, vol. 10 p. 348)
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage discusses a key Hanafi legal maxim: 'Transfer of attributes occurs in sharʿi attributes, not in real attributes.' The term 'saraya' refers to the extension of a legal obligation or attribute from a primary subject to something connected to it, such as a child born to a female slave. The text clarifies that this transfer only applies to sharʿi attributes—obligations established by the Law, like debt, which are conceptual and can be deemed to attach to the slave's neck (raqaba)—not to real attributes like the physical act of a crime. The author first explains the immediate obligation of handing over a slave or paying ransom for a crime: the master must choose one and it must be done without delay because the obligation is on a specific object (the slave) or its substitute (ransom) that stands in its place; deferment is invalid. Then, the principle of saraya is invoked to distinguish between two cases involving a female slave who gives birth: if she incurred debt, the child is sold with her because debt is a sharʿi attribute that transfers to the child; but if she committed a crime, the child is not given in compensation, because the obligation arising from the crime (handing over or ransom) is on the master, not on the slave, and the crime itself is a real attribute that does not transfer. Examples given include kitabah (contract of manumission), tadbir (postponed emancipation), and rahn (pledge), which also transfer to the child.

Key Terms
سراية (sarāyah): The legal extension or transfer of an attribute or obligation from a primary subject to a connected entity.
أوصاف شرعية (awṣāf sharʿiyyah): Attributes established by the Law, such as debt, which are conceptual and exist in the dhimma.
أوصاف حقيقية (awṣāf ḥaqīqiyyah): Physical or real attributes, such as the act of a crime, which are inherent and cannot transfer.
ذمة (dhimma): Legal liability or responsibility; the 'person' of legal obligation.
رقبة (raqaba): The 'neck' of a slave, representing his or her person or value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle of saraya, which of the following attributes transfers from a mother slave to her child?
A) Debt
B) Crime
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'Saraya in shar‘i attributes, not real ones' means that conceptual legal obligations like debt transfer to a child, but physical acts like crime do not.

Ghasb Cuts Off the Causal Link (Sarāyah) in Death from Injury
Principle that ghasb severs causation from initial injury to death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that ghasb severs the causal link (sarāyah) and apply it to determine liability in cases of injury followed by usurpation.

Core Idea
When a usurper takes possession after an injury, he assumes liability for death from that injury because ghasb creates retroactive ownership.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi legal maxim: 'Al-ghasbu qāṭiʿ li-l-sarāyah' (Usurpation cuts off the causal link). The principle addresses a scenario where one person inflicts a wound, and later another person usurps the victim before the wound proves fatal. Who bears liability for the death? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced answer grounded in the nature of ghasb as a cause of ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Maxim and Its Context
Read the Arabic maxim 'الْغَصْبُ قَاطِع لِلسَّرَايَةِ' and its translation. Understand that the lesson is set in the chapter on diyat (blood money) and discusses liability when a wound is followed by usurpation (ghasb) and then death.
The maxim is a ruling with an embedded proof.
2
Distinguish the Two Scenarios
Examine the two cases from al-Jami' al-Saghir: (1) Master cuts slave's hand, then slave is usurped and dies from the cut in the usurper's hands—usurper liable. (2) Master cuts slave's hand while the slave is already in the usurper's possession—usurper not liable. Identify the difference: the timing of the cut relative to the usurpation.
The first scenario triggers the maxim; the second does not because the cut occurred during the original owner's control.
3
Analyse the Proof: Why Ghasb is a Cause of Ownership
The text argues that ghasb cuts off sarāyah because ghasb is a cause of ownership (sabab al-milk). In Hanafi law, when a usurper pays compensation (ḍamān), the misappropriated item is owned by the usurper retroactively from the start of the ghasb. Thus, the victim becomes the usurper's property, making the usurper liable for his death.
This is a rational proof (dalīl ʿaqlī) based on analogy (qiyas) and istihsan.
4
Apply the Principle to Determine Liability
Apply the maxim to the first scenario: Since the usurper took possession after the injury, the causal chain is broken. The death is attributed to the usurper, who must pay the value of the slave (as a person with a missing hand). The original cutter is absolved.
The liability is full value, not just the hand, because the death occurred under the usurper's ownership.
5
Note the Intra-Hanafi Authority
The ruling is attributed to Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir (Zahir al-Riwayah). It is the relied-upon position (muftā bihi) in the Hanafi school. Other schools may differ on this principle.
This is a classical Hanafi stance, not necessarily shared by all madhhabs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب غصب العبد والمدبر والصبي والجناية في ذلك 
(۶۶) الْغَصْبُ قَاطِع لِلسَّرَايَةِ. •
ترجمہ: نصب سرایت کو ختم کر دیتا ہے۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ فعل غصب ایسا فعل ہے جو قتل کی طرف زخم کے سرایت کرنے کوختم کر دیتا ہے، یعنی زخم کے بعد اگر وہ آدمی مر جائے تو قتل کی نسبت غاصب کی طرف ہوگی نہ کہ قاطع ید کی طرف، کیونکہ قطع ید جو مفضی الی القتل تھا اب غصب سے وہ نسبت ختم ہو گئی۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ ایک آدمی نے دوسرے کا ہاتھ کا ٹا ، پھر اس مقطوع الید کو کسی نے غصب کیا اور وہ غاصب کے ہاتھ میں مر گیا، تو اس کی قیمت غاصب پر ہوگی نہ کہ قاطع ید پر، کیونکہ غصب سے سرایت ختم ہو جاتی ہے، جب سرایت کو غصب نے ختم کر دیا تو موت کی نسبت اب قاطع کی طرف نہیں ہوگی، بلکہ غاصب کی طرف ہوگی اور اس پر تاوان آئے گا اور وہ تاوان میں مقطوع الید والی قیمت ادا کرے گا۔ الهداية كتاب الديات، باب غصب العبد والمدبر والصبي والجناية في ذلك، ج ۴ ص ۶۱۸
(قال) أى محمد فى الجامع الصغير ومن قطع يد عبده ثم غصبه رجل ومات في يده من القطع فعليه قيمته أقطع، وإن كان المولى قطع يده في يد الغاصب فمات من ذلك في يد الغاصب لا شيء عليه، والفرق بين المسألتين (أن الغصب قاطع للسراية، لأنه أى لأن الغصب سبب الملك لأن المضمونات تملك عند أداء الضمان مستندا إلى أول الغصب ولما كان الغصب من أسباب الملك واقع اصول ہوا یہ رائج
Chapter: Usurpation of the slave, the mudabbir, the minor, and the crime therein. (66) Ghasb cuts off the causal link (sarāyah). Translation: It cuts off the transmission of the injury. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that the act of ghasb is an act that removes the transmission of the wound towards killing. That is, after the wound if that person dies, then the attribution of the killing will be to the usurper, not to the one who cut the limb. Because the severing of the limb which would lead to death, its causal link has now been removed by ghasb. The author of al-Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, explains that if a person cuts another's hand, then someone else usurps the one whose hand was cut, and he dies in the hand of the usurper from the cut, then the price of the hand is due on the usurper, not on the cutter. Because ghasb cuts off the causal link. When the causal link is cut by ghasb, the attribution of death is now not to the cutter, but to the usurper, and compensation is due on him, and he will pay the value of the one whose hand was cut. Al-Hidayah, book of diyat, chapter on ghasb of slave, mudabbir, minor, etc., vol.4 p.618. (He said) meaning Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir: Whoever cuts the hand of his slave, then another man usurps him and he dies in his (the usurper's) hand from the cut, then upon him (the usurper) is the value of the slave as one with a missing hand. And if the master cuts his hand in the hand of the usurper, and he dies from that in the hand of the usurper, nothing is upon him. The difference between the two cases is that ghasb cuts off the causal link. Because ghasb is a cause of ownership, since guaranteed things become owned at the time of payment of guarantee, tracing back to the beginning of the ghasb. And since ghasb is among the causes of ownership, this principle is established.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This lesson presents a key Hanafi legal maxim: 'Al-ghasbu qāṭiʿ li-l-sarāyah' (Usurpation cuts off the causal link). The passage is a ruling with an embedded proof, typical of the Hidayah's style. The principle means that when one person inflicts a wound on another (e.g., cutting off a hand), which would normally lead to death (sarāyah), and then a second person usurps (ghasb) the victim, the causal chain from the original injury to the death is broken. Consequently, if the victim dies while in the usurper's custody, the death is attributed to the usurper, not the original cutter. This is because the usurper has taken possession of the victim, making the victim's life and death his responsibility. The proof lies in the legal nature of ghasb: it is a cause of ownership (sabab al-milk) because, in Hanafi law, when someone guarantees (ḍamān) a misappropriated item, they effectively own it retroactively from the moment of ghasb. Thus, the usurper becomes responsible for the victim's death. The text then contrasts two scenarios from al-Jami' al-Saghir by Imam Muhammad: 1) A master cuts his slave's hand, then someone usurps the slave. The slave dies in the usurper's hands from the cut. The usurper must pay the value of the slave (as a person with a missing hand, i.e., less his hand's value). The cutter is not liable because ghasb severed the causal link. 2) If the master cuts the slave's hand while the slave is already in the usurper's custody (i.e., the usurper had already taken possession), and the slave dies from the cut, then the usurper is not liable because the causal link remains with the master. The cut happened during the master's ownership, and the usurper's possession does not break it because the cut was not a consequence of the ghasb. This illustrates the nuance: ghasb breaks the chain only when it occurs after the injury and the victim is removed from the owner's possession. The key is that ghasb is a cause of ownership, establishing liability on the usurper for consequences arising during his possession.

Key Terms
الْغَصْبُ (al-ghasb): Usurpation; taking another's property without right.
السراية (al-sarāyah): The causal link between a wound and subsequent death; the progression of the injury towards death.
قاطع (qāṭiʿ): That which severs; here, the act of ghasb severs the causal chain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master cuts his slave's hand, then a thief usurps the slave, and the slave dies from the cut while in the thief's possession, who is liable for the slave's value?
A) The master, because he cut the hand.
B) The thief, because ghasb severs the causal link.
C) Both are equally liable.
D) Neither, because it was an accident.

Key Takeaway
Ghasb severs the causal link (sarāyah), making the usurper liable for death resulting from a prior injury, based on the principle that ghasb is a cause of retroactive ownership.

The Interdicted Slave is Liable for His Actions
States a legal maxim on slave's liability for actions despite interdiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim regarding an interdicted slave's liability and apply it to cases of ghasb and other torts.

Core Idea
Interdiction (hajr) blocks verbal transactions but not physical actions; thus the slave is liable for his torts.

This lesson introduces a foundational Hanafi principle: a slave who is interdicted from trade (mahjur alayhi) remains fully accountable for his physical actions. The maxim clarifies that interdiction only restricts verbal capacity, not tortious liability. We will explore its application to ghasb (usurpation) and other acts, drawing on classical Hanafi texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maxim and Its Key Terms
Read the Arabic maxim 'al-mahjur alayhi mu'akhadh bi af'alih'. Understand that 'mahjur alayhi' is a slave whose master has restricted him from transactions. The maxim states he is held accountable for his actions.
Memorize the maxim as a key Hanafi qawida.
2
Understand the Scope of Hajr
Hajr only applies to statements (aqwal) like sales and purchases, not to physical actions (af'al). Thus, the slave's bodily acts are legally effective for liability.
This is the core distinction: the slave's actions create daman, but his statements regarding financial obligations are suspended until freedom.
3
Apply the Maxim to Ghasb
Consider the case: an interdicted slave usurps (ghasb) another interdicted slave, and the latter dies in his possession. The usurper is liable (damin) because the act is a physical wrong, not a verbal transaction.
Use the maxim to reason: the act of ghasb is an action, so hajr does not prevent liability.
4
Distinguish Between Apparent and Confessed Usurpation
If usurpation is proven by clear evidence (apparent), the slave is liable immediately and can be sold to pay compensation. If proven only by his confession, liability is deferred until emancipation.
The distinction arises because confession is a statement (qawl), which is subject to hajr.
5
Note the Exception for Hudud and Qisas
If the slave confesses to a hadd or qisas, it is binding immediately because these corporal punishments are treated as bodily matters, similar to actions.
This follows from the principle that the slave's body is not fully owned by the master in such matters.

Source Text

The interdicted slave is held accountable for his actions. Translation: The slave upon whom interdiction is placed will be held accountable for his actions. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that the slave upon whom the master has placed restriction and prohibition in trade, if he causes harm to someone, then compensation will be imposed upon him, because hajr (interdiction) only pertains to statements, for example sale and purchase; it does not pertain to actions. The author of al-Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, is explaining that if a slave who is interdicted usurps (ghasb) another interdicted slave and that slave dies in his possession, then this usurping interdicted slave will be liable (damin), because in the aforementioned situation, the action of the interdicted slave is actual usurpation, and the interdicted slave is held accountable for his actions. Therefore, in this situation too, the usurping interdicted slave will be liable. "Kana muhallil al-ghasb bayna al-jinayah wa al-sarayah qati'an li al-sarayah" (al-Binayah: Book of Diyat, Chapter on Usurpation of Slave, Mudabbar, Child, and Injury in That, vol. 13 p. 316); al-Hidayah: Book of Diyat, Chapter on Usurpation of Slave, Mudabbar, Child, and Injury in That, vol. 4 p. 618. And his statement: "And when he usurps the interdicted slave" is clear. And his statement "held accountable for his actions" means in his state of slavery. As for his statements, if it is something that necessitates hudud (fixed punishments) or qisas (retaliation), then it is similar (i.e., he is held accountable). But if it is something that necessitates financial liability, then he is not held accountable in his slavery; rather, he will only be held accountable after emancipation. (al-Inayah: Book of Diyat, Chapter on Usurpation of Slave, Mudabbar, Child, and Injury in That, vol. 10 p. 367). He (i.e., Muhammad) said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: "If a slave who is interdicted usurps another interdicted slave and the latter dies in his hand, then he is liable. This is if the usurpation is apparent, in which case he is liable immediately and can be sold for it, because the actions of a slave are considered. But if the usurpation became apparent through his confession, it is not obligatory except upon emancipation. Thus said the jurist Abu al-Layth, may Allah have mercy on him, and that is because slavery necessitates interdiction in statements but not in actions. And if the interdicted slave confesses to a hadd (fixed punishment) or qisas (retaliation), it is binding upon him immediately, because in that matter he remains upon the original principle of freedom. (Because the interdicted slave is held accountable for his actions) i.e., in his state of slavery. (al-Binayah: Book of Diyat, Chapter on Usurpation of Slave, Mudabbar, Child, and Injury in That, vol. 13 p. 317); al-Hidayah: Book of Diyat, Chapter on Usurpation of Slave, Mudabbar, Child, and Injury in That, vol. 4 p. 625.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents a legal maxim (qawida) from the Hanafi school: "The interdicted slave is held accountable for his actions." The commentary opens by explaining that the term 'mahjur alayhi' refers to a slave whom the master has restricted from engaging in trade. The key point is that the interdiction (hajr) only applies to verbal transactions (aqwal) like buying and selling; it does not restrict the slave's physical actions (af'al). Therefore, if such a slave causes damage through his actions, he remains liable for compensation. The author applies this maxim to a specific case: if an interdicted slave usurps (ghasb) another interdicted slave and the usurped slave dies in his possession, the usurper is liable (damin) because his act of usurpation is a physical action. The text then cites numerous sources to support this principle. Al-Binayah and al-Hidayah are referenced for the general principle. Al-Inayah specifies that for statements, the slave is held accountable if it involves hudud or qisas (because these are corporal punishments that can be executed on the slave's body), but for financial liabilities arising from statements (like debts from sales), the slave is not held accountable until after emancipation, because slavery prevents ownership of property. The passage also quotes from al-Jami' al-Saghir by Imam Muhammad, distinguishing between apparent usurpation (where the slave is liable immediately and can be sold to satisfy the claim) and usurpation proven by confession (where liability is deferred until emancipation). Finally, the opinion of Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi is cited: if the slave confesses to a hadd or qisas, it is binding immediately because in those matters the slave retains the original status of freedom (i.e., the body is not subject to the master's property right in the same way). All these citations reinforce the core maxim that actions of the interdicted slave are legally effective for liability purposes.

Key Terms
محجور عليه (maḥjūr ʿalayhi): The interdicted slave, i.e., a slave whom the master has legally restricted from engaging in transactions (buying, selling, etc.)
حجر (ḥajr): Interdiction; a legal restriction preventing someone from executing financial transactions due to incapacity or status (e.g., slavery, insanity, minority)
غصب (ghaṣb): Usurpation; taking another's property wrongfully and forcefully, with the intention of ownership or control
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability; the obligation to compensate for loss or damage caused
مواخذة (muʾākhadhah): Accountability; being held responsible for one's actions, leading to legal consequences

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because interdiction does not apply to actions (af'al), only to statements (aqwal).
B) Because all slaves are always liable for all acts regardless of interdiction.
C) Because the master is responsible for his slave's actions.
D) Because ghasb is a financial transaction.

Key Takeaway
The interdicted slave's physical actions create immediate liability, even though his verbal transactions are suspended until emancipation.
