Condition of the Cause is Like the Cause When Transgressive
Legal maxim with applied example in diyat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim that a transgressive condition of the cause is elevated to the status of the cause, and apply it to determine liability (daman) in cases of usurpation of a free child.

Core Idea
A transgressive condition becomes the legal cause of liability.

In Hanafi tort law, not every harm triggers liability. The key is causation, and sometimes what appears to be a mere condition can be treated as the cause if it was created by transgression. This lesson presents a powerful maxim that bridges the gap between condition and cause, with a concrete example from the law of usurpation of a free child.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Maxim
The maxim states: 'The condition of the cause is placed in the rank of the cause when it is transgressive.' This means a circumstance that is normally not a direct cause of harm can become equivalent to a cause if the person acted transgresively in creating that circumstance.
This is a qa'ida (legal maxim) used to extend liability.
2
Distinguish Condition from Cause
In Hanafi law, a cause ('illah) directly produces a legal effect, while a condition (shart) is merely a prerequisite. Normally, a condition does not create liability. But if the condition involves transgression (ta'addi), it is treated as if it were the cause.
Example: Placing a child in a dangerous place is a condition; the snake bite is the cause. But the transgression in creating the condition makes it equivalent to the cause.
3
Identify the Role of Ta'addi
Ta'addi means exceeding lawful bounds. In the example, moving a free child to a place where there is possibility of falling, snake bites, or predators constitutes transgression. Without ta'addi, the condition remains a mere condition and no liability arises.
The text explicitly says: 'when there is transgression in the cause, the cause becomes a cause of liability.'
4
Apply to the Free Child Case
A usurper (ghasib) of a free child is not liable if the child dies naturally (fever, sudden death) because there is no transgression beyond the usurpation itself. However, if the child dies from an external calamity (thunderbolt, snake bite, predator) that was facilitated by moving the child to a dangerous place, the usurper's transgression makes the condition (dangerous place) equivalent to the cause, and thus the 'aqilah must pay diyah.
The ruling is 'istihsan' (juristic preference).
5
Compare the Two Scenarios
Natural death: no transgression in the condition → no liability. Death from external cause: transgression in the condition → liability. The decisive factor is ta'addi, not the death itself.
This distinction prevents unjust outcomes while holding wrongdoers accountable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شَرْطُ الْعِلَّةِ يُنَزِّلُ مَنْزِلَةَ الْعِلَّةِ إِذَا كَانَ تَعَدّيًا . ترجمہ: علت کی شرط علت کے درجے میں اس وقت اترے گی جب وہ متعدی ہو۔ تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ علت اس وقت علت کہلائے گی جب اس کے علت بننے میں آدمی متعدی ہو یعنی تجاوز کرنے والا ہو ورنہ علت علت نہیں کہلائے گی۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ اگر غاصب نے آزاد بچہ غصب کیا، پھر وہ غاصب کے پاس اچانک بخار وغیرہ کی وجہ سے مر گیا تو غاصب پر کچھ بھی لازم نہیں ہے، کیونکہ آزاد آدمی کا غاصب غصب کی وجہ سے ضامن نہیں ہوتا، اور اگر اتلاف پایا گیا پھر ضامن ہوگا یعنی اگر وہ بچہ بجلی کی کڑک یا سانپ کے ڈسنے یا درندے کے کھانے کی وجہ سے مرگیا تو غاصب کے عاقلہ پر دیت واجب ہوگی ، کیونکہ اب اس نے ایسی جگہ جہاں مکمل گرنے اور سانپ کے ڈسنے کا امکان ہے، بچے کو منتقل کر کے تعدی کی اور جب علت میں تعدی ہو تو علت ، علت ضمان بن جاتی ہے۔ ومن غصب صبيا حرا فمات في يده بحمى أو فجأة فلا شيء عليه وإن كان من صاعقة أو نهشته حية أو أكله سبع فعلى عاقلة الغاصب الدية استحسانا. (الجوهرة النيرة كتاب الديات، باب غصب العبد والمدبر والصبي والجناية
The condition of the cause is placed in the rank of the cause when it is transgressive. Translation: The condition of the cause will descend to the level of the cause when it is transgressive. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that the cause is called a cause when the person is transgressive in it becoming a cause, i.e., he is one who transgresses; otherwise the cause is not called a cause. The author of al-Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, is explaining that if a usurper usurps a free child, and then the child dies suddenly due to fever etc. in the usurper's possession, then nothing is obligatory upon the usurper, because the usurper of a free person is not liable (damin) due to usurpation alone. But if destruction (itlaf) is found, then he will be liable: meaning if that child dies due to a thunderbolt, or a snake bite, or a predator eating him, then the blood money (diyah) is obligatory upon the 'aqilah (tribal group) of the usurper by way of istihsan (juristic preference). Because now he has transgressed by moving the child to a place where there is possibility of falling and snake bites. And when there is transgression in the cause, the cause becomes a cause of liability. And whoever usurps a free child and he dies in his hand due to fever or suddenly, there is nothing upon him; but if it is due to a thunderbolt, or a snake bite, or a predator eating him, then the blood money is upon the 'aqilah of the usurper by way of istihsan. (al-Jawhara al-Nayyira: Book of Diyat, Chapter on Usurpation of Slave, Mudabbar, Child, and Injury in That)
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents another legal maxim: 'The condition of the cause is placed in the rank of the cause when it is transgressive.' The commentary explains that a condition (shart) of harm is not itself the direct cause ('illa) of liability unless the person acted transgresively (ta'addi) in creating that condition. The author of al-Hidayah applies this to the case of a free child who is usurped. Since a free person cannot be owned, mere usurpation (ghasb) of a free child does not make the usurper liable for the child's death if it occurs naturally (like fever). However, if the child dies due to an external calamity that the usurper's transgression made possible (e.g., moving the child to a place prone to lightning, snakes, or predators), then the usurper's transgression in placing the child there is treated as the cause of death. Because the condition (the dangerous environment) was created by transgression, it is given the legal status of the cause itself. Consequently, the diyah (blood money) is wajib upon the 'aqilah of the usurper. This ruling is by istihsan (juristic preference). The text concludes with a direct quote from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira, which states the same rule: for fever or sudden death, no liability; for death from external causes in a dangerous place, liability on the 'aqilah.

Key Terms
علة (ʿillah): Cause; the effective reason that directly brings about a legal ruling, here specifically the cause of liability (daman)
تعدی (taʿaddī): Transgression; exceeding the lawful bounds, acting wrongfully or negligently in a way that causes harm
شرط العلة (sharṭ al-ʿillah): Condition of the cause; a circumstance that is necessary for the cause to operate but is not itself the cause
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability; obligation to compensate for harm
دية (diyah): Blood money; financial compensation paid to the heirs of a victim for unintentional killing or injury
عاقلة (ʿāqilah): The tribal group or relatives (usually male agnates) who are collectively responsible for paying diyah in cases of unintentional killing

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because he usurped the child, which is always forbidden.
B) Because moving the child to a dangerous place is a transgression (ta'addi) that turns the condition into the cause of death.
C) Because the child's death is always the usurper's fault.
D) Because the 'aqilah is only liable in cases of direct killing.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim: a transgressive condition of harm is treated as the cause of harm, imposing liability where otherwise there would be none.

The Principle of Transitive Cause and Liability for Diyah
Ruling principle and its application in kidnapping case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that a transitive condition of a cause is equivalent to the cause itself and apply it to determine liability (diyah) in cases of kidnapping a free child.

Core Idea
A transitive condition of a cause is legally equivalent to the cause itself, imposing liability for diyah.

In this lesson, we examine a key principle from the Hanafi school: the condition of a cause (shart al-illa) is treated as the cause itself when it is transitive (ta'addi). This principle is applied in the case of kidnapping (ghasb) a free child. The passage from al-Hidayah distinguishes between natural death and death from external dangers, showing when the kidnapper's 'aqila must pay blood money.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
Read the opening statement: 'The condition of the cause occupies the position of the cause when it is transitive.' This is the core legal maxim governing liability.
The principle is that a mere condition (shart) can be elevated to the level of the cause ('illa) if the action is transgressive (ta'addī).
2
Analyze the Two Scenarios
The passage presents two outcomes for a kidnapped child's death: (1) natural causes (fever, sudden death) → no liability; (2) external causes (lightning, snakebite, beast) → diyah on the 'aqila. Identify the decisive factor: transitivity of the kidnapper's act.
In the second scenario, the kidnapper's act of placing the child in a dangerous location is the transitive condition that caused exposure to external risks.
3
Understand the Proof (Istihsan)
The ruling for external causes is based on istihsan (juristic preference). Istihsan overrides qiyas because it leads to a more just outcome by recognizing the causal link via transgression.
Without istihsan, the strict analogy would deny liability since the kidnapper did not directly cause the external event. Istihsan fills this gap.
4
Apply the Principle to Modern Analogies
Consider situations like illegal confinement where the detainee dies from an accident (fire, collapse). The transgressive act of confinement is the condition that leads to death, thus liability arises.
The principle is not limited to kidnapping; it applies to any transgressive act that exposes someone to danger.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شَرْطُ الْعِلَّةِ يُنَزِّلُ مَنْزِلَةَ الْعِلَّةِ إِذَا كَانَ تَعَدّيًا . وَمَنْ غَصَبَ صَبِيًّا حُرًّا فَمَاتَ فِي يَدِهِ بِحُمَّى أَوْ فَجْأَةً فَلَا شَيْءَ عَلَيْهِ وَإِنْ كَانَ مِنْ صَاعِقَةٍ أَوْ نَهْشَتْهُ حَيَّةٌ أَوْ أَكَلَهُ سَبُعٌ فَعَلَى عَاقِلَةِ الْغَاصِبِ الدِّيَةُ اسْتِحْسَانًا.
The condition of the cause occupies the position of the cause when it is transitive. And whoever kidnaps a free child and he dies in his hand due to fever or suddenly, then nothing is upon him. And if it was from a lightning strike or a snake bit him or a beast ate him, then upon the 'aqila of the kidnapper is the blood-money, by istihsan.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents a legal principle from the Hanafi school: a condition of a cause is treated as the cause itself if it is transitive (mut'addi). The application is in the case of kidnapping a free child. If the child dies naturally (fever or sudden death), the kidnapper is not liable because his act of kidnapping was not the cause of death. However, if the child dies from an external cause such as lightning, snakebite, or beast attack, then by istihsan the kidnapper is liable because he transitively caused the death by placing the child in a dangerous location. The liability is on the 'aqila (paternal relatives) of the kidnapper, who must pay the diyah (blood money). This shows that the cause of liability is not mere possession but transgressive action.

Key Terms
علة (ʿilla): The legal cause that establishes a ruling.
تعدي (taʿaddī): Transgressive action beyond normal bounds.
غصب (ghaṣb): Wrongful taking of property or person without right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) If the child dies of any cause.
B) If the child dies from a cause like lightning or snakebite.
C) Only if the child is killed intentionally.
D) Never, because the child is free.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle of transitive cause holds a kidnapper liable for external-cause deaths because his transgression placed the victim in danger, and diyah is payable by the 'aqila by istihsan.

Soundness of Intention Not Considered in Rights of People
Establishing a usuli principle with its application in guarantees

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that intention is not required for liability in huquq al-ibad and apply it to cases where a child destroys property or causes harm.

Core Idea
In rights of people, liability follows the act, not the intention.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah establishes a foundational usuli principle: 'Soundness of intention is not considered in the rights of people.' The author explains that in huquq al-ibad (rights of individuals), liability arises from the act itself, regardless of whether the actor had a legally valid intention. The primary example is a minor who destroys someone else's property: even though a child lacks sound intention, he is held liable, and his guardian must compensate. The passage also extends this principle to the case of a child taken without permission who dies from a thunderbolt or snakebite, where the taker's 'aqila pays diyah by istihsan. By studying this lesson, you will understand the distinction between huquq al-ibad and huquq Allah and appreciate how Hanafi fiqh prioritizes the protection of rights over subjective intent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
Read the first sentence: 'Sihhat al-qasd la mu'tabar fi huquq al-'ibad.' Understand that this is a categorical rule: in the domain of rights of people, the validity of intention is irrelevant.
This principle contrasts with huquq Allah, where intention often matters (e.g., in acts of worship).
2
Analyze the Child Example
The author applies the principle to a child who destroys property. Even though a child lacks a formally valid intention (qasd sahih), he is liable (damin) for the loss, and his guardian must pay compensation. This is based on the maxim that the act itself creates liability.
The exception is when the property was a deposit (wadi'ah), as there is disagreement (khilaf) in that case.
3
Examine the Istihsan Proof
In the case of a free child taken without permission (ghasb) who dies from a thunderbolt or snakebite, the taker's 'aqila pays diyah. The justification (istihsan) is that liability is not due to usurpation (since a free person cannot be usurped) but because the taker caused the child to be in a dangerous place, leading to death.
The proof text: 'Wajh al-istihsan anna damanahu laysa li-kawnihi ghasiban bal li-tasabbuhili li-itlafihi bi-naqlihi ila makan fihi al-sawa'iqu wa al-hayyat.'
4
Note the Deposit Exception
The passage clarifies that if a child destroys a deposit, there is disagreement (khilaf) among Hanafi scholars. In other words, the principle may not apply uniformly; in deposit, some consider the child's lack of intention as excusing liability.
This nuance shows that the principle is not absolute but applies to direct destruction (itlaf) outside deposit.
5
Confirm with Adult Analogy
The author reinforces the principle by comparing the child to an adult: an adult who destroys property is liable whether or not he had a sound intention. Therefore, the same applies to a child. This analogy (qiyas) is the hidden analogy (qiyas khafi) that underlies the istihsan.
The adult example demonstrates the general rule: in huquq al-ibad, intention is irrelevant.

ترجمہ: حقوق العباد میں قصد کی صحت کا کوئی اعتبار نہیں ہے۔ تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ حقوق العباد میں کسی کی حق تلفی ہو تو اس میں محض اس فعل پر مواخذہ ہو گا تلف کرنے کا قصد صحیح ہو یا صیح نہ ہو، مثلاً بچے کا فعل ۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلار ہے ہیں کہ اگر بچے نے کسی کا مال ہلاک کر دیا اور وہ بچے کے ہاتھ میں ودیعت نہ ہو تو بچہ ضامن ہو گا اور اس کے ولی کو تاوان دینا ہوگا، کیونکہ یہاں حقوق العباد میں حق تلفی ہوئی ہے اور حقوق العباد میں قصد کی صحت کا اعتبار نہیں ہوتا، لہذا مذکورہ صورت میں بھی قصد کی صحت کا کوئی اعتبار نہ ہوگا، جس طرح اگر کوئی بالغ آدمی کسی کا مال ہلاک کرے تو اس پر ضمان آتا ہے، چاہے اُس کا قصد ہو یا نہ ہو، پس جس طور پر بھی ہو ضامن ہوگا۔ چونکہ یہ حقوق العباد میں سے ہے اس لیے اس میں قصد کی صحت کا اعتبار نہیں ہے۔ في ذلك، ج ۲ ص ۱۴۱)
(وَمَنْ) (غَصَبَ صَبِيَّا حُرًّا أَى ذَهَبَ بِهِ بِغَيْرِ إِذْنِ وَلِيِّهِ وَذِكْرُهُ بِلَفْظِ الْغَصْبِ مُشَاكِلُهُ إِذْ الْعَصْبُ لَا يَتَحَقَّقُ إِلَّا فِي الْأَمْوَالِ وَالْحُرُّ لَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ (فَمَاتَ) أَيْ الصَّبِيُّ (فِي يَدِهِ) أَى فِى يَدِ الدَّاهِبِ بِهِ (فَجُأَةً أَوْ بِحُمَّى فَلَا شَيْءَ عَلَيْهِ وَإِنْ مَاتَ (بِصَاعِقَةٍ) أَوْ نَهْشِ حَيَّةٍ فَعَلَى عَاقِلَتِهِ) أَى الذَّاهِبِ (دِيَتُهُ) أَى دِيَةُ الصَّبِيِّ اسْتِحْسَانًا..... وَجُهُ الاسْتِحْسَانِ أَنَّ ضَمَانَهُ لَيْسَ لِكَوْنِهِ غَاصِبًا بَلْ لِتَسَبُّهِ لِإِتْلَافِهِ بِنَقْلِهِ إِلَى مَكَان فِيهِ الصَّوَاعِقُ وَالْحَيَّاتُ . استهلك الصبى مالا لرجل ،ضمن، وهذا فى غير الوديعة وهو معنى قوله: (يريد به من غير إيداع أى يريد محمد بقوله لا ضمن في غير الوديعة، وفيه اتفاق. وفى الوديعة إذا استهلكها خلاف سبق آنفا لأن الصبي يؤاخذ بأفعاله...... وصحة القصد لا معتبر بها فى حقوق العباد ، والله أعلم بالصواب) هذا كله جواب عما يقال: إن الصبي ليس له قصد صحيح، فكان ينبغي أن لا يضمن فقال: لا اعتبار لصحة القصد في حق العباد. ألا ترى أن البالغ أيضًا إذا استهلك مالا لإنسان فيضمن، سواء كان له قصد صحيح في ذلك أو لم يكن، فعلى أى وجه كان يلزمه الضمان البناية كتاب الديات، باب غصب العبد والمدبر والصبي والجناية في ذلك، ج ۱۳ ص ۳۲۴، ۳۲۵)
الهداية كتاب الديات، باب القسامة، ج ۳ ص ۶۲٤
Principles of Hidayah 273. (29) Soundness of intention is not considered in the rights of people. Translation: In the rights of people, soundness of intention is not considered. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that when someone's right is violated in the rights of people, then in that only the act itself is taken into account, whether the intention to destroy is sound or not, for example the act of a child. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle and explains that if a child destroys someone's property and that property was not a deposit in the child's hand, then the child is liable and his guardian must pay compensation, because here in the rights of people a right was violated and in the rights of people soundness of intention is not considered, therefore in the mentioned case too soundness of intention will not be considered, just as if an adult destroys someone's property, liability falls upon him, whether he intended it or not, so in whatever way it happens he will be liable. Since this is among the rights of people, soundness of intention is not considered. In that, vol. 2 p. 141. (And whoever) seizes a free child, i.e., takes him without permission of his guardian, and mentioning it with the word 'ghasb' is metaphorical since ghasb only applies to property and a free person is not like that. (Then if he dies) i.e., the child (in his hand) i.e., in the hand of the taker (suddenly or from fever, then nothing is upon him. And if he dies) from a thunderbolt or snakebite, then upon his 'aqila' (i.e., the taker's) his 'diya' (i.e., the child's blood money) by istihsan... The justification of istihsan is that his liability is not because he is a usurper, but because he caused his destruction by moving him to a place where there are thunderbolts and snakes. (Majma' al-Anhur: Book of Diyat, Chapter on Usurpation of Slave, Mudabbar, and Child and Injury in that, vol. 2 p. 276) Al-Hidayah Book of Diyat, Chapter on Usurpation of Slave, Mudabbar, and Child and Injury in that, vol. 4 p. 621. (He said) i.e., Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir: And if he destroys property, he is liable, i.e., for. The child who destroys property of a man is liable, and this is outside of deposit, and that is the meaning of his statement: (He intends by it without deposit, i.e., Muhammad intends by his statement 'no liability' in other than deposit, and there is agreement. And in deposit if he destroys it, there is disagreement mentioned previously because the child is held accountable for his actions... And soundness of intention is not considered in the rights of people, and Allah knows best what is correct.) All this is a response to what is said: The child does not have a sound intention, so it would be expected that he should not be liable. So he said: Soundness of intention is not considered in the rights of people. Do you not see that an adult also if he destroys property of a person is liable, whether he had a sound intention in that or not, so in whatever way it happens, liability is incumbent upon him. (Al-Binaya Book of Diyat, Chapter on Usurpation of Slave, Mudabbar, and Child and Injury in that, vol. 13 pp. 324-325) Al-Hidayah Book of Diyat, Chapter of Qasama, vol. 3 p. 624.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is an elaboration of a fundamental usuli principle: 'Soundness of intention is not considered in the rights of people.' It is from the commentary on Al-Hidayah. The author begins by stating the principle in Arabic, then provides an Urdu translation and explanation. The key point is that in matters involving the rights of others (huquq al-ibad), liability is based on the act itself, regardless of whether the actor had a valid intention (qasd sahih). The example given is a child who destroys someone's property. Even though a child may not have a legally valid intention, he is still liable (damin) for the loss, and his guardian must pay compensation. This is contrasted with a deposit (wadi'ah): if a child destroys a deposit, there is a different ruling. The passage then quotes a portion from Al-Hidayah about a free child being taken (ghasb) and dying in the taker's hands. If the child dies suddenly or from fever, no liability; if by thunderbolt or snakebite, the taker's 'aqila pays the diyah, based on istihsan. The reason is that the taker's liability is not due to usurpation (since a free person cannot be usurped) but due to causing the child's death by moving him to a dangerous place. The passage also references al-Jami' al-Saghir and Al-Binaya to confirm that a child is liable for destroying property, and the principle of disregarding intention in huquq al-ibad is the underlying rationale. The authority weight is from Zahir al-Riwayah as it references core texts.

Key Terms
صِحَّةُ الْقَصْدِ (sihhat al-qasd): The legal validity of intention; whether the person intended the act in a legally recognized manner.
حُقُوقُ الْعِبَادِ (huquq al-ibad): Rights that pertain to individuals, such as property, life, and bodily integrity; as opposed to huquq Allah (rights of God).
ضامن (damin): Legally liable to compensate for loss or damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle 'sihhat al-qasd la mu'tabar fi huquq al-ibad', if a child accidentally breaks an adult's window, what is the ruling?
A) The child is not liable because he lacks intention.
B) The child is liable, and his guardian must pay compensation.
C) The owner must bear the loss because the child is a minor.
D) The child is liable only if he intended to break the window.

Key Takeaway
Liability in huquq al-ibad is based on the act causing damage, not on the actor's intention; this principle ensures protection of rights.
